Remember, inspiration can be found in a million different places. An old song, a news article, a documentary, the logo from a sign on a downtown storefront, and from books... like this illustration, taken from _The Exploits of the Incomparable Mulla Nasrudin_, became inspiration for the animated film [The Thief and The Cobbler][WtFYDFOeNc]:

![image](./images/unnamed_1202.png)

When diving into the researching process, think about how to find some images for attitudes, hairstyles, costumes and ideas on portraying your characters physically. Look around for any design inspiration you can find. The deeper you dig, the more you can find imagery that can influence your choices in the right direction. Let's say you're required to design a _group of pretentious high school girls..._

Research and reference real people & photo references for the types of personalities, ethnicities and clothing/hair styles you are exploring for your characters:

![image](./images/unnamed_1451.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_260.png)

Rough Exploration Sketches by Brittany Meyers

Think like you are casting for a movie. Look at real people or photos and take notes on what makes that person them. Everyone is different - they have their own stories, tastes, habits, body shapes, and attitudes.

Everyone has different charms in their own way, and paying attention and capturing these details in your characters will make them feel genuine and unique.

![image](./images/unnamed_197.png)

Color Designs by Brittany Meyers

Finding references can help to push your style and characters into all sorts of different directions, often times a happy accident occurs when sketching and exploring a wide range of personalities:

![image](./images/unnamed_743.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_698.png)

#### Thumbnails

Planning is a critical stage in the [process][QPdVZcMKgW]. You'd be surprised how important it is to really think through and plan your designs before you get started. It's extremely important and helps you avoid problems that could show up later, and also discover things that can really enhance and bring out the best in your potential designs.

**Thumbnails** is a term used in many areas, like roughing out poses for animation:

![image](./images/unnamed_053.gif)

The quick and loose preliminary sketches in storyboards:

![image](./images/unnamed_1036.png)

And those tiny title card images you see on YouTube or Netflix:

![image](./images/unnamed_1466.png)

In Character Design, thumbnails are all the small rough sketches made to hash out your ideas.

While you are collecting references and preparing the info you need, you will begin to have an idea of what you want this character to represent. Start making lots of thumbnails by sketching the character(s) you have in mind, but keep your sketches very loose and [rough][QsuAHGIEzy] at this stage.

![image](./images/unnamed_815.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_099.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1409.jpg)

Make LOTS of sketches! \-- This can be the most fun part of the process...

![image](./images/unnamed_927.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_224.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_774.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1289.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_785.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_281.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_038.gif)

**'Roughs'** can mean different things to different artists. For example, animation director Glen Keane is known for drawing VERY rough:

![image](./images/unnamed_403.png)

Other artists prefer to draw their roughs much cleaner:

![image](./images/unnamed_800.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1473.jpg)

Just like your handwriting, everyone has their own unique personal style of sketching.

![image](./images/unnamed_942.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1254.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_084.png)

Doing random doodles can sometimes lead to happy accidents and discoveries.

![image](./images/unnamed_182.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_912.jpg)

By Jeremiah Alcorn

**Rough Drawings** tend to be not as rough as Thumbnails, but are not Clean/Final Lines either. It's always your personal preference for how rough you want to be, everyone has their own style on how they produce 'roughs', there's no right or wrong method, whatever you feel most comfortable with. Many artists/directors have varying opinions on this, but it doesn't matter too much anyways, as long as the IDEAS are clear and presentable. Producers and art directors all have their own preferences for how many stages to take between thumbnails/concept to clean+color, and how rough or final they'd like to see designs delivered and presented as. Simply go with their requests and adjust accordingly.

![image](./images/unnamed_1380.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_397.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1292.png)

- **Thumbnails** are VERY loose and sketchy, it's lines and shapes that are searching for something.
- **Roughs** have most of the details and structure present, they also look fast & scratchy, but all the shapes and volumes are in there.
- **Tie-downs** are cleaner than roughs, they're almost final lines but not quite, all the details are there.
- **Clean-up/Line art** has all the final lines laid down, the lines are refined to the weight and style needed.

![image](./images/unnamed_179.jpg)

You can say there's no difference in the volume of these two circles (shown below) and you wouldn't be wrong. The one on the right is two-dimensional and so is the one on the left. What is important is what you do not see --- the thought process behind the lines---that helps you to create more dimensional sketches with flow to them.

![image](./images/unnamed_492.png)

Don't get caught up with the details in the beginning; this is the moment to play with proportions and shapes and see what feels right for the character. Try to come up with a lot of variety at this stage.

![image](./images/unnamed_596.png)

Always start off very loose and rough, then draw over your own work, over and over to refine and shape the character. [Sketching][nozsJTPJtB] is NOT aboutâ  refinement. The darker lines show your "decision lines" and the lighter "searching lines" become less important. â 

![image](./images/unnamed_823.png)

By [Griz & Norm][fWKsbrhoOU]

**Quantity will find quality:** When starting a sketch, be sure to draw quickly and confidently. Gloss over mistakes and don't spend too much time erasing. If you're not happy with the way your drawing is going, just start a new layer to build something fresh or iterate again. Through this process, something will eventually click and you'll know when things feel right. A lot of the time you may not know what you want to draw until it's directly in front of your eyeballs.

![image](./images/unnamed_1685.png)

For example, the story about the **pottery teacher** is a parable about the importance of quantity over quality when learning something new. The original version was told by photographer Jerry Uelsmann to motivate his _Introduction to Photography_ students at the University of Florida. The story was later retold in the book _Art & Fear_ by David Bayles and Ted Orland, while the art medium changed from photography to ceramics.

In the story, a pottery teacher splits his class into two groups and gives each group a different assignment...

Group A: Make one pot per day for 30 days

Group B: Work on only one pot for the entire 30 days

At the end of the month, the teacher judges the quality of the pots and finds that the highest quality pots came from Group A, the group that made 30 pots quickly. The teacher explains that by focusing on quantity, the students were able to learn from their mistakes and steadily improve their skills. They made 29 pots, one at a time, learning from their faults and missteps, they had to work fast, having to complete one per day, and by the last pot, #30, they had refined their process to create a well-made pot. The students in Group B, on the other hand, became paralyzed by perfection and were left with little to show for their efforts, even though they had a whole 30 days to perfect the one attempt at a single pot.

The moral of the story is that **quantity leads to quality**, and that it\'s better to create and improve over time than to focus solely on perfect quality from the start.

![image](./images/unnamed_773.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1463.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1232.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1029.jpg)

There are several ways to do thumbnailing, but it is always meant to be a way to jot down lots of [small ideas][DAVVOdKjZq] as a visual rehearsal. They can also be just blocks and blobs and simple silhouettes, or quick gesture drawings made of squiggly lines, or just various shapes and forms to see what works and what doesn't work.

Sketches by Erin Shin:![image](./images/unnamed_1276.png)

![image](./images/unnamed_1228.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_789.png)

Sketches by Jay McKay:

![image](./images/unnamed_1273.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_363.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_193.jpg)

Keep spending time researching and discovering plenty of imagery to inspire you, go online, find the types of design aesthetics, costumes, and environment that your character will be in. Compile lots of references, then start by roughing out some concepts of what the character should look like, explore and experiment with loose & quick sketches. All of this will help you to learn in a more [tactical way][dvUmEtqwUQ]. A well-researched character will have a lot more credibility and really resonate with their audience. When you are finished researching, you should have enough reference material to start designing your character.

Sketches by Glen Keane:

![image](./images/unnamed_355.png)

Digital sketching is a low-stake scenario, so try not to stress too much about drawing. It's okay to make mistakes in the beginning. Embrace it and try not to worry about the details. The main goal when doing initial rough sketches is to jot down your wild ideas and then build upon them afterwards. It can look like senseless blobs, but as long as it makes sense to you, you're doing the right thing.

![image](./images/unnamed_371.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_372.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1315.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1001.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1322.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_088.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_902.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_190.jpg)

**Once you've created lots of thumbnails:**

- Revisit your most successful sketches and refine them further
- Pull together the most compelling visual elements into a definitive design

After all this sketching, ideally you create something that you want to push further. At this point you can create a new layer in your drawing program, while also lowering the _opacity_ of the previous one, so you can easily draw on top of it. It's important to stay loose and to not stiffen up when starting a "cleaner" drawing. Strengthen the design and the shapes you already put forth. In the past I often strayed too far from the sketch and became unhappy with the drawing. You chose to move forward with your sketch for a reason, so don't disregard it too much when fleshing it out.

#### Shape Language

There are three basic shapes in your toolbelt: round, box, and triangle. If you follow your intuition, each shape conveys a personality. For example:

- **Round** = charismatic, harmless, endearing, friendly, soft, cute, innocent (circles, ovals, eggs, beans)
- **Box** = reliable, uniform, traditional, stable, balanced - (squares, rectangles)
- **Triangle** = movement, harmony, cunning, dynamic, competent (downward pointing more aggressive or villainous)

![image](./images/unnamed_229.png)

- Shapes can also be **combined** for more complex characters

![image](./images/unnamed_1362.png)

There's a variety of other combinations/variations like diamond-shaped, tear-drop-shaped, oval/egg-shaped, hourglass-shaped, and bean-shaped.

![image](./images/unnamed_367.png)

When we deconstruct visual images, most objects will break down into one or more familiar shapes, each carrying their own visual signposts. See how the characters from **_Pixar's Up_** can be visually broken down to [basic shapes][bLrilynTnz].

![image](./images/unnamed_229.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1206.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_287.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_666.png)

As you can see from the images above, it can help to think of the human body as a series of relatively [simple shapes][OIGzLwsTlD] when you are starting your design.

Squares and rectangles are reliable, rigid and sturdy, possibly stubborn/unchanging too. Visually they're used to symbolize order and robustness. Think of a robot or a heavyset man, which are often represented by rectangles in [caricature][SzLVxbqTsF], and note the correlation of sturdiness to each one.

Circles (and ovals, ellipses, egg-shape) are friendly, harmless and fun. Due to the continuous nature of the line in a circle, they also represent progress and synergy. Animated characters made up of the circular look are generally protagonists, though sometimes bumbling or innocuous ones at that. Think about the Powerpuff Girls, Homer Simpson and Santa. Babies, teddy bears, dopey types and jolly characters all fall under this category of cuddly, friendly circular fun.

Triangles are dynamic shapes, indicating both balance and energy. Down-pointing triangles are used in character design for fit, active characters: think of the V-shaped chest of an athletic character, or the classic hourglass figure of a femme fatale which is largely a series of triangles stacked on top of one another.

![image](./images/unnamed_1308.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_349.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_754.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1214.jpg)

> ðŸ‘‰ [Watch this approach to defining cartoon anatomy and shape language][IjwPvNEuHg]

![image](./images/unnamed_1426.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_388.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_679.png)

> **All these main shapes can be squashed, stretched, combined & mixed together:**

![image](./images/unnamed_1156.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1584.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_548.png)

Shapes are unlimited; it simply comes down to the most important ingredient in design, which is its organization. Break your human character shapes into three parts: head, full torso, and legs.

![image](./images/unnamed_1031.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_315.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_013.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1263.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1464.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_018.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1103.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1332.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1555.png)

Head shapes of various sorts can immediately create a distinct personality for your character:

![image](./images/unnamed_1185.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1382.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_976.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1305.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1453.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1049.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_323.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1114.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1419.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_977.png)

Three-dimensional structure is extruded from these basic shapes:

![image](./images/unnamed_443.png)

> **There are thousands of possibilities when exploring various Shape Dynamics:**

![image](./images/unnamed_677.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_514.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_629.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1359.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1330.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1157.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_872.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_446.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_172.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_230.png)

See how Porky Pig starts off as a kidney bean-shape:

![image](./images/unnamed_342.png)

Shapes can contrast and compliment each other, while having shapes within shapes to balance each other as well:

![image](./images/unnamed_418.jpg)

Look at the many different shape combinations used here:

![image](./images/unnamed_007.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1190.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_218.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_065.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_237.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_806.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_958.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_900.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_314.png)

The shapes that make up the character can symbolize what they represent or give hints as to their personality, background and alignment. Combining shapes together will have their own visual significance depending on their arrangement. A rectangle and triangle together can make a house or an arrow, just as a character design with a rectangular body and circular head could be a dimwitted lackey (large rectangle, small circle) or a mega-mind genius (thin rectangle, large circle). Of course, you can always flip the tables to play with expectations. Maybe the small circular little girl is the powerful dynamo, or the stocky square guy turns out to be unstable and hyperactive.

![image](./images/unnamed_1023.png)

> Advice from [Cory Toomey][qLZBIaHYov]:

![image](./images/unnamed_1443.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_820.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_934.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1420.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1258.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1047.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_278.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1326.png)

### Exploration Sketches

A character designer is the artist responsible for conceptualizing and designing all of the characters in an animated production. Sometimes you are given a predetermined style to follow, and therefore very little reference searching is required of you. Other times, a lengthy process of exploration studies at the very beginning is made to discover what the style should be. This specific branch of the design team focuses on using comprehension of design language as well as their considerable draftsmanship skills to create new characters based upon the stories and descriptions provided to you.

> See [**this solid advice**][XiVIdVlSuS] from Don Corgi for strategies on design and color.

When you first try to experiment with what your character might look like, produce some rough drawings that allow you to rehearse, explore and discover a variety of shapes, sizes, and proportions to see what feels best. This stage is often referred to as Concept Design or Predesign.

![image](./images/unnamed_473.png)

Character Development or Visual Development (**VisDev**) is what many artists and producers will also call these stages.

In the **writing** stages; Character development is the process of creating and evolving the personalities, motivations, goals, and emotions of your animated characters. It involves defining their traits, flaws, strengths, weaknesses, relationships, and arcs.

The designers do their best to visualize these concepts, materializing them into a character.

This is one of the most creative parts of the entire production process.

![image](./images/unnamed_595.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1104.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1186.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_035.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1118.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_594.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_090.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1242.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_347.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1024.png)

These initial **research drawing andrough sketching** stages are all about the visual character development process. Creating and evolving the look of your animated characters, first through research than LOTS of sketches. It involves defining their shape language and visualizing their personality, from the lead characters to supporting cast. All with the purpose to drive the narrative and engage the audience on an emotional level. A well-designed character possesses a strong visual aesthetic that instantly captivates the eye. This is not easy to achieve, but through lots of practice, you'll get better and better at nailing it down sooner and sooner.

Reference. Reference. Reference.

![image](./images/unnamed_462.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_431.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1454.png)

You'll see and hear about **character development** - which can be a broad term, often you are not only trying to figure out what the characters look like, but also WHO they are and what their personalities are. As you construct their design it is easy to see how much the personality description and writings obviously influence the evolution of their design, but then the designs can also have an affect on their personality as well.

Similar to how a television actor is influenced by the writings in the scripts they read, what their character does, how they do the things they do, and what they are told to say as dialogue, all shape what the character they are meant to portray. The actor studies the written material, has discussions with writers, directors, producers about performance choices. But the actor themself adds and subtracts their own opinions and qualities to the behaviours, mannerisms and quirks that the character has. Inevitably evolving the character persona into something different than what the original intentions might have been.

![image](./images/unnamed_1280.jpg)

These preliminary sketches can be referred to as the **Concept** stages of the characters' visual development. Where several variations and iterations of the characters are drawn up, assembled together so you and others can compare them against each other, sometimes you mix and match and swap out traits and body parts to assemble pieces together from various designs to see what might work. Usually these can be very rough to start with, all made to visually explore and see what feels best.

![image](./images/unnamed_940.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_933.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1571.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1237.png)

Rough Character Designs & Exploration Poses/Expressions by Lauren Faust â˜ï¸ for My Little Pony: Friendship is Magic

![image](./images/unnamed_1029.png)

> â˜ï¸ "You need to be smart with where you spend time. If you focus on detailing everything, things can become overly cluttered quickly. You can see that this elephant warrior has a lot of implied details. The lines on the elephant's core wrap around its form. Although this is rather simplistic, it gives you a good idea of what the armor can look like with just a few strokes of the pen. There are also scribbles on the deltoid armor pieces and in the elbows, implying some kind of sigil or crest. All of these "details" are conveyed with minimal work." -Erik Ly

![image](./images/unnamed_262.jpg)

By Dan Norton

The more art books on animated TV & media production you'll see, the more you'll notice there can be many, many versions of your character that need to be [explored][JxbqaZsQIc]. Sometimes, by chance, you nail it on the first few tries, other times, you need to revise your work several times, only to realize you need to start from scratch with a whole new approach.

![image](./images/unnamed_460.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1064.jpg)

Sometimes exploration artwork can be more refined and colored...

![image](./images/unnamed_1299.png)

But most times, to start out, they stay very loose and rough...

![image](./images/unnamed_1400.png)

There's no _right_ or _wrong_ method, it's whatever you prefer, and adapt according to what your client prefers on a case-by-case basis.

![image](./images/unnamed_273.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_211.png)

You'll see that there's a certain charm and tactile energy in rough drawings that can't be shown in cleaner line art.

![image](./images/unnamed_180.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_407.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_120.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_713.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1028.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_039.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1430.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1000.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1397.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1310.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1714.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1664.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1287.png)

Explorations sketches, conceptual sketches, thumbnailing, blocking out, vis dev, shape design exploration - it can have many names, but it's all the same approach; loose, rough, experimental and a visual rehearsal to see what works best. It's fast, it's messy, and some artists naturally draw with cleaner lines, others like [Glen Keane][tGAdPLgqNo], can be notoriously very rough - no matter what the style, this stage is meant to be all about developing the look of your character.

![image](./images/unnamed_1595.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1386.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1632.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_359.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_625.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1521.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1116.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1161.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_561.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_490.png)

![image](./images/unnamed_509.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1236.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_320.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1605.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_138.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1157.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1522.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_069.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_840.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_024.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_071.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_197.jpg)

As you can see from all these examples, this stage of visual development for character concepts is all about trying out lots of different things, to see what looks good, and what works best for the character's function and personality and the story/themes being portrayed. Sometimes color concepts are asked for as well to be mixed in with the roughs, especially when there's one being fine-tuned to be the final possible design.

![image](./images/unnamed_1575.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1070.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_133.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_629.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_650.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_592.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_188.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_651.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_328.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_306.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1081.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1352.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_320.jpg)

These sketches can explore variations of the same design with color concepts included to provide some options and clearer representation of how the character would look, colors are easy to replace later, they're not meant to be final quite yet, just a way to see the tones and how they could possibly work.

![image](./images/unnamed_961.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_162.jpg)

Sometimes your director/client will ask to go straight to color concepts with your design explorations:

![image](./images/unnamed_534.png)

By Olivier Silven

![image](./images/unnamed_1535.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_497.png)

![image](./images/unnamed_484.jpg)

Proportions can be explored to almost-endless amounts of variations:

![image](./images/unnamed_738.jpg)

Roughing out exploration sketches like these will help you to discover what style you (or your client / director / producer) may be looking for, and allows you to figure out how the anatomy and mechanics of your character may work. In addition, you figure out the strengths and weaknesses in the design, see what the patterns are in the nuts and bolts of the design and what makes it work.

![image](./images/unnamed_860.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_385.png)

Exploring various patterns on your character are sometimes required as well:

![image](./images/unnamed_1683.png)

Sometimes you need to be very specific, and explore how certain details or body parts should look when posing the character:

![image](./images/unnamed_223.png)By Esther Morales

Exploration sketches allow you to create many versions of the character too, so that you (or your supervisor/client) can narrow down some options to arrive at the ideal solutions and get closer to the look you're all searching for.

Just like for the exploration sketches, the costuming and color design process consists of conceptualizing various clothing options with several colors palettes as well:

![image](./images/unnamed_203.jpg)

Hair and clothing variants are the most common alternative versions of the design you will do.

> [How clothing affects a character's personality][jbedFGzKzw]

![image](./images/unnamed_945.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_544.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_753.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1266.jpg)

Researching clothing design can be lots of fun too.

![image](./images/unnamed_711.jpg)

Shapes, forms, proportions, anatomy, costuming and many other aspects of "style" must be experimented with. The word itself, **style**, appears nearly 500 times in this document - because it has such a broad definition that covers many aspects of visual art. Once certain parts of a character are nailed down, there may still be other aspects that need further exploration. Usually [clothes][FeXjmiKEpB] and hair are specific areas that can be further explored separately to show many more possible varieties, to see what feels best. As they say with actors; the hair styles, make-up and costume choices help to define the characters:

![image](./images/unnamed_1258.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_011.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_275.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1655.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1601.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_193.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1256.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1609.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_750.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_842.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_115.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1660.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1424.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1401.png)

Just like an actor, **costuming** and **hair styling** alone can be an entirely grand and long venture to embark upon to discover the many outfits your character can have and which costume should be their default. However, with over 40 years of [The Simpsons][HCPRBonMoz], you'll notice they've worn the same clothes the whole time. The reason for this is because it can be difficult in the production pipeline to change a character's outfits, it's more efficient to keep them usually the same as often as possible, when you have a large crew of animators across several studios producing a series.

![image](./images/unnamed_1316.png)

**WATCH & LISTEN:**

- ðŸ‘‰ [Tracing vs Studying vs Using Reference][IaUSrthkqU]
- ðŸ‘‰ [Elements of Character - Gesture & Form][AVznCSVsAe]
- ðŸ‘‰ [Ben Eblen's Method of Breaking The Rules][aCfGeTISuu]

#### Concept Art

Exploration drawings (shown above), along with research drawings, visual development art, character development art, predesign art and concept art - all serve the same purpose, and they can be black and white, rough, clean, or color - but these can all be considered the _conceptual stage_ of design. Sometimes concept art is loose and rough character sketches, sometimes it's full color, or mixtures of rough colors thrown in with some greyscale tonal studies, or just rough black-and-white line art. It can have roughly painted environments integrated with them, displaying what a snapshot of a scene could look like (color keys or lighting keys), fully illustrated with the character placed in a location. Concept Art is simply short for "_Conceptual Artwork_".

Here's concept art for 'Puckhead' by _Tavis Silbernagel_. Developed for a video game that was meant to be a parody/ripoff of [Cuphead][vOfCqznaEg]:

Character Development Art by _Chris Sanders:_

![image](./images/unnamed_382.jpg)

Teacher [Jazno Francoeur][DNOOqxTtni] explains: Chris Sanders' development work on **Lilo & Stitch** is a masterclass in unified vision---where design, story, and tone emerged from a singular voice. His loose, expressive linework radiates warmth, roundness, and imperfection, making the characters feel handmade and emotionally immediate. These weren't just sketches; they were the DNA of the film's identity. Sanders, in collaboration with Dean DeBlois, carved a heartfelt, offbeat narrative that broke Disney convention by grounding the story in personal, familial stakes rather than traditional fairy tale tropes. His clarity of vision permeated every department. I recall animating a sequence where the genetic guns blast through the bay doors on the mothership---my instinct was to have the blasts scatter randomly. Chris paused and said, "Think of it like a rotating Gatling gun---clockwise, circular." That suggestion reframed the entire moment and made the scene work better in regards to both timing and design. Suddenly, it wasn't chaos---it was controlled rhythm. That's Chris in a nutshell - not just a designer of characters, but a shaper of moments, bringing purpose and clarity to even the smallest beat. The film's enduring charm owes much to that meticulous yet intuitive touch.

![image](./images/unnamed_515.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_572.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1009.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1361.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_440.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_767.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1310.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1251.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1461.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1482.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_756.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1043.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_644.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_922.jpg)

#### Pre-Design/VisDev

Visual Development, Pre-Design, Concept Design, all interchangeable terms relating to the process of researching styles, developing the character art style, creating exploration sketches, and producing color roughs. It's all about 'finding the look' \-- sometimes the style is already locked in, but you still need to try out lots of different options to see which one works best with the character's description and personality\...

### Character Turnaround

Now that you've collected all your references, sketched out lots of concepts and exploration drawings, and started choosing which roughs to nail down for the look of your character, based upon any prerequisites or parameters you might have to follow. You are now beginning to lock down what it is you (and your supervisor/director/client) are looking for. You are now ready to get started in producing the actual individual views of the character design by refining the look and various angles of the character's physiology. Here you begin to explore how the structure and anatomy of the character work, it's time to begin developing a model sheet for your character!

![image](./images/unnamed_1286.png)

#### The Rotation

It starts with the 5 basic views, allowing yourself and others to see how the characters look when rotated and viewed from different sides.

![image](./images/unnamed_444.png)

To illustrate a character model sheet or turn-around sheet, typically you will be required to draw your character from 3-5 different angles, to develop a rotation (or [partial rotation][eTXtqJtTQm]). Depending on how much of a main or primary character they are in the story, this will determine how many views will need to be produced, the more present they are in the story, the greater the chance the animators will need more reference to know what the character looks like from different sides. Another factor is the style of the animation (will it require lots of turning or changing perspectives), You may need 5, 8 or even 14 views to fully see the character from all views to ensure consistency. But to start off, your primary views will typically include: **front**, **front three-quarter(3/4)**, **side (profile)**, **back** and **back three-quarter** views.

![image](./images/unnamed_676.png)

> With the initial 5 views completed, you'll have the foundation to create many more.

##### 1 - Draw the front view of your character

The first pose is the frontal view. Your standard turnaround should have 5-views of the same character: a **front view**, a **back view**, a **side (profile) view**, a **3/4 front view** and a **3/4 back view**, these all help modelers/builders and animators better understand the volume & structure of the character and what details are seen from various rotations. But a 4-view turnaround works good too, it's the bare minimum for most productions, but for asymmetrical characters you may need up to 8-views to accurately get the full rotation, since the left and right sides of their bodies aren't mirrored.

![image](./images/unnamed_195.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1051.png)

**Symmetry:**

![image](./images/unnamed_806.png)

**Asymmetry:**

![image](./images/unnamed_1219.png)

**Symmetrical:**

![image](./images/unnamed_879.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1280.png)

Also be mindful and producing any draw-throughs, to show any areas that might be covered up by hair, clothing, or overlapping body parts:

![image](./images/unnamed_910.png)

A good way to ensure consistency between the different views you will be drawing next is to use one of the poses as a background, and the frontal view is the best one to start with.

![What Is a Turnaround in Character Design and How To Draw One 3](./images/unnamed_1547.png)

##### 2 - Create some guidelines

Once you have your character\'s frontal view, place some guidelines at different levels to keep consistency between the poses. These lines will help the character have the same height throughout the sequence, but also maintain the proportions of the different parts of its body.

Accuracy in the proportions and positions of all the elements of your character's rotation is always important. Creating these guides in Photoshop is very simple. Just drag the mouse from the document rulers. Make sure that the \"Snap to\...\" function is not active because it will make the drawing process difficult.

![What Is a Turnaround in Character Design and How To Draw One 5](./images/unnamed_729.png)

##### 3 - Draw the 3/4 view

The 3/4 pose next, using the front view as a template. Imagine what the features of the character would look like if they were a little tilted. The nose and other facial features will have to be altered slightly and not quite in the same position. The shoulders, symmetrical in the front view, will now change and have slightly different sizes. The legs will no longer be the same size, either. One will be wider than the other because it will be closer to the imaginary point of view.

![image](./images/unnamed_890.jpg)

Once you have made the first approach to drawing the new 3/4 pose, do the flipping or mirroring method. It will be easier to see if something doesn\'t have the proportions it should.

It\'s essential to pay attention to the feet and the relationship with
the ground and make sure they don\'t appear to be shifted.

![image](./images/unnamed_1352.png)

> If your eye level is at the waist, then lines above the mid-section will gradually curve slightly upwards, and lines below the mid-section will gradually curve slightly downwards. See [here][cbwTsQENPZ] for more info about this.

All characters follow some sort of perspective rules, same as any geometric shapes:

![image](./images/unnamed_036.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_515.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_714.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1129.png)

Notice the ground plane that the character designs stand on, sometimes it's flat, like they're standing on a flat line, other times they are in perspective.

> Pay attention to consistency in foot placement & perspective for the turn-around views:

![image](./images/unnamed_1300.png)

Also, in this above example, the camera would be in line with her eyes, not her waist, this is more common in character sheets with turnarounds, Notice her collar, sleeves, belt, pants all curve downwards.

Even when certain character designs only follow their own rules of flat-graphically-stylized perspective, some 'cheats' need to be made to have the characters look good in whichever views they're being depicted in:

![image](./images/unnamed_792.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_068.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_607.png)

##### 4 - Check the guidelines

Once the first rough drawing of the pose is finished, check the guidelines to make sure everything aligns properly. Not all of the elements will at first, but that\'s ok. Just make adjustments and corrections as needed.

It is essential to spend a good deal of time on this and check carefully because it is better to notice any possible mistakes now than when we are already working with the refined version of the drawing.

![What Is a Turnaround in Character Design and How To Draw One 7](./images/unnamed_1178.png)

One of the most important things to remember when drawing your [character turnaround][OYcNAFtLGc] is that each part of your character should line up exactly in each angle. A good way to make sure you are getting your proportions correctly is to [draw guidelines][edNUilwYOA] that correspond with key elements of your character. Example: top of the head, eyes, nose, mouth, shoulders, and other key parts of the body and costume, and where the joints are.

##### 5 - Draw the side view

For the side view, it is crucial to consider that it is the pose that will show the character\'s volume, especially for facial features like the nose or lips. Some designers draw the arm as a separate detail in this view or raised up so that it doesn't block what the torso looks like, but it\'s up to you. Don\'t forget, as with the rest of the poses, to check that every element is properly aligned before refining the lines of your sketch.

##### 6 - Draw the back view and 3/4 back view

The 3/4 back view might be one of the trickiest poses, but don\'t despair. The back view is more straightforward. Just copy the front view\'s silhouette and add all the elements in the back of the character. You may never have considered what your character looks like from behind, things like how long the hair is, or what decal might be imprinted on the back of their sweater, for example. These are important details in a turnaround because modelers (builders/riggers) will use them to achieve a more faithful character reproduction.

![What Is a Turnaround in Character Design and How To Draw One 9](./images/unnamed_299.png)

Then comes cleaning up the linework and choosing final colors (more about this later, in the [Color Styling](#color-styling) chapter). Often providing a few different color schemes and variants can be helpful if you have a producer/director or client needing to do approvals on your work - offering a few choices can be helpful in arriving at a consensus for the final color pass.

![What Is a Turnaround in Character Design and How To Draw One 1](./images/unnamed_1428.png)

Depending on your chosen line art style, your [clean-up][PbnjpfamUJ] pass is essentially tracing your rough artwork with a 'final line', ensuring consistency and volumes are maintained. Study other design [critiques][pSIOtMTgCT] to learn from other designers\' improvements.

**Head Angles** are an additional aspect that is common to include in more detailed model sheets:

![image](./images/unnamed_1411.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_524.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_694.jpg)

Head angles can be challenging to do, combinations of cheating the perspective to make the angle work while still making it "look good" is key.

![image](./images/unnamed_1271.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_695.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_481.jpg)

Here's another similar approach to the turnaround process...

Rough out some concepts to discover who your character is for their look and personality. Produce exploration drawings to find the shapes, structure, and style you're going for:

![image](./images/unnamed_1409.png)

Then refine your character(s) down to a few possible body types:

![image](./images/unnamed_1030.png)

Refine and lock down the look and structure of your character.
To make your turnaround views, setup your horizontal lines, use them as your guide - rough out basic forms:

![image](./images/unnamed_512.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1183.png)

Then a detail pass (roughs) to refine the shapes, make decisions on anatomy, clothing, hair:

![image](./images/unnamed_323.png)

Line Art pass (clean-up) - pay attention to the accuracy of the rotation, the volumes and proportions of all body parts relative to one and other - the position of all details and any revisions and adjustments you want to make:

![image](./images/unnamed_184.png)

Color pass - start off with flat colors as seen here, but this may also include shading & shadows, if that's the style you're going for:

![image](./images/unnamed_1476.png)

> ðŸ‘‰ [Here's a great short summary of this process][dwqjcAxqHI]

Finding useful [templates][UsFuviQhMw] (or creating one of your own) can have you be more efficient when reusing the same body types for multiple characters.

Sometimes the drawing of the character in each view is in a different pose, instead of the standard arms resting on their sides or pointing outwards in the same postures for each view, this is purely the preference of the designer and their supervisor/director.

![image](./images/unnamed_1565.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1000.jpg)

Sometimes, all you need is 2 or 3 views because the amount of screen time they have is short and limited and they do not animate to make full turns in the scenes they are in. This saves you time by not designing more views than is needed, the storyboard will show how much they are moving, so always refer to that. From this information you can determine how many views are actually required, if there are no [storyboards][GGiTaVxwLS] to reference, then start off with the first view (3/4) and once that is approved, depending on the time you have and the requirements set out by your director/supervisor, you complete, 3, 4, 5 point turnaround views as your time restrictions allows.

When there's a team of designers, often two views are enough to get general approvals from all necessary people in the production. A junior designer or assistant designer then completes all the other views of the turn. The lead character designer or designer supervisor or art director then approved the accuracy of the additional turn around views.

![image](./images/unnamed_645.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_299.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_086.jpg)

Rough design explorations with final design comparisons, by Tracy Butler:

![image](./images/unnamed_846.png)

Don't forget to have those guidelines there as reference for the position and volume of your character as you figure out how to make the rotation for the turn-around:

![image](./images/unnamed_604.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1290.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1539.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1099.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1334.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_439.jpg)

> ðŸ‘‰ Jackie Droujko's amazing character design tips: [short versions][DLMlgGDIOs] / [long versions][AZjzytPKkH]

Once you have completed the design you can hide the guidelines and you should be left with something that looks like a finished design turnaround sheet. Of course, don't be afraid to revise and refine your work to perfect the rotation of your character.

**4 point turnarounds:**

![image](./images/unnamed_130.jpg)

Front 3/4 Side Back

![image](./images/unnamed_1403.png)

Front Three-Quarter Side Back

![image](./images/unnamed_351.png)

3/4 Front Front 3/4 Back Side

The first 4 basic views are essential for any new character, regardless of the style.

![image](./images/unnamed_1542.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_170.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1133.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_500.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_407.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_345.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1406.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_312.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_869.jpg)

**5-point Turnarounds:**

![image](./images/unnamed_039.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_160.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_831.jpg)

By Tommy Kinnerup

![image](./images/unnamed_1262.png)

By Jerimiah Alcorn

![image](./images/unnamed_910.jpg)

By Soyeon Yoo

![image](./images/unnamed_1184.png)

By Maaike Scherff

![image](./images/unnamed_590.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1486.png)

By Bryan Arnett

5-pt turnarounds are probably the most common set you will produce. This is because it covers all the basics you need. With these first 5 views, it's easy to expand to 8 or 12 view rotations.

![image](./images/unnamed_854.png)

**8-point turnarounds:**

In 2D animation, most of the time designers need to 'cheat' the look of each individual view since most times they are drawn not how they would actually look three-dimensionally, but more how they should look to appear the most appealing from that angle.

> Study these many samples to see how other design styles illustrate their character rotations:

![image](./images/unnamed_1410.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1171.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_040.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_043.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_055.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_082.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_052.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_325.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_213.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_663.png)

![image](./images/unnamed_648.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_184.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_432.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_816.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1283.png)

**How many views must you create for your character?**

This often depends on the production's needs and even the time constraints of the schedule. The more primary the character is, the more screen-time they'll have, the more views and references need to be drawn. Usually the script and [storyboards][RuSmScFWJf] are a good indicator of how much the character will be present on-screen for. The more screen-time they have, the more they'll act and move around, therefore the more likely you'll need more views in their rotation for the model sheets to depict how they'll look from various sides. Main characters will be seen the most and will be in the most episodes (in a series) and therefore will need more references since more robust and refined builds will need to be made since they will be doing the most action and [acting][VZPMRybOIg].

![image](./images/unnamed_1269.jpg)

\@Spodness

![Turnarounds are an Essential Part of Character Design Sheet](./images/unnamed_330.jpg)

![How to Make a Character Design Sheet](./images/unnamed_633.jpg)

\@randybishopart

Sometimes **4-point turnarounds** consist of the **3/4 Front**, **Front**, **Side**, **3/4 Back views**, other times they consist of **Front**, **3/4 Front**, **3/4 Back**, **Back views**:

![image](./images/unnamed_906.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_806.png)

By Evan Palmer

It's just a matter of preference and often what the storyboards will show to be visible in the shots the characters will appear in. The less scenes and the less turns they will do, the less likely you will need to create multiple views for that character.

![image](./images/unnamed_597.jpg)

By Bob Flynn

**8-point turnarounds:**

> Notice all the horizontal guidelines â˜ï¸, these are vital to getting your turn-around views accurate

![image](./images/unnamed_083.gif)

By Renae Fong

![image](./images/unnamed_037.gif)

By Ujwal Nair

![image](./images/unnamed_015.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_025.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_085.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_060.gif)

[The Line Studios][AdULiOqUhz]

There's also an optional 5/8 and 7/8 views (the views between the Front and 3/4, and between 3/4 and Side), sometimes called _Off Centre Left_ and _Off Center Right_ (depending on which way they're facing) or _Front Near Left_ / _Front Near Right_, these are usually reserved for the main cast of characters, since they get lots more screen-time than incidental or secondary characters. Adding these inbetween views makes for a more complete rotation, examples of this further below.

To distinguish between these inbetween views like 5/8 and 7/8.

Remember that 5/8 is between the Front and 3/4, and 7/8 is between the 3/4 and Side:

![image](./images/unnamed_925.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1338.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_172.png)

Adding in these in-between views creates 10 and 12 point rotations.

**10-point turnarounds:**

![image](./images/unnamed_1084.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_508.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_117.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_183.png)

Even more inbetween views can be made, it all depends on the production style of the animation, and what is demanded of the characters in their stories. 12 point Turnarounds can have an additional view between the Side view and 3/4 Back view as well, or additional view between the 3/4 Front view and Side view. You could add more inbetween views for the full rotation, adding up to a 16 point turnaround.

**12-point turnarounds:**

![image](./images/unnamed_1047.jpg)

In addition, there's the view between the 3/4 Back and the Back View, these would be called **5/8 Back View**, as seen here.

![image](./images/unnamed_1407.jpg)

Sometimes you will need to create new poses between each main view of an 8-point rotation, to make it a 16-point turnaround:

![image](./images/unnamed_768.png)

It may seem technical or complicated, but when it comes to adding more inbetween views; these are actually easier to draw because it\'s a very close reference to the views around the ones you've already made. It becomes only a slight rotation change, often reusing the same artwork, and only shifting and turning the body parts slightly in perspective to complete these new views.

Turnarounds are not the only type of character model sheet you will want to create for builds artists and animators to reference. Some other common design sheets include: Expression Sheets, Pose Sheets, Color Keys, Hand Models, Mouth Charts, and various Style Guides!

### Expression Sheets

Creating an expression sheet is a great way to provide the animators with a basic guide on the way a character expresses different emotions. Try to include a combination of extreme and subtle expressions in your illustrations for these model sheets. Expression sheets are super fun to sketch as you can really explore a range of personalities for your character.

![image](./images/unnamed_064.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_085.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1085.jpg)

Have fun with poses and facial expressions - What you will find helpful in the design stage is sketching the characters in action (poses) as well as their facial expressions, to see what feels right and suits them best. Think about how this character would act in different situations. What kind of faces do they make when they are happy or excited or angry?

![image](./images/unnamed_146.png)

Let them emote and communicate! This is the moment when the characters reveal themselves and truly come to life.

Head tilts and rotations can be difficult to do, often you need to create a [construction][pXlCPOjLve] reference for yourself to see how it will look three-dimensionally. This way you can plan out how to accurately to show the facial features at various angles:

![image](./images/unnamed_1372.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_021.png)

[Head Angles][GZHFBuvLfI] or Head Charts are sometimes part of the Expressions Sheets, sometimes part of the Posing Sheets, sometimes they're a designated design asset on their own. [Head angles][dSeyJmTZcw] like this are common to be made for primary characters for important reference material for animators:

![image](./images/unnamed_1209.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1620.png)

Breaking down a character design's expressions can be great learning practice:

![image](./images/unnamed_1204.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1115.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1095.png)

Create your expressions to be unmistakably clear as to what they're doing, how they're feeling, and what is happening. Basically, always think about producing strong poses & expressions, not ambiguous ones.

An inspirational example here is some old _Tiny Toons_ expression sheets. There were some fantastic moments and some incredible animation shown in this series, and a few times some crazy over-the-top & hilarious situations were shown. The cartooniest and most enthusiastic artist on the crew was **Eddie Fitzgerald**. Here are some of his sketches of the _Tiny Toons_ cast:

![image](./images/unnamed_1475.jpg)

This Buster character looks pretty conservative for Eddie. His exaggerated style of expressions seem a bit subdued here, but these are still really good, really pushed, and great inspiration.

![image](./images/unnamed_841.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_607.jpg)

There\'s soooo much to learn from these. It\'s free, loose, energetic, and seemingly effortless drawings of expressions and body language. These show a special love for the character. Late in Eddie's career, he actually got to storyboard on the 1999 version of _Woody Woodpecker_ too.

![image](./images/unnamed_221.jpg)

Check out these awesome expressions. You have to be a real cartoonist to realize the creative beauty and humorous potential of Warner Bros\' seemingly most conservative character.

![image](./images/unnamed_350.jpg)

These are some more great poses. It\'s weird to see Porky wearing pants, but somehow Eddie manages to draw it well. He knows what people like about the old Porky Pig character.

![image](./images/unnamed_618.jpg)

You know somebody can really draw if they like to do back poses - and can make them funny! Many artists are afraid of drawing characters from the back - not Eddie. He embraces the backside.

![image](./images/unnamed_1319.jpg)

See how he can get perfectly clear poses, construction and attitudes in just a few lines on a tiny thumbnail drawing. Eddie\'s storyboards would be super fun to lay out, because he\'s done the hard part for you. You can\'t go wrong with appealing poses like this.

Here\'s some Plucky Duck expressions that look way better being rough and exaggerated than the final version that got toned-down on screen.

![image](./images/unnamed_889.jpg)

Eddie was one of the first directors during the idealistic days of _Tiny Toons_\' birth in 1989, and he was the perfect choice for the job. He had a unique vision, a strong individual drawing style and a really funny way of seeing the world. Plus, he intimately knew all the original _Looney Tunes_ cartoons that they were basing these baby versions on.

Use this as inspiration to stay loose in your roughs, be exaggerated, and use the wild poses/expressions as a starting point, then tone down, refine, and afterwards, reduce them down to the style and structure they need to be.

### Pose Sheets

Similar to expression sheets, pose sheets/action poses are used to show the type of gestures a character is likely to make. These can be really important to provide your animators with information on the way a character moves in accordance with their personality and how far to push dynamic poses as well. Whilst traditional character turnaround sheets can feel a bit stiff, pose sheets allow for a much wider range of motions and body language to be expressed.

![image](./images/unnamed_1257.png)

Your poses are meant to be the tip of the iceberg, making the observer wonder: what's this character's back story, and giving hints as to their personality and intentions.

![image](./images/unnamed_1405.jpg)
![Action Poses on A Model Sheet](./images/unnamed_1245.jpg)\@randybishopart
![image](./images/unnamed_1015.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_935.jpg)

- ðŸ‘‰ Inspiration for how to practice drawing the structure and volumes of your characters [here][PJLHYNuRvD].
- ðŸ‘‰ Inspiration for poses can be found everywhere, like "dynamic action posing" as we see [here][PKnbwZDnDV].

Use **C-Curves** and **S-Curves** in contrast when Posing your characters:

![image](./images/unnamed_567.png)

The **line of action** is a key ingredient to making your character's poses look more dynamic.

This helps your poses \"read\", it makes them clear and understandable and gives them a distinct non-ambiguous direction.

![image](./images/unnamed_813.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1383.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_753.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_649.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1493.png)

- â€‹â€‹ðŸ‘‰ [Don Corgi's advice on how to express body language in your poses][vcXtTpwpIE]
- ðŸ‘‰ [How to use reference images when posing][YiQqqygMnt]

![image](./images/unnamed_1444.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_834.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1136.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_917.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1199.png)

![image](./images/unnamed_563.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1045.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_668.png)

**Body language** is a crucial element in character design, used to convey a character\'s personality, emotions, and intentions through posture, gestures, and movement. It\'s a visual shorthand that helps audiences understand a character\'s inner state and relationship with others, even without dialogue. It\'s a visual shorthand that helps audiences understand a character\'s inner state and relationship with others, even without dialogue.

- **Conveying Personality:**
  - Body language can be used to visually represent a character\'s traits. For example, a confident character might have an upright posture, open body language (like hands on hips with relaxed shoulders), and a wide stance. A nervous character might slouch, cross their arms, shoulders up, and have a downcast gaze.
- **Expressing Emotions:**
  - Different postures and gestures can indicate various emotions, like joy, fear, confidence, or doubt. A character lowering or raising their hands might express fear, while a character with a straight back and head held high might convey confidence.
- **Showing Intentions:**
  - Body language can also reveal a character\'s intentions. For example, a character leaning forward might indicate they are trying to engage in conversation, while a character looking away might be trying to avoid something.
- **Building Relationships:**
  - Eye contact and how characters physically relate to each other (e.g., touching, embracing, or keeping a distance) can also convey the nature of their relationship.
- **Considering Context:**
  - The context of the scene and the overall style of the project (e.g., realistic drama, cartoon comedy) will also influence the type of body language used. A realistic drama might require subtle and nuanced body language, while a cartoon comedy might be more exaggerated.
- **Individual Voice:**
  - Each character should have their own unique body language, reflecting their personality and habits. Universal gestures (like smiling, nodding, or shaking hands) can be supplemented with individual quirks, like shaking a leg when nervous or avoiding eye contact.

![image](./images/unnamed_273.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1021.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1091.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1304.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1320.jpg)

**Samples of Pose Sheets:**

![image](./images/unnamed_612.jpg)

![image](./images/unnamed_528.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_707.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1004.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_590.jpg)

Drawing your characters in various perspectives and angles can be the most difficult thing to do, especially when the design of your character is very flat and stylized, they may not be very "three-dimensionally-friendly".

![image](./images/unnamed_427.jpg)

**Pushing the pose** doesn't always mean exaggerating the gesture to the max - it means that each exaggeration is further supporting the 'big idea' of the pose with readable emotions:

![image](./images/unnamed_399.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1449.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_422.png)

**What does your pose say about your character?**

![Example of shoulder up "aggressive" and shoulder down "passive" poses](./images/unnamed_952.jpg)

The pose you choose for your character can communicate a lot about their personality, emotions, and desires. For example, using a concave (c-shaped,) line of action for your character's pose can help them seem timid or sad, whereas a convex curve can be used to make them seem more confident or happy.

So, before you choose your pose, think about what you want to say about your character and how you can best illustrate this information through curves, tilts, and expressions!

![One character six sitting poses](./images/unnamed_244.jpg)

Drawings by Tony Bancroft â˜ï¸

There are so many different ways you can alter your character's pose to make it more effective.

![image](./images/unnamed_921.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_716.png)

Once you have got your pose roughed out, you can start thinking about how you will pose your character. An easy way to check whether or not your pose is working is to create a silhouette of the pose and see if it still reads well. Good poses are easily understandable even when silhouetted!

Block in the character. If you can still recognize what their body language is doing, then it has a strong, readable silhouette.

![image](./images/unnamed_1329.png)

By making strong posing choices for your characters you will greatly improve the readability of your character designs!

![image](./images/unnamed_1059.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_819.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1210.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1460.png)

Recognizability in silhouettes can be great for your audience to instantly identify the character on screen.

![image](./images/unnamed_093.png)

Especially when making a cast of various characters, some thought should be put into the character's outer shape. Ideally some contrast between a pair or trio of characters makes for a more appealing ensemble.

![image](./images/unnamed_1515.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_997.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1325.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_448.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1013.jpg)

The Corpse Bride:

![image](./images/unnamed_311.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_296.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_182.png)

Using real photo references is always good inspiration:

![image](./images/unnamed_1511.png)

When posing your character, you will come across some logistics you may not have considered before:

![image](./images/unnamed_1686.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_270.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1722.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1622.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_142.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_560.png)

\*Not all design styles have varied line weight or tapered lines, so follow the style reference first and foremost, if you are designing the characters and determining these varied [line weights][SErFxVZBuS], experiment with and without them, and consult your director/producer/client for any advice on preferences and how it can impact things down the line in builds and animation.

Start off loose and rough to figure out what body language or attitude you want to try out, then draw over these foundational sketches with your character's design.

To discover the range of motion with your characters, begin with rough thumbnails drawings:

![image](./images/unnamed_932.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_855.jpg)

**Stages of Drawing Poses:**

![image](./images/unnamed_531.png)

Thumbnail - Rough - Clean-up - Color

The thumbnail/rough stage of posing allows you to see aspects of the character you couldn't see before. Look back to what you found out about the character before you started designing, when researching different aspects of the character, you're being purely creative and exploring, you do a Turnaround but once you start POSING your character, you see areas in the design that might need to be revisited and tweaked. It's like taking your model out for a _test drive_.

Then you enter the "clean-up" phase where you refine the posing, correct any issues with [proportions][EXidESPzYn], volumes and find the final line you want to have.

![image](./images/unnamed_060.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_440.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_938.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_203.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1592.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_557.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1333.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_563.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_547.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1022.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_692.jpg)

When posing, start off loose and rough to get a feel of how the character's acting and attitudes could come through.

Sometimes pose sheets and expression sheets can be combined, their purpose is always to show animators and builders/riggers what the range of anatomy and flexibility is intended for the character's style, and how to illustrate various attitudes and postures.

See Victor Navone's collection of thumbnail sketches [here][KzeqASNAjY]. He developed these to work out the posing and acting for the characters in his shots prior to animating his scenes. Created as a way to rehearse the poses for the best performance and actions, finding the attitude and mindset of the characters. He'd use these sketches as a way to explore how to depict their mood and body language.

> ðŸ‘‰ Illustrator Cedric Hohnstadt has this insightful 95-minute video about how to improve your character posing skills [here][rqnHbVfACl].

**Variations & Re-dressing:**

Often, creating variations in costuming and hair styling can be common practice before full turnarounds are drawn. This way the director can see what looks better for the purposes of the story, location, and the sequence the characters are in.

![image](./images/unnamed_1211.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1586.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1339.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_908.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_867.png)

Other variations are often designed for extras and secondary or incidental characters, they serve as background filler and often share similar interchangeable design traits to keep things efficient and simple:

![image](./images/unnamed_077.png)

By Marta Andreeva

Incidental characters are usually background crowds or random extras needing to populate the scene, reacting to the events transpiring in the shots. Sometimes you're able to re-use body types, interchange clothing, hair styles and skin tones in order to be more efficient with your time and to easily add variety amongst a crowd of characters.

![image](./images/unnamed_871.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1291.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1188.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_937.jpg)

Variations in age are more rare, but still a possibility depending on the story being told with the character. For example, if there will be flashbacks (or flash-forwards) or visual ways to chronologically depict the character at various stages in their life, then variants in the designs to show younger and older versions will be needed. In which case, displaying the states of their changing physical appearance needs to be mapped out.

![image](./images/unnamed_1457.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_519.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_744.png)

Sometimes when developing the character, variations are requested to make them a bit older or younger, adjusting [proportions][tJwigYIbKD] and hairstyles/clothing can go a long way in showing these iterations.

![image](./images/unnamed_1223.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_056.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_988.png)

Learning to draw and pose hands is very important, even the most 'rigged' animated productions will need many custom-built hands and as much reference as possible for how to draw hands in the appropriate style.
