### Color Styling

A **color stylist** (also known as color designers) chooses the colors for characters, props, and FX in an animated production.

Using their knowledge of color theory, their skills as a painter, and their design sensibilities, they create palettes and lighting that allows the characters to fit within the backgrounds and match the overall style of the show.

As always, it's important to remember no two productions are alike, and the roles of a color stylist can vary greatly from show to show.

![image](./images/unnamed_196.jpg)

When a color stylist first begins on a show they will receive information from the Art Director about the style of the show and the various painting approaches that are employed when coloring the characters. This is known as a style guide.

Whether an actual document or just a conversation with the Art Director, the style guide serves to give parameters to the color stylists to help the character treatments look consistent.

For example, a style guide might say such things as...

> _"The shadows of the characters are always hard edged and so when you are blocking out shadows, use the lasso tool."_

![image](./images/unnamed_329.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_442.png)

> ðŸ‘‰ [The Color Wheel and How it Works!][ziHXPZcBXE]

Terms to know:

- Hue is color (blue, green, red, etc.)
- Chroma is the purity of a color (a high chroma has no added black, white or gray)
- Saturation refers to how strong or weak a color is (high saturation being strong/vibrant)
- Value refers to how light or dark a color is (light having a high value)
- Tones are created by adding gray to a color, making it duller than the original
- Shades are created by adding black to a color, making it darker than the original
- Tints are created by adding white to a color, making it lighter than the original

#### Handout

The color stylist's job begins once the clean black and white character designs have been approved and turned.

Similar to the other design positions, the color stylist receives their new assignments from the Art Director along with any caveats or specific instructions the Art Director would like for the color scheme.

The Art Director will also give them any context for the character/scene that may affect color.

Once the Art Director has delegated the designs, it's off to the races!

![image](./images/unnamed_1067.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_626.jpg)

#### Paint With All The Colors

While the Art Director may have some specific thoughts about certain aspects of the character, the color stylist for the most part makes all of the decisions about the color schemes of the character from hair color to skin color to outfit color/coordination.

Occasionally there may be characters that directly references or parody a real person and in these specific cases, the palette might be pulled from the reference in order to help it as a reference or something already in existence.

> ðŸ‘‰ See [here][XwglmnmQWk] for the fundamentals of color theory and terminology.

![image](./images/unnamed_836.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1423.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1120.jpg)

Sometimes "color roughs" are needed, where the artwork is still rough, but a color pass is needed:

![image](./images/unnamed_924.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1581.png)

> ðŸ‘‰ [Color Theory for Artists][UhUNZklDYJ]

![image](./images/unnamed_1380.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_450.jpg)

#### Matching The Backgrounds

Another very important consideration the color designer has to make is whether the character's color scheme/treatment will make sense against the background.

Often, color stylists will bring in the finished backgrounds where available to make sure that the color scheme of the world and the color scheme of the character are logical and aesthetically pleasing.

![image](./images/unnamed_1405.png)

Your character colors can clash with the background, it's always good to make a quick test to see:

![image](./images/unnamed_638.jpg)

#### Color Design Review

Once the color stylist has done a few color iterations (usually 3 to 5) of the character in 3/4 front view, it is time for the Art Director and Showrunner to review.

The Showrunner will pick their preferred color scheme from the line... or possibly frankenstein pieces from different versions.

For example: The Showrunner may say, _"Let's use the hair from #1, but the skin tone and outfit from #4"_

The color design usually goes through two or three reviews before it is approved.

Once the 3/4 front view colored version of the character is approved, the color stylist will then go in and paint the rest of the black and white turn around.

![image](./images/unnamed_483.png)

It's a full time job because you have to do that for every single character, prop, or FX in every single lighting situation that happens in a show. The character designer(s) on a production can sometimes do the colors for their own characters. The smaller the production, the more tasks each artist is made to do. On small productions, a designer may have to do concepts, roughs, turnarounds, clean-up and colors for all the design assets they create. While on larger productions, those tasks could be divided up amongst 4 or 5 different people since there is such a large amount of characters to do each week, designing characters, props and color styling ALL of them.

![image](./images/unnamed_1273.png)

Color Design might sound like a relatively simple job, but it's not. You have to take into account a lot of things: how the colors will read on multiple surfaces and backgrounds (and clashing with multiple colors within one background), when you should have shadows on the model, how the shadow actually affects the model, why you're choosing cool colors or warm colors, how colors look after they've been compressed to be seen on phones versus broadcast TV/streaming, and even the subconscious meaning of colors culturally/psychologically.

> ðŸ‘‰ See [here][uZSpMhLFdX] for how color combinations can be applied.

Colors combinations can help to create a very iconic casts of character:

![image](./images/unnamed_896.png)

#### Local Colors and Lighting

Color stylists create and set the character palettes for the characters, props and FX within an episode, taking into account lighting changes that might happen while said elements are on screen. Something that can vary from show to show is whether color stylists are responsible for doing the light adjustments for characters under different lighting conditions.

Local Colors (the base colors of a character under neutral lighting) can be greatly impacted by the lighting of specific shots.

_For example: A scene takes place in a nightclub with neon lights could turn a brunette character's hair bright pink or have rim lights added on._

Sometimes color stylists do these lighting passes, and sometimes they are done by the Art Director.

If you watch the [Infinity Train][reCiPfWneU] [pilot][FoigFWwsLV], Tulip is a different color palette in the puzzle car, a different palette outside, a different palette in the corgi car, a different palette inside the temple, and a different palette in the foggy area. Then she has to have a different palette every time she looks at glowing numbers on her hand in each of those locations. All of that just for one character, not including any props or lighting that affects other characters.

Here's a quick rundown from that pilot episode (note, the colors are a little washed out because the screenshots were from youtube, but you can still see the difference):

![image](./images/unnamed_1005.jpg)

As you can see, she doesn't just have a green hoodie, she has like 9 different green hoodies. When eye-dropping these colors, there are colors in there you don't even realize were being used in there. These are all decisions that a color stylist makes.

> ðŸ‘‰ Here's an excellent portfolio example from an experienced color stylist: [Grey Chen][FWSnjwqNeQ]

Here's a short guide by [Jess Marfisi][YBUtjoolAD]:

![image](./images/unnamed_1459.jpg)

For example, a character wouldn't be colored the same in broad daylight than in complete darkness, right?

![image](./images/unnamed_789.jpg)

[LINK][rxQkTmHLsv]

So **Props** and **FX** need to be colored as well, keeping the character and background colors in mind while designing the color palettes, so that they will go well together side-by-side. Every object that a character touches and every puff of smoke or falling leaves that might show up in an episode all need to be designed.

![image](./images/unnamed_489.png)

A pool cleaner from the _Craig of the Creek_ episode ["Lost in the Sewers"][aaoHyvdaaJ]. Note that the color stylists have set the palette for when the object is in shadow. The episode was designed by [Maaike Scherff][zlrdLrTfHb] and [Angie Wang][OZGVvnjyXf]. Color by [Carolyn Ramirez][VcYSmrZJsy].

![image](./images/unnamed_1118.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_697.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1395.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_506.jpg)

Here's an example of very specific FX design from the _Unikitty_ episode "Action Forest". The eye glint effect has been given several frames of action for animation reference. Designed and colored by Jess Marfisi.

> ðŸ‘‰ Here's an excellent portfolio example from an experienced color stylist: [Cam Chan][rLIjtPxWIK]

This can vary from show to show, not all productions have the color stylish responsible for exclusively character colors, it's common for them to also do all the colors for other objects and FX. On some productions, the color stylist is responsible for any color design that is not a background.

On other productions, there may be a whole other designer for props, oftentimes a prop designer both creates the designs and colors for all hand-held objects featured in the series.

![image](./images/unnamed_1009.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1619.png)

Another thing that can improve the effectiveness of your character design sheet is a color guide! To do this, all you need is a simple key of all the color swatches used in the character design, alongside an example illustration of the character in full color. This is really helpful when you are handing off a design for a whole team of artists to use, as it ensures all of the colors remain consistent.

> "Digital media gives access to every color out there. While this is truly amazing, I think limiting yourself to a few key colors is actually a better strategy. Using a limited color palette helps a sketch feel cohesive. Using too many colors can sometimes create a sense of messiness and a lack of focus. I would say to stick with between one and three primary colors initially. Afterwards, you can dive between the values of each tone when detailing. I truly believe that color is the most important piece of the puzzle when creating eye-catching art. I've done a few "tests" on social media. When posting a drawing with no color, versus the same drawing with added colors, the latter will always get a stronger reception. So if it vibes with your artistic vision: be bright, be bold. It also doesn't hurt to get inspiration from photos or other artists' color palettes." -Erik Ly

![image](./images/unnamed_793.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_937.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_260.jpg)

#### Right on Schedule

Similar to both background design and character design, there are usually about 2 weeks allotted in a production schedule for color design for each episode, however the timeframe can vary. These 2-week-cycles include producing the color concepts, having them reviewed, painting the one view of the character again, having that reviewed, revising those colors, then painting the other views, at least 5 views for a primary characters and at least 2 views for a secondary/incidental character, and usually you are working on two episodes at once, with multiple characters, with some partial re-use and re-dressing from previous episodes, and all overlapping at various stages.

Color stylists usually finish around 3 to 4 fully-colored character turns per day. Sometimes a very extensive character may take more iterations of exploration.

![image](./images/unnamed_465.png)

Lighting Keys:

![image](./images/unnamed_1528.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_401.png)

#### Rim Light and Rim Shadow

Sometimes you need to make a custom color palette for night time scenes, which includes creating samples for light sources and how they are painted.

![image](./images/unnamed_788.jpg)

Sometimes it's not just a dark tint, but an entirely new color scheme:

![image](./images/unnamed_1002.jpg)

Night time colors with shadows:

![image](./images/unnamed_1378.jpg)

#### Workflow Pipeline

Start off with only coloring one view at a time, you want your color palette approved before applying it to all the other turns.

Also, if you were not the one that designed the characters (you are painting someone else's design) can you communicate with them about "color details"? - This is the discretion of adding in some highlights or shading or the removal of lines once the shape is painted.

![image](./images/unnamed_049.jpg)

Or the adding of textures or patterns, like this:

![image](./images/unnamed_1172.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1017.jpg)

Because character design can sometimes shift right up until the episode's assets go out to the Builds Artists and Scene Setup crew, sometimes color design can also be tweaked after the design was already approved. It's not the norm, but it happens!

![image](./images/unnamed_1016.jpg)

Most shows have a level of flexibility and fluidity in their pipeline to account for changes that may occur along the way.

No pipeline is perfect and TV animation schedules are notoriously hectic with many many moving parts all working at the same time with various stages all overlapping each other with many levels of changes and adjustments being made on every design asset right up until they get given to the animation crew.

![image](./images/unnamed_1341.jpg)

#### Who Do I Work With? - Part two

Primarily, color stylists work closest with the Art Director, this is the person foremost in charge of the overall look of the series.

The Art Director guides and supports the designers to make sure all the color treatments suit the style of the show.

Color Stylists may communicate with Character Designers to discuss their black and white designs which are the basis the color stylists work off of.

![image](./images/unnamed_1373.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_706.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1317.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_246.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1550.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_513.png)

#### Alternates (ALT)

Sometimes creating versions of color schemes and surface details are referred to as ALTs.

![image](./images/unnamed_341.jpg)

#### Who Do I Affect? - Part two

Color Stylists most heavily affect the Builds team, they take the finished and approved color designs and convert them into rigged assets, which then goes to the Scene Setup (scene prep) crew to assemble each individual shot for each sequence of the episode, which then goes to the animation crew. When the animators open up their assigned scenes, they see everything has been imported, placed in, sized up and laid out for them to use, and then they can begin [animating][dHEZequKzE] the characters.

> Early color explorations models can be made to determine which combination works best:

#### COLOR CONCEPTS

![image](./images/unnamed_1092.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_693.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_134.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_149.png)

Placing the color exploration designs over the background art of the location the characters will be at, is usually a great way to see how the colors will work together. This way you can arrive at the colors most appropriate for the scene:

![image](./images/unnamed_839.png)

These can be called "color keys" as well, since they give you a snapshot of what a finished scene would look like.

So the background colors are usually more subject to change since we want the characters to stay consistent in their personal color schemes.

![image](./images/unnamed_199.png)

Color explorations are often needed to see which tones and palettes work best - for the purposes of character personality.

Also, placing your painted character in their environment (if one is available) is a good way to spot any clashing colors as well, or to adjust for the possibility of the character blending in with the background too much.

> ðŸ‘‰ [This Simple Color Theory Always Works!][ktrKnPFPIU]

![image](./images/unnamed_551.png)

Usually a character stays in their same outfit for the entire episode, and most of the time for entire lengths of multiple seasons! The Simpsons, for example, have been wearing the same clothes for over 40 years.

![image](./images/unnamed_1080.png)

Character "redress" is when a different versions is required to be designed for your character, this could be a drenched in water version, a dirty/muddy version, or a covered-in-paint version, but most commonly it's a hairstyle or costume change:

![image](./images/unnamed_801.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_724.jpg)

> ðŸ‘‰ [Wouter Tulp's Advice on Color!][YjBXckxSlT]

Sometimes less is more. You can limit the color palette for unity and impact. When working with three colors, keep the **60-30-10** rule in mind. Pick one color to make up about 60% of the character, a second color to make up about 30%, and the last color is about 10%.

![image](./images/unnamed_1234.png)

When working with just two colors, use the **70-30** rule. One color is about 70%, the second is about 30%.

![image](./images/unnamed_605.png)

Colors have a similar subconscious effect on our visual interpretation. Creating characters with an appealing and suitable color palette can make an immediate impact in grabbing and holding a viewer's attention. There are a few different aspects of color theory to consider when designing your character:

#### Symbolism

Each color carries its own innate effects on a color scheme. Red is universally alert and energetic, white is universally neutral. Warm colors are associated with action while cool ones are linked with calmness.

If you're creating content for an international audience it's worthwhile keeping in mind that different cultures have different views on color symbolism. Red, for example, can signify danger in the western world, but happiness in China. Likewise, blue represents freedom in many parts of Europe, but can symbolize villainy in Japan. Your intended audience will determine your use of [color symbolism][XVzLkpjNEA].

#### Color Relationships

The relationship between the different colors in your character design is just as important, if not more so, than the separate color significance. Some of the most well-known color arrangements include: monochromatic (different light values of the same hue); Complementary (two colors which are directly opposite each other on the color wheel), Analogous (colors which are adjacent on the color wheel), and Triadic (three colors spread around the color wheel in a triangle formation).

Each color scheme and color combo has its own advantages: monochromatic and analogous palettes are often soothing, complementary colors are both contrasting and harmonious, while triadic color schemes are dynamic and rich. Take a look at these color wheels and use these systems to help find a color scheme that'll be naturally pleasing to the eye.

![image](./images/unnamed_068.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_033.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_029.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_026.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_968.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_426.png)
![color>orange | Winnie the pooh drawing, Disney characters christmas, Tigger](./images/unnamed_282.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_105.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_405.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_372.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1102.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1549.png)

You can see how the character's colors can showcase their personalities. How we perceive color's significance and meaning is both psychological and cultural.

> ðŸ‘‰ [Color Theory in Storytelling!][LSdmPuhiuI]

#### Complementary Colors

Complementary colors are the exact opposite of each other on the color wheel. This gives a highly contrasting and bold look. If you want your character to stand out, a complementary color scheme is perfect!

![Complementary color scheme](./images/unnamed_164.jpg)

As you can see in the below example, the use of complementary colors for a character design really allows for the details to pop in the illustration! This can be a useful tool when you are creating a protagonist who needs to be the focus of your drawing.

![A character design with complementary color scheme](./images/unnamed_1333.jpg)\@meikearts

#### Monochromatic Colors

A monochromatic color scheme uses only the tones, tints, and shades from a single hue on the color wheel. This creates a really unique and often emotive look in an illustration. If you are looking to make relatively simple color choices, this could be the best method for you!

![Monocromatic color scheme](./images/unnamed_835.jpg)

This color scheme is great for creating characters with well-defined traits that may be best expressed through a specific color. If you have a very complex character design, monochromatic colors can also help simplify their appearance and make it more visually appealing.

![image](./images/unnamed_1192.png)

A monochromatic color scheme is also a great choice if you are looking to improve your understanding of values when coloring and shading your work!

![Example of a character in monochromatic color](./images/unnamed_1143.jpg)\@meikearts

#### Triadic Colors

If you are looking to have a bit more variation in your color scheme, the Triadic method might be just what you need! These color combinations use 3 colors that are equally spaced apart on the color wheel.

When it comes to using triadic colors, it is best to choose one of the three to be the most prominent in your illustrations and use the other 2 for highlighting smaller details.

![Triadic color scheme](./images/unnamed_195.jpg)

This color scheme creates a very vibrant look, even when the colors are toned down. It is best used for characters with playful energy, as these color combinations can often feel very youthful. This makes triadic colors perfect for characters aimed at a younger audience!

![Example of triadic color scheme](./images/unnamed_568.jpg)\@meikearts

#### Warm vs Cool Colors

![Cool vs Warm Colors](./images/unnamed_278.jpg)

When it comes to using warm vs cool colors there are a few key things to consider. When added to an illustration, warm colored objects will typically feel closer to the viewer, whereas cooler ones will feel further away. This is because of atmospheric perspective, which is essentially just the effect of particles in the atmosphere making things further away have a bluish tint.

The color temperature of your characters will have a similar effect, for example, warmer-colored characters will stand out and feel confident, whereas cooler-toned characters may appear more closed off and mysterious.

![Include Color Swatches in Character Design Sheet](./images/unnamed_1343.jpg)

\@meikearts

![image](./images/unnamed_045.gif)

By Noah Ruff

Remember, sometimes there's added details that need to be inserted in. Like rotations of hands, the underneath area of hair, arms, robes, capes, cloaks and wings, including underneath the feet and the inside of mouths - these are all factors that need to be designed for certain productions in order to provide consistent design and color reference for animators later on.

- **Video essays to watch:**
- ðŸ‘‰ [Color Theory in Character Development 1][pgjizKEHsb]
- ðŸ‘‰ [Color Theory in Character Development 2][WMtwqyZUbL]

Consider incorporating colors that contrast with the main ones you intend to use in the production. This can help accentuate specific parts of the character that you want to highlight.

â€![image](./images/unnamed_368.png)

- ðŸ‘‰ [Suggestions for color combos][PonveIDUuM]
- ðŸ‘‰[Resource for color palettes][ljVQXqQcKs]

â€- Red: Passion, Love, Anger, Creativity

- Orange: Energy, Happiness, Vitality
- Yellow: Happiness, Hope, Deceit
- Green: New Beginnings, Abundance, Nature
- Blue: Calm, Responsible, Sadness
- Purple: Royalty, Wealth
- Black: Mystery, Elegance, Evil
- Gray: Moody, Conservative, Formality

![image](./images/unnamed_264.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1700.png)

Also, it's important to note that even though you can use as many colors as you want, it is recommended not to span the chromatic variance of the characters too much due to the simplicity of the models.

Example of the muted color variations found in the characters of _Paranorman_ from Laika Studios:

![image](./images/unnamed_287.jpg)

Muted colors are the opposite of vivid colors, and they are created by simply adding black, white, or grey to a base color.

![image](./images/unnamed_1483.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_188.jpg)

Dull or muted colors like these, refers to colors that have a low saturation or chroma.

![image](./images/unnamed_030.jpg)

By Dermot O' Connor

Other elements to include in color keys (color model sheets) are night-time color schemes, any semi-transparent elements, and what colors can be found in their inner sleeves and under their shoes:

![image](./images/unnamed_326.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_596.jpg)

By William Nichols

#### COLOR BREAKDOWNS

![image](./images/unnamed_624.jpg)

Another aspect a color stylist needs to do is to provide alternate versions of the model (usually close-ups) so that other hard-to-see areas are made more visibly clear. Insides of mouths, insides of sleeves and collars, eyelids, and under shoes/feet, just to name a few examples.

When a whole cast of characters are arranged together, often adjustments need to be made so that the color schemes for each character that are often seen together should compliment each other, and not clash or compete with each other.

![image](./images/unnamed_1198.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_636.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_832.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1309.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_943.png)

In 3DCG animated productions, these color designs are used by UV/Texture artists to re-create the surfaces & color shaders for the character models.

There's many types of color schemes you can create, try out a few different kinds for your characters to see what works best, experiment and see which ones match your characters' personalities too.

![image](./images/unnamed_1461.png)

> ðŸ‘‰ See [here][xKEBYhPQDt] for more fundamentals of color theory.

Color Styling / Color Designing is an integral part of the animation production pipeline.

It's not unusual for color stylists to move into background paint, and maybe eventually to an Art Director role. Everyone's journey is different.

![image](./images/unnamed_1327.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1165.png)

Being observant of the way colors interact in the world around you, and exploring different ways to build out color palettes will sharpen the skills you need to excel as a color stylist.

![image](./images/unnamed_634.jpg)

#### Advice for the Aspiring Color Designer

If you're interested in becoming a color stylist/designer, here are some helpful things to keep in mind for both your personal growth and your portfolio!

1. **Characters and Colors**
   - A strong color design portfolio shows you know how to make clever and appealing color choices.
2. **Embrace the BG**
   - Character color palettes must always exist within a world, having work in your portfolio that includes a background, and shows you know how to color key a character to their world in an appealing way. If you don't have your own BG to use, grab one from anywhere and BLUR it a lot. If you're still not game with that, use a dark gray background, it's better than stark white.
3. **Light and Variation**
   - Another exercise that can both help you hone your skills and showcase them in a portfolio, is doing several color variations of the same design... as well as doing lighting variations to show a character under varying lights (example: warm light, cold light, neon light).
4. **Masks Abound**
   - Less of a thing for a portfolio and more of a skill to practice is utilizing layer masks to cut and color different parts of your character designs and character linework. While each show will have its own style guide, getting familiar with the use of layers and masks can make it easier to transition to designing within a production pipeline. In a TV production, making changes fast and easily is everything so it's important to become acquainted with setting files up in a way that allows you to color shift quickly if needed.
5. **Reference and Style**
   - An important consideration color stylists make is how to choose color schemes that are appropriate for the character in the sense of age, personality, and fashion. Looking up references of different outfits, palettes and clothing sets will help hone your eye for what sorts of colors pair nicely and what the effect of such colors have. Example: More muted, earth tone colors tend to read as "mature" while brighter colors tend to read more youthful, and black.gray may read as edgy/dark. The color of an outfit can radically change the way a character's personality/attitude will be interpreted.

### Mouth Charts

For reference for lip sync animation, designs must be made of the various mouth positions that animators will need for reference. There are many different ways of going about it, typically there's anywhere from 8 to 14 main key positions (for each view) to make.

![image](./images/unnamed_076.gif)

Front, 3/4 Front and Side views are the 3 positions to build mouth charts for.

As with everything, the style of the production will dictate how exaggerated or subtle the changes in the mouth shapes will be, and how far to push mouth shapes and expressions. Also, usually there's a '**happy**' version and a '**sad/angry**' version made for each speaking character, sometimes a third '**neutral**' version as well. For main characters, a front, side, and 3/4 view is made for each set of mouths as well.

![image](./images/unnamed_795.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_718.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_296.jpg)

Even with simple/cartoony mouths, keep the character's anatomy (and graphic style) in mind, think about what range of squash and stretch you may need, what are the limitations, many characters need to talk without teeth as well, many factors need to be considered:

![image](./images/unnamed_1464.png)

These mouth shape guides are usually the last step in the creation of a full character model pack, as you can see, different productions have different codes and slightly different variations on the look for each syllable.

### Size Comparison Charts

Line up your cast of characters side by side to create a reference chart for every character's scale relative to one and other. This is usually made later on the the design process, once the main/primary characters of the series or film are made. All other characters and props created afterwards, use the one main character as the ruler to visually see the scale for all other designs in comparison. Storyboard artists and Animators will use these charts as reference throughout the production.

![image](./images/unnamed_328.png)

### Distance Models

A less common aspect of character design for animation is creating 'distance models'. As the name suggests, these are characters that have been re-designed to have less details so that they are more easily read-able as they are further away in the distance.

Usually only seen in 2D animated series. Simply shrinking down the usual design causes the lines to get mashed together, so new custom-made models for far shots are created, to make a better and more readable design.

Here's a typical **turnaround model sheet**:

![image](./images/unnamed_822.png)

And here's a custom design made for a **Distance Model**:

![image](./images/unnamed_136.png)

So they can be reduced in size and their simplified linework will make the character more recognizable from afar:

![image](./images/unnamed_136.png)

More samples:

![image](./images/unnamed_1429.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_739.jpg)

Here's they are in action:

![image](./images/unnamed_542.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1065.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_654.png)

These models are made for **Wide Shots/Long Shots**, when the subject is far away from the camera.

Not every production uses them, but something to keep in mind.

You can also have 'extra detail' versions of character models for close-ups:

![image](./images/unnamed_1489.png)

### Style Guides

Design Guides, Posing Guides, Drawing Guides, Breakdown Sheets, Construction Models, Style Guides - there's lots of names for them, depending on which department creates them, what group they're for, which studio produces them, and what sort of production they're for. These are becoming more numerous and more frequent in this industry to be made and used, and usually assembled once characters are all completed and finalized. The more time there is in the schedule for them, the more 'DOs & DONTs' and 'How to Draw' guides are drafted from the character designs, art directors and sometimes the showrunners/series directors, to help other artists & animators understand their construction and mechanics.

For example, here are some DOs & DONTs/breakdown sheets made for animators to use as reference for the Devil character from the _Cuphead_ series:

![image](./images/unnamed_400.png)

Hand Guides from _Green Eggs and Ham_:

![image](./images/unnamed_1479.png)

Hand models are a unique aspect of character design in 2D animation. They are the one item that is difficult to build a library of assets for, often lots of examples of hand poses are made, but unlike all other body parts hands are usually drawn out by the animator. Pre-made hands can be made available in the build for the character, but animators will need to also custom-make new hands all the time for all the actions, gestures and acting performed by the characters. Therefore as much reference as possible should be made for them to use as a guide for how to draw and pose hands.

> ðŸ‘‰ See lots more from The Art of SpongeBob SquarePants [here][bUqSXmojjE].

The internet grows exponentially every year, and now more than ever, you'll see old and new design sheets and breakdown sheets like these becoming available.

If the production is more traditionally-animated in style ([hand-drawn][JrWHzXUThg]), then there will be more guides produced on how to draw and pose the characters. If the style is more puppet/cut-out style, then libraries of pre-drawn body parts are made. The animators pick and choose which body part and pose they need. Then stop-motion-style, they move and swap the pieces around frame by frame, therefore much less drawing is required.

### Storytelling in Character Design

An informative essay by [I][xMAGMMIqfX] [ndia Swift and Michael Doig][zrXYzcNBMw]:

Designing characters can be so much more than just creating a \'cool\' or \'appealing\' image. True innovation comes from digging deep into your character\'s personality and their role in your story!

It\'s not too much of an overestimation to say that character design is one of the most sought after positions for freelancers and studio artists. Designing characters all day is an amazing, fun and inventive process that allows for a lot of rough exploration and idea generation, so it\'s no wonder that so many people want to pursue it as a career. To become truly excellent, I believe it\'s important to weave the character\'s central concept/conflict into their visual identity!

I\'m not a professional character designer, but I wanted to talk a little about the way I go about designing my characters and why I do it that way! Hopefully it will help people avoid some of the common pitfalls. Please feel free to share your tips and advice in the comments below, too. I would love to hear your thoughts and techniques!

#### Way Past Cool

The most obvious and common pitfall is designing a character just to look \'cool\'. A character should be designed around a function or personality! Sonic is designed to look cool, sure - but he is also designed to be the fastest thing allliiiiiive! Hence the \'blown back\' spikes (from running so fast, obviously) reminiscent of comic speed lines, his red racing car sneakers, and the endless triangles in his designs!

![image](./images/unnamed_613.png)

You should have known I would use Sonic as an example. I love Sonic. More about shape design later!

His cool attitude is exemplified not only through his design, but through his pose. Getting inventive with the way the character stands or sits, using that to squeeze the most personality into your design you can is key! No more t-posing or facing the camera with hands by your sides.

![image](./images/unnamed_1633.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_594.png)

Have fun with it. How would your character sit, stand, wait, run? All of this is part of the design of the character, not just the job of the animator. When you think about iconic characters like [Bugs Bunny][tdinZPPyHJ], [Superman][pLmveyPLgf], or [Jafar][FKbKEfwjhd], they all have poses that they call to your mind. An important thing to note too is that they are all confident characters. However, the way that this comes through in their posing is very unique to each - they all hold themselves completely differently.

#### Keep it simple

So, a side effect of having no true idea of purpose or function to your character, and of designing them simply to look appealing is festooning your character in stuff that clutters up their design. Needs more belts!

![image](./images/unnamed_019.png)

Try to give any accessories your character has a reason for being there. It could be environmental - maybe if they live in a harsh world, they need to wear/equip some kind of day to day protection. Maybe it\'s something that was given to them and has sentimental value. Maybe it\'s a relic of their past. Maybe it demonstrates their personality. Whatever it is, use it as a way to deepen the world or the character. If it doesn\'t have a function, your character design might be better off without it.

![image](./images/unnamed_1245.png)

Using Midge from my comic [The Girl and the Glim][WetrvGUuMn] as an example, I chose to give her two accessories. One is a friendship bracelet, and one is her brother\'s old hoodie. Midge is a character whose core conflict revolves around the fear that people won\'t like her. The bracelet represents how valuable friendship is to her as well as being an indication of her past, a reminder that moving house means she has left her friends behind. The hoodie is a familiar object that Midge uses to hide herself from others. It helps me a lot when demonstrating her shyness and gives me lots of nice posing options that are unique to her as a character.

> \"Perfection is achieved, not when there is nothing more to add, but when there is nothing left to take away.\" \- Antoine de Saint-ExupÃ©ry

#### Style and Influences

The style you choose to design your characters in will vary depending on the story you are designing them for. Your target audience will also play a big role in these decisions! Setting Stranger Things in the 80\'s had no functionality story-wise. The same story could easily have been told with a modern day setting. However, the creators/designers knew that the story was targeting the generation who grew up on The Goonies and E.T., and wanted to evoke that nostalgia in their audience, and so they chose to set the story within this time period to create the kind of mood and atmosphere they felt would connect with their audience.

![image](./images/unnamed_1138.png)

Each of the styles here communicate something different about the story\'s intent.

The Ghibli style character top left is unlikely to be involved in the same kind of story as the screaming Arin Hanson caricature below her. Likewise the far right Unicorn\'s world will be very different to the bird girl\'s below.

Choosing a style that communicates is important, as establishing audience expectation and then not living up to that promise can cause a knee-jerk reaction from your viewers (though you can also deliberately use it to create twists!). Just as important is being consistent with this style.

A pet peeve I have is when a show or comic will have a lineup of characters, 90% of which are consistent with one another, but then have one design that will completely throw off the aesthetic of the show.

![image](./images/unnamed_1458.jpg)

[A Silent Voice](<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/A_Silent_Voice_(film)&sa=D&source=editors&ust=1759597864977746&usg=AOvVaw1ohg0UDwc-zuWFgCFXFLpj>) (2016)

I don\'t have a problem with designs being broad and having more variety in lineups is something I am strongly in favor of. However, if you\'re going to make zanier design choices, you need to sprinkle that throughout your world, rather than having just one character following a different ruleset.

In the above screenshot, Tomohiro the larger character has a head that is disproportionately large and cartoonish compared to the rest of the cast. I think it\'s great that they included a character with a larger body type, but it feels like he is from a different movie. This dissonance kept pulling me out of this fantastic movie, which is a shame. I think they could have pulled his facial design more in line with the rest of the cast to avoid this problem. Also - go and see A Silent Voice if you get the chance, it\'s a wonderful film!

There are times when you might want to break this ruleset deliberately - for example, if you are deliberately making the statement that this character is not of this world, and doesn\'t belong. However in general you should try to keep your style consistent in order to keep your world feeling believable.

![image](./images/unnamed_1503.png)

> When experimenting with the various possible design elements that make up your character, think about what _associated imagery_ you can use:

![image](./images/unnamed_446.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_761.png)

- ðŸ‘‰ Advice from some experts:
- [Advice 1][kYqEwbsrkn]
- [Advice 2][cfyNQqejjq]
- [Advice 3][BsWQgvsHXy]

### Function and Purpose

Design for Animation Needs:

A further consideration when designing characters is how they will function with the chosen animation style. A character animated with the puppet tool in After Effects will have very different animation needs to one designed for traditional frame-by-frame animation in TV Paint or Harmony.

Think about which character animation style will work for your chosen aesthetic and carefully research what type of world your story is set in. A cartoony, squash-and-stretch animation style may work great for a quirky cast of characters.

![image](./images/unnamed_110.png)

While a more rigid or realistic structure & anatomy will work better for stories holding a bit more gravity, since they're grounded in a less-cartoony, more realistic world.

![image](./images/unnamed_488.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_657.png)

> ðŸ‘‰ Conceptualizing Intent: [Jack Burke's Process][NDsCMyuOtQ]

The word 'shape' appears more than 90 times in this document, you will see that it's the most basic element to have and use in all

character design creation.

![image](./images/unnamed_774.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_884.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_432.png)

Designs can range from the **very simple...**

![image](./images/unnamed_754.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_414.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_656.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_537.png)

To the **very complex...**

![image](./images/unnamed_129.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_772.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_362.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1583.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1477.png)

There's a wide spectrum between simple and complex, characters can vary
from **_realism_** to **_caricature_** and excessive to minimalism - inhabiting many
degrees of diversification and stylization.

![image](./images/unnamed_1130.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_484.png)

The character designs you create will influence how the animation **style of movement** will develop.

This includes the characters' flexibility, mobility, and type of hair & clothing.

There can be a predetermined set of rules and parameters in the style of animation that may influence how you design the characters as well. For example: you may be on a production that won't be able to accommodate for drapery and long flowing capes, cloaks and hair, for either a 2D or 3D production, because these technical limitations could be beyond the scope of the software (or budget), and this would restrict you to creating more tight-fitting clothes and maybe LEGO-style stiff/blocky hair.

Most of the time designers need to research and ask lots of questions and perhaps even see the shooting-style of the storyboards in order to ascertain what limitations there may be in the characters for their restrictions in movements, clothing, hairstyles and range of expressive attitudes. Sometimes there's technical limitations with what the characters can do; Perhaps they must have form-fitting clothes and short hair because it would become too complex to animate long-flowing hair and loose-baggy clothing.

> ðŸ‘‰ The dangers of over-designing your characters are well explained [here][ljdybaRaLN].

#### Simplifying vs Complexity

![image](./images/unnamed_104.jpg)

#### Iconography in Character Design

Digging a bit deeper on the use of images and symbols to represent ideas and how there's a range within the spectrum of all art and illustration when it pertains to realism, stylization, and visual symbolism. Adding symbols, icons, or signs to your character design can add another layer of depth and mystique. This could be giving your character an object that is strongly associated with them, adding [symbolism][iCYTczMpyS] in the character\'s clothing, or including a unique scar or tattoo.

From the _Arcane_ series, see here how they implemented basic symbolism into Jinx and Vi\'s character design:

![image](./images/unnamed_364.png)

These symbols are used as metaphors - imagery that represents their character, visual metaphor is a common tool in film to help convey a story\'s deeper meaning. They can help establish themes, character arcs, and communicate important ideas. They can also help audiences relate to the story and grasp complex ideas more intuitively.

> See this excerpt from Scott McCloud's wonderful book: [Understanding Comics][daTvVvacwd]

[Visual Motifs][FtByyfAbtx] can be depicted in your character design's outfits, or the props they hold, or within the environment they live in. All this can play a part in informing the audience as to their personality and history. Visual development artist Sam Nielson created a reference sheet for these types of representation and their meanings:

![image](./images/unnamed_078.gif)

Downloadable large version [here][HhSTidwRPN].

#### Design for Technical Needs

If the production is using cut-out/puppet-style animation, you'll need to design each part of your character with a mindset in how it will connect and rotate with other body parts. [2D rigging][HOJLeQfbbp], with bone tools and deformers, requires lots of forethought on the interactivity of the body parts and often a trial-and-error process to determine how each body part is connected and what pivot shapes will work best for creating smooth or believable movement, while keeping the rig [functional][VXuXsYxqKr] and easy-to-work with for the animators that pose and operate the characters.

![image](./images/unnamed_011.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_006.gif)

#### Knowing the Limitations

Being aware of the production's parameters and any specific restrictions involved is important knowledge to have and use in your quest to determine the style. Oftentimes, because the design process can start very early in the production pipeline, there are no clearly defined lists of conditions to be conscious of. At some point, your thought process should be mindful of any constraints in the artistic and technical aspects for the characters' anatomy and physical capabilities. Budgetary concerns and even what software/technology is being used can also be factors to consider.

![image](./images/unnamed_1244.jpg)

For example - the series [Pickle &
Peanut][hRYsVMGjQB] of the characters in Toonboom Harmony could be made practical and functional for animators. The stories and circumstances the characters would find themselves in required them to manipulate, bend, deform, and contort their bodies to meet the needs of the animation style.

A hybrid animation approach was taken for some aspects of the animation (a combination of traditional animation with rigged animation), since their faces and limbs were so flexible and unique in their poses and expressiveness from scene to scene. Therefore a unique production pipeline had to be made: Where the character rigs were customized from scene to scene to meet the demands of the exaggerated and dynamic posing the characters had to do. This way, the models the artists would animate could perform the actions depicted in the storyboards. Very rough storyboards were made and delivered to a team of _character layout artists_, who would use those storyboards as reference and hand-draw/clean-up and add details to the extensive posing/acting of every shot, placing the characters on-model on as they go. Then another team would take these drawn out character [layouts][fYnQIqqPXV] and make customized rigs using the character builds and breaking them, to make the poses match the artwork scene by scene. Then a team of animators would animate the movements from pose to pose using these custom rigs.

If you're designing with no finished scripts in hand, you may only have some rough ideas as to what sorts of stories these characters will find themselves in, this is also common. Getting just a hint of what the characters will need to perform in their scenes by analyzing scripts from the episodic stories will help in the refinement of your designs. Knowing if it's a fast-paced action/adventure series is very different from knowing it's a slow-paced lots-of-talking style series. On feature films the story is constantly evolving through to the very end of production. You simply adapt with the changes from the very beginning as things are developing right until the end of the main production phase as you assist and change designs according to the needs of the production.

The term **function over form** comes from the architectural principle "form follows function," which means that the shape of the building should primarily relate to its intended function. Make-up Design, Set Design and Costume Design all have the same principle. In interior design, _function over form_ means prioritizing how you will use the room or piece of furniture over how it looks. In character design, the same term can be identified when trying to reach a balance between the artistic needs and requirements of your design, versus its practical use for the purpose of the story being told. "Form" is the look of your design, how good, and how cool, and how appealing it looks. "Function" is how practical and usable it is for animation production.

When developing the look and structure of your characters, you must ask yourself: why are you creating this character, what environment are they from or live in, what are the stories and scenarios they will appear in, and who are you creating these characters for; the target audience is something to consider when designing your characters.

![image](./images/unnamed_1229.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1569.png)

> In fine dining, every _second_ counts.
> In animation, every _frame_ counts.
> In character design, every _line_ counts.
>
> - Every line must have a purpose.

Designing how characters will **move** can often be overlooked. The manner in which they move and the mechanics of how they move, how much stretch-ability and flexibility they may have, are all factors to consider. Especially once the demands of the production kick in. Getting to explore and design how movements communicate and enhance character in any Pre _Production Test Animation_ stages can often spotlight some changes in your designs that need to be made. Never forget: your work will be taken by many other artists and they will animate your designs!

_A famous story in animation:_

Bill Melendez, the director of the 1965 TV Special "A Charlie Brown Christmas", had to explain to broadcast executives that the characters in a _Peanuts_ cartoon simply could not hold items 'above their heads' - No matter how much the producers insisted upon it, this wasn't physically possible since their arms were so short and their heads were so big. So _cheats_ and creative solutions always had to be made when staging the action.

![image](./images/unnamed_295.png)

Modern animated series like _Green Eggs & Ham_, _Animaniacs_, and _Central Park_ are just some of many that had traditional model sheets drafted for animators to use as reference, there were NO rigs or Builds created at all for the characters even though they were entirely digitally-produced. Classical frame-by-frame animation methods were used to create every scene in every episode, and it was animated using _Harmony_ software. They were made to look as if drawn traditionally on paper. Tablets and Cintiqs were used to essentially animate in classical methods with entirely digital paperless-animation tools, with very specific custom brushes built for the clean-up animation process for the look of the _final line_.

![image](./images/unnamed_726.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1482.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_829.png)

The more traditionally-animated the characters are, the more beneficial it is to keep the characters simpler in design, this reduces the amount of work for animators must do to make them move. The less lines and textures to animate, the easier it will be to have the characters perform complex actions and acting.

![image](./images/unnamed_010.gif)

Animation By Tim Larade

![image](./images/unnamed_077.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_032.gif)

Animation by Mihka MÃ¤ntynen

As a character designer you may be asked to create the full cast of main characters for a story. You will eventually need to assemble your designs into a model pack. At first you may only be assisting a lead designer, but if you are in charge of multiple characters and to make an ensemble cast out of them, a model pack is inevitable:

**The Model Pack** refers to the character design sheets for a cast of characters from an animated film or series. A Model Pack attributes to just the characters. While a Main Design Pack (MDP) usually refers to all main characters and key locations/props, that sets the style and tone for the entire series or project.

A [full model pack][BiziSAcLoh] would have the main characters with full turnaround views and poses/expressions sheets + select incidental characters with some various views drawn out for them as well, all contained in a workbook / reference guide as shown [here][uBuTtqcBOU]. Some studios also refer to the term "Fun Packs" - these are usually reserved to being a bundle of bare-essential designs, like main characters and key locations/environments, for the [Storyboard Artist][FICGszhFPc] to use as reference.

In an animated series, Storyboard Artists must often create all sorts of new incidental/secondary and alternative designs that go beyond the initial design pack they were given to use as reference. Therefore, after a storyboard/animatic is done and approved, a _post-board design pass_ is done; A new comprehensive list is made, artists then complete all remaining on-screen design elements that will need to be animated later on, this builds up and finalizes a more complete design pack. Layout Artists and Build Artists/ Riggers use these new designs to complete all remaining assets needed for the animators to have in their scenes to animate with.

A consolidated design pack is made after the storyboards are done, and before [Rigging][HYFOHjdvwm], [Background Layout/Paint][SpVYNiuiQD] and Scene Planning has started.

![image](./images/unnamed_240.png)

In the beginning, the designs you develop and sketch and create are to find out what the characters would look like, and eventually they get [refined][hGFtgtQUwQ] and locked in, and decisions are made as to which design is best suited for the character.

Afterwards, these designs are made to be used as references for all the artists that come **after** you, to use in production. So keep in mind, the more visual information you can offer, the more helpful it will be to the crew that will bring your characters to life.

![image](./images/unnamed_1129.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_392.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_664.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1510.png)

In some cases, animation tests are made, and the outcome of those tests causes there to be revisions in the designs. Unforeseen limitations or obstacles are discovered in the testing process, leading to adjustments made to the designs and builds in order to make them more functional for animation purposes.

![image](./images/unnamed_087.gif)

If the story requires a character to perform an action that is physically impossible for them to do, then changes to their design may be needed in order for the performance to work, or 'cheats' are made in the poses and builds to accommodate the action to happen.

Cartoon Saloon Animation Tests:

![image](./images/unnamed_024.gif)

See this rigged animation test for _Loud House_, this was not only a way to see what the animation style would look like but also to test the limits of the design and how far poses could be squashed, stretched and exaggerated:

![image](./images/unnamed_057.gif)

### Thoughts on Appeal

Spend enough time talking to animators and you'll start to hear the word 'appeal' come up. In terms of animated characters, appeal is what makes an audience see the character as charismatic, relatable or realistic. It's what makes us feel for them, and remember them, even after the animation is over.

Advice from [Dean Heezen][xpAyYgKZjg]:

![image](./images/unnamed_314.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1198.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_574.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_502.jpg)

It's imperative to keep in mind the purpose of these characters and their stories, what is the intended audience of the animated work you are designing for, and how your character design will fit within the context of the production.

From simplistic, with a wide range of exaggeration:

![image](./images/unnamed_1018.png)

And simplistic, with a more limited range of expressions and exaggerations:

![image](./images/unnamed_884.png)

To more complex in details, with a wide range of exaggeration:

![image](./images/unnamed_1629.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1347.png)

And complex, with a limited range of exaggeration, with more realistic mechanics of movement:

![image](./images/unnamed_076.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_144.png)

Design sheets can offer a wide range of information for several departments in the production.

The art direction, your client's needs, and the requirements of the production will dictate how simplified your character style needs to be. You may want a more artistic look to help your characters stand out from the crowd - this may call for more of a 'broad' character design, or a stylized, abstract, or angular look, maybe a combination of realism and strong graphic qualities.

![image](./images/unnamed_538.png)

Breakdowns by Brooklyn Walker:

![image](./images/unnamed_556.png)

Advice from the Master, Sherm Cohen:

![image](./images/unnamed_143.png)

> ðŸ‘‰ Video Essay: [How Spongebob's Design Evolved Over The Years][FdskZfvZNJ]

When presenting a cast of characters, it's often a good idea to show the dynamics of the group.

For example, you could do a standard line-up like this:

![image](./images/unnamed_944.jpg)

Or show more personality, by posing characters like this:

![image](./images/unnamed_491.png)

Depicting better what the show could be in terms of tone and style.

Sometimes when creating characters for a client, they need them for their Pitch Decks and Require the final character designs to presented more like this and less like standard Model sheets where they are only standing up straight with no expressions.

Creating appeal not only comes from the way a character acts and moves, but the very [building blocks][sBpNtPLDgq] of how they are [visually constructed & designed][XgveNnGNrs]. Animated characters come in many different shapes and forms of complexity, from minimal/icon-style all the way up to near-realism.

> [Watch this excellent profile on Character Design Master, Torsten Shrank][XPVoCmNSBA]

![image](./images/unnamed_799.png)

Breaking down the structure and shapes of character designs & poses can be a great learning venture:

![image](./images/unnamed_072.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_067.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_027.gif)

See more of his stunning work [here][ejcGijJSZM].

> ðŸ‘‰ See [this breakdown][JXaBJydejk] for how the designs in _Primal_ can show off the characters' unique personalities and display a specific appeal for the graphic style of the series.

![image](./images/unnamed_1282.jpg)

by Stephen DeStefano

**Tilt**, **Rhythm**, & **Twist** - Here's tips to use when posing. Former Disney animator Glen Keane coined tilt, rhythm, and twist; if you have a background in studio art or art history, you'll be familiar with the term Contrapposto ("Counter Pose") Tilt and Twist are easy enough to explain: you can get tilt/twist in the head, shoulders, and hips, this will help the pose feel less rigid--you're essentially applying overlapping action to the pose, causing counter-weights or contrast in the postures.

![image](./images/unnamed_1255.png)

Rhythm is a slightly more complex concept. Rhythm is about the flow of your lines and how they lead the eye around the drawing. It's hard to describe in words, hopefully this graphic helps:

![image](./images/unnamed_1306.png)

**Simple vs. Complex** - Rhythm is also tied into the concept of simple vs. complex. You see simple vs. complex in the elements that make up a character, but also in the pose as a whole, and the textures within the character design and scene composition. These contrasts within the pose itself helps to create appeal.

![image](./images/unnamed_1723.png)

Part of achieving **Appeal** in character design is to apply these theories when you can, the root of this comes from the [Solid Drawing Principle][aJwwSupriS] in 2D animation.

- ðŸ‘‰ Watch Mollsie Art talk about the subject of appeal [here][HqbmENUGlx].
- ðŸ‘‰ Watch J. Marrons's tutorials in structural drawing and anatomy [here][eBqolRcLbU].

![image](./images/unnamed_445.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_228.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_196.png)

Avoiding Tangents in designs and posing is another factor that helps with appeal:

![image](./images/unnamed_948.png)

Simply overlap or separate shapes and lines, preventing them from merging at the edges.

![image](./images/unnamed_171.png)

You can fix tangents by shifting the line or pushing the line back.

The Annie Awards are the top annual prizes in animation. See the wide variety of styles that were picked unique features for their appeal and own unique features: [The Character Designs that were nominated for an Annie Award in 2022][lsfwPsvxgj]

![image](./images/unnamed_1452.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1321.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1402.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_868.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_827.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_918.png)

The Mask Method:

![image](./images/unnamed_1614.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1630.png)

Style guide for "Coco" (2017):

![image](./images/unnamed_657.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1128.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_094.jpg)

3D models also get draw-overs in order to communicate quickly how to make adjustments, boost up overall appeal, and to push the sharpness and improve the balance of the shapes, forms and contours that make up the character's body language and structure:

![image](./images/unnamed_669.png)

See how the accumulation of many small and subtle changes can add up an overall more appealing design.

![image](./images/unnamed_1578.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_353.png)

Creating appeal in your designs can be the most challenging aspect of the work. There's thousands of variables that all play off of each other. Only with time, countless hours of study & keen observations, with lots and lots of practice, can you build up your instincts to know how to recognize appealing designs and how to create them.

> ðŸ‘‰ Watch Brookes Eggleston talk about the subject [here][bkyazpUsYu].

By Shiyoon Kim

### Anatomy & Structure

The following section is about analyzing the inner-workings and construction of various cartoon character styles, how they are rooted in realistic forms and volumes found in figure-drawing, all from referencing real people and animals. Studying, analyzing, and practicing anatomy can be one of the most challenging and important aspects to learn on your journey to becoming a character designer.

![image](./images/unnamed_645.png)

- See [here][ZCvHmNuIle] for a guide on the very core basics that you need to master first by Marc Leone.
- See [here][LcDllexluX] for a guide on understanding cross contours for thinking more like a 3D modeler.
- See [here][YdhnBwbvUT] for a guide on how to study anatomy.
- See [here][RhZVEtQcFK] for a guide on developing the structure of character anatomy by Rad Sechrist.

![image](./images/unnamed_1423.png)

See [here][VwGXjTAyhC] for a guide on the advantages of using more _structural drawing_, from Tracy Butler. Her work is great to sift through, with plenty of tutorials.

![image](./images/unnamed_277.jpg)

#### How to Improve Your Character Form and Structure

One thing to practice as much as possible - Take screenshots from shows, videos, movies you watch and make draw-overs, analyze the structure, figure out what makes it work, break things down, create these studies for yourself to learn how they did it:

![image](./images/unnamed_1301.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_093.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_238.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1092.jpg)

A key part of creating strong character designs is having a strong understanding of form and anatomy. Here are some exercises and tutorials on how to improve these skills:

1. Do many many many anatomy drawing studies.
2. Take a life drawing class in person or online.
3. Practice drawing areas of the body that you find the most difficult - hands, feet, head angles, etc.
4. Visit a zoo or natural history museum and sketch the animals - keep it loose, fast, rough.
5. Go to a park and draw different plants and quick-sketches of people you see.
6. Do lots and lots of [gesture drawings][dKjsnclEDZ], practice the human form.
7. Practice your [proportions][ZEaUAWozAW].
8. Follow the methods from [experts][IUxNUFeHqy].

![image](./images/unnamed_1308.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1167.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_480.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_092.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_503.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_903.png)

Rough turnaround and construction guide by Jesse Bechtold:

![image](./images/unnamed_1437.png)

Everything has anatomy and structure, even these designs:

![image](./images/unnamed_555.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_769.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_811.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_007.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1096.jpg)

Design sheets are often made showing breakdowns and constructions to figure out the mechanics and movements of your character and their range of motion for animators to have as reference, but these are also made to maintain some rules for the solidity and structure of your characters and useful strategies on how to pose them.

![image](./images/unnamed_1282.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_615.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1175.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1400.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_719.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_925.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1576.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_097.png)

To maintain the volume consistency and structure of your character when posing them for their turnaround views and pose sheets, you must analyze and [practice the fundamentals][PaQgnDQNsR] of human (and animal) anatomy. This will give you knowledge on how to simplify and stylize them to accommodate the specific anatomy for the characters you're creating. By doing so, you'll learn how to customize the anatomy for your own characters, to make it fit in the style that is intended.

![image](./images/unnamed_782.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1469.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1001.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_826.png)

These breakdowns are rooted in all forms of structural anatomy and these theories can be adapted to most design styles, but every character has their own unique construction:

![image](./images/unnamed_703.png)

> Samples of breakdowns for the construction of a character design:

![image](./images/unnamed_429.jpg)
