#### Figure Drawing

Drawing quick gestures is a great method of practice as it is a significant exercise that can help you fully grasp how to draw a body.

The practice of gesture drawing involves making a quick and simplified sketch of your subject matter (typically a human body).

When [gesture drawing][smGwJICKdU], you are trying to plot down the main elements of the figure in quick and simple linework to capture the general feel of the body and the pose you are trying to communicate.

![image](./images/unnamed_505.png)

This is very helpful in allowing you to get a feel for the flow of the body and how to visually communicate body language, gestures, and poses, in a very simplified manner.

To do gesture drawing, all you really need is a pen and paper, or maybe a drawing tablet or an Apple Pencil and iPad, and some sort of reference.

![image](./images/unnamed_528.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1146.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1652.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1020.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1366.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_606.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_073.png)

This can simply be an online reference image, or if you wanted to try gesture drawing the traditional way, you could use a live model --- maybe even try to find a class to attend.

Then, all that's left to do is start [sketching][RwuRmPQTwe] the body as simply and quickly as you can, ignore specific details, and instead try to draw the flow and linework of the body.

> More about the benefits and techniques of Gesture Drawing [here][LHMRqGopnT].

![image](./images/unnamed_1499.png)

Remember to give yourself a time limit, ranging from 30 minutes to under 30 seconds.

As well as this, don't use an eraser! Just let your pencil flow as you try to capture the essence of the body.

This time limit and lack of eraser will force you to really try and quickly plot down the main elements of the human form, making you more easily grasp any important details and therefore providing you with a better understanding of how to draw a body.

![image](./images/unnamed_1344.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_318.png)

A lifetime can be spent learning, analyzing, practicing sketching the human body.

> Watch these tips and techniques from [Robert Marzullo][BixSotjvBT].

#### Human Anatomy

Every character you create will have their own unique style of anatomy, the key is to stay consistent within your own style, you will have different body types and body shapes for different characters, but as you create a cast of characters in the world/style you are creating. The more you'll see the range of proportions you will need to do, the more you'll refine the anatomy to match that character's physique and personality.

![image](./images/unnamed_047.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_517.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_041.png)

The body is difficult to capture through art as there are many components to consider. You must remember to engage the [fundamentals][tUAdNiDxIl] of art, specifically considering form, structure, and composition -- as well as considering proportions and figure anatomy, body movements, and stylization. This sort of approach to how your characters are constructed from the inside out, takes time to develop, like any illustration skills, there's several ways to mold and shape your characters, and every style has their own brand of [anatomy][vMtkBrqztu].

![image](./images/unnamed_1246.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_984.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_232.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_357.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_888.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1587.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_155.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_958.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1269.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_048.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1313.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1467.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1729.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_634.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1562.png)

There's a wide range of anatomy styles to pick from and to create yourself.

![image](./images/unnamed_541.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1086.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_890.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_643.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_009.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_266.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_597.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_235.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_608.png)

Hands, in general, can be the most diverse and complex things to pose and the most difficult to master.

There's many lessons [online][ytVOzkPKxN] to help you improve.

When you're doing more human anatomy based in realism, there are different factors to consider, compared to when you do more flat/graphic/cartoony styles.

![image](./images/unnamed_882.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1071.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1215.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_805.jpg)

Before you can begin adding personality and stylization to the bodies you draw, it is integral to understand the proportions of human anatomy. Getting the body proportionally correct can be difficult, but there are a few shape-work techniques you can use to sketch the human body as shown in the samples above and below. Practice drawing your [forms in poses][wvBeUDKyNK] that demand a challenging perspective.

![image](./images/unnamed_380.png)

Drawing a body is a difficult task, especially because there are so many different components to consider. Trying to create an [anatomically correct figure][HczDszGlDA] is not always easy. The body is integral to many aspects of design; you need it for any character you might want to create or even in environmental artworks with people in the background.

So studying and practicing how to draw a body is important. You want to feel comfortable creating stylized figures in whatever poses and designs you would like.

![image](./images/unnamed_1526.png)

#### Animals/Creatures

How to breakdown and learn from the references you save & compile...

Here's a post from the amazing, [HelpfulHarry][KPcKcvPwBK]:

> **So let\'s make Bad Art of a Deer because I happen to have one handy**.

Start with a photo of your subject in a nice/neutral pose with all four feet visible (so not like me).

![image](./images/unnamed_1132.png)

Freehand copy it. Try not to stylize, focusing instead of matching proportions and pose. Don\'t get too detailed!

![image](./images/unnamed_1465.png)

Either _lasso + distort_ or redraw a copy of your first sketch with your reference behind it (scaled to match the main body of your sketch).

Put the original and modified sketches together and compare the differences. Write it down if you want. This shows you where your eyes saw things the wrong size, so you can correct for that next time.

![image](./images/unnamed_699.png)

It\'s ok if your art looks terrible and has broken legs. I\'ve drawn LOTS of deer so I have a leg up. Everyone\'s art sucks in their own eyes and here\'s where mine went wrong: [Video][cbzhFGEWUm]

After learning about both deer and yourself, try freehand copying again.

![image](./images/unnamed_220.png)

Marvel at your newfound knowledge and skill!

But there\'s always room for improvement...

![image](./images/unnamed_1688.png)

You can stop here and move on to your real drawing, Or do another freehand-fix-compare cycle. I actually overcorrected my \"draws heads too big\" and veered into \"heads too small.\"

![image](./images/unnamed_424.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_402.png)

Another note on tracing: Learning HOW to trace is more important than anything you could learn by tracing. Draw the Anatomy, not the outline. In real life, things don\'t have outlines, they have bones.

![image](./images/unnamed_506.png)

These are from the same shoot which is extra useful for consistency. The lines are minimal and follow where the animal\'s joints are, and only important parts are drawn.

You won\'t know what Important Parts means right off the bat, which is where in-depth study comes in. You need to do this over and over, to learn the hard parts, to do the easy parts right.

##### How to study Bones and Muscles

- \"Study the anatomy"
- "Study the anatomy\"
- But they never tell you HOW.

It\'s not \"read a book,\" It\'s more like flailing around wildly and crashing your browser from too many tabs.

This is all about How to **make** a bones and muscle chart. Because even if your art sucks, you learn so much more by doing than by seeing, finding skeletal and muscle anatomy references online. Get set up. Get photos, like below, but it doesn\'t have to be the same photos. And now\... **gather and organize references.**

![image](./images/unnamed_181.png)

References I gathered:

- [Refrence 01][csrNLaIDoZ]
- [Refrence 02][OjkZaBdyRm]
- [Refrence 03][rwGYNKXeVR]
- [Refrence 04][WoUziRsVQs]
- [Refrence 05][qEnSQkbWSB]
- [Refrence 06][DVavSBySXW]
- [Refrence 07][TQaZBuVSTk]
- [Refrence 08][CWuGDfxaEB]

We\'ll start with bones. Search up \"\[animal\] skeleton\" and get photos or super scientific illustrations. Add in things like \"top view\" to spice it up.

Next, search \"\[animal\] skeleton sketchfab.\" This pulls up 3D models that you can rotate in your browser. Remember that these are art and the anatomy is only as good as the artist, so pick a good one.

![image](./images/unnamed_205.png)

Time for bone!

The spine is the most important, and in a lot of animals it will surprise you. Draw it in over your photo and then add spikes because skeletons are punk. These are not scientific and I didn\'t count them because their number doesn\'t matter to art. So you better be referencing scientists and not me!

![image](./images/unnamed_611.png)

The rest of the bones and some notes. These are my notes to _myself_ about things I want to remember. My personal discoveries in anatomy that made my art better. You can make the same notes but also make sure you have your own thoughts on there as well. That\'s how you help yourself the best. Be as detailed or vague as you want.

![image](./images/unnamed_991.png)

Same deal with muscle. Here are my personal notes to myself. Label stuff that is important to you. I actually grouped a bunch of muscles together based on what is visible from the outside. Muscles are way more complicated than this, but Baby\'s First Anatomy Chart gets to be simple.

![image](./images/unnamed_1158.png)

This is good enough for me because I have intimate knowledge of the other muscles working under and over these ones. Feel free to add as many or as few muscles as you like. You chart your own course.

This is very VERY much not an anatomical chart. I\'m sure there\'s nerds out there pulling their hair out looking at this. But listen, it works for art!

![image](./images/unnamed_1572.png)

And you know the wildest part about this?

I don\'t need to look at it to use it. The act of making your own anatomy chart puts that knowledge in your brain. Like how you can make \"cheat sheets\" even for tests that don\'t allow them - the act of making the sheet helps you remember what you struggle with most.

And after all that complexity? Your simplification will be based on Real Knowledge and you\'ll put those random circles in the right spots.

![image](./images/unnamed_1390.png)

Look at all this hard work you\'ve done. Eventually this will be second nature to you.

And you can apply this TO ALL HUMAN AND ANIMAL ANATOMY - your skill levels will advance VERY quickly the more you do these types of breakdowns and analysis.

#### Boost your Design IQ

Knowing the fundamentals, and only the fundamentals, very very well, gives you the solid foundation you need to approach any design style with confidence. Here's some exercises, some are very simple, others are more demanding. Giving yourself more and more [exercises][vLYcuAByVG] that repeat the fundamentals in new and challenging ways will improve your draftsmanship.

Explore the [Iterative Drawing Method][aupfRcwaXZ] to improve your draftsmanship.

There's many examples of people learning and improving through [repetition][XnSIzSdPin].

Some advice on your state-of-mind when it comes to producing art from [Greg Broadmore][JBGgZnEPAc].

##### Back to basics

The following exercise will improve your hand dexterity and ability to efficiently [draw the forms][kgOZJMpgIC] you need to construct anatomy by sketching circles, ellipses and other shapes while also getting you into the mental state of calmness needed to start work on a real drawing, here's a breakdown:

![Image of circles and ellipses](./images/unnamed_1431.png)

You should practice filling a page or digital canvas everyday with circles, it's great for motor skills and to get you hand to sketch these out fast and with confidence.

Avoid this:

![Macintosh HD:Users:ron:Downloads:circlesnoooo.png](./images/unnamed_1672.png)

It needs to look more like this:

![Macintosh HD:Users:ron:Downloads:fHowtodrawawesomecirclesIndustrialdesignsketching.png](./images/unnamed_804.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1466.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_963.jpg)

Practicing drawing [cylinders][kFZbdUnWWT] is a good way to warm up and is a surprisingly useful way to get perspective sketched out freehand.

![image](./images/unnamed_616.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1016.png)

Cylinders are quite versatile. You'll often end up in a situation where it is useful to be able to draw them quickly and accurately. For example, it is helpful to approximate arms and any limbs [foreshortening][hraKBfasyU] through cylinders when drawing the human form.

![image](./images/unnamed_558.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1113.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1540.png)

You can also deform cylinders into organic shapes.

![image](./images/unnamed_576.png)

It's just useful to be able to freehand cylinders! And it is a great drawing exercise when you have no reference to work from or no inspiration. Become better at drawing by doodling circles, ellipses, cylinders and cubes. All character designs can be constructed with these [forms][lFQTMnXkff].

![image](./images/unnamed_470.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_593.png)

##### TIME TO DRAW CUBES

![image](./images/unnamed_156.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_825.jpg)

More on the great benefits of practicing drawing boxes [here][hmFIxQJUZw] and [here][DMclFlyNbE].

![http://drawabox.com/images/lesson1/boxes_knowing.jpg](./images/unnamed_1420.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_745.jpg)

Practicing these fundamentals as warm-up exercises is a great way to get the motor skills running before you start any new design task. There's no better way than doing lots and lots of lines, circles, ellipses, cylinders, boxes and cubes, here's an expanded version:

- ðŸ‘‰ [Draw A Box Course][dtlpGIDmaU]
- ðŸ‘‰ [Improving your Skills with these Drawing Exercises][uYAAeuRjCr]

#### Analytical Sketching

![image](./images/unnamed_406.png)

Applying all the techniques shown above to analytic drawing will have you practicing constructs with 2D projections of 3D shapes to create the structure of your forms, allowing you to visually rough out any subject into its basic shapes and depict it with proper perspective from any angle.

- Examples from Andre Pijet: [(1)][htRfShTXoE] [(2)][MldBLVVsCc]
- Examples from Robert Lazslo: [(1)][HFClBYFezl] [(2)][vKmahqCXdi] [(3)][pZnowHiKEX] [(4)][MuZQqjEFsj]
- The Kim Jung Gi method of training and learning: [(1)][ZXeuGCiDPg] [(2)][AWHXufvNNB] [(3)][egFikBlBof]

### Stylization

This can be a very subjective thing to define. How much 'style' should you put into your design? What is 'style'? And where do you start on this broad spectrum?

> _"Often your limitations dictate a lot of your stylistic and creative choices, and I think that's OK, as long as you have an interesting concept to work from." - Drew Daniels, Cinematographer_.

This involves you finding your specific and individual style of drawing, and sticking to it consistently throughout your artwork. In a broader sense, the stylization process and the stylizing of character designs can encompass the countless ways of creating art, and everyone will find their own style through practice. It\'s all about how to make these drawings and designs feel like your own, and for each new production and concept you dive into, you find the style that best serves the character personalities, and the world and scenarios they will find themselves in.

![image](./images/unnamed_540.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_386.png)

How much style is too much style? Everyone has their own preference and opinion, and many factors are involved in the defining of **style**, and how far to push the stylization of something and how much detail to add or subtract. Many decisions are made on how much exaggeration should be injected or removed from the designs.

Here are designs by Ana Piedrafita for the Pocoyo series:

![image](./images/unnamed_022.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_642.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_603.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_822.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1178.jpg)

Simplicity and cuteness is obviously the goal for these designs.

![image](./images/unnamed_023.jpg)

Of course, ascetic minimalism itself holds little meaning. Context imbues significance. The process of simplifying designs and the art of reducing the amount of details often expresses more with less. Restrictions hone communication to indispensable signals, the easier and faster a designer is recognized, the more successful your character model will be. At the intersection of restraint and intent, very simple designs with basic shapes and forms uplifts truth and beauty: [Keep it Simple][WnLsqXrytX]

Examples of Bold Exaggerations:

- **Johnny Bravo** -- Crazy hairdo, bulging pecs, and arrogance exaggerated for laughs
- **Adventure Time** -- Friends frolicking through surreal candy-filled worlds
- **Samurai Jack** -- Jagged lightning style pushed into epic presentation
- **Borderlands** -- Dark humor and visual edge match its absurdity

Johnny Bravo turnaround model:

![image](./images/unnamed_1613.png)

Tiny Titans comic book:

![image](./images/unnamed_290.png)

Pioneers of low-poly / pixel art / 8-bit styles:

- **Minecraft** -- LEGO-like blocks empower creative construction
- **Re-Volt** -- Toy RC cars gain tactile charm
- **Voxatron** -- Voxel retro effects evoke gaming's dawn
- **Habbo Hotel** -- Minimized avatars enable diverse expression
- **Thomas Was Alone** -- Geometric shapes brimming with personality
- **Super Mario / Megaman / Link from Legend of Zelda** -- Nintendo mascots

![image](./images/unnamed_244.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_828.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1462.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_581.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1341.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_904.png)

Simple geometric models tap a nostalgia vein through their blocky charm. Low poly art employs hard edges and elemental shapes rather than pursuing endless detail. Less complexity focuses attention on possibilities over realities.

Abstract shapes leave space for imagination to fill the gaps. Our minds complete the visions through personalized perceptions. Low poly models guide eyes through implied motions along crisp vector paths. By finding virtue in technological limits and distortion, low poly art spotlights stirring essence below surface elaboration.

![image](./images/unnamed_913.png)

All styles contain strengths to amplify their personalities and stories. Photorealism conveys humanity, cartooning enables humor, and low poly maximizes minimalism. Wise character creators mix and match sensibly by considering impacts on emotion, branding, and performance. Most importantly, they pour passion into every line and pixel. That personal touch makes all the difference.

![image](./images/unnamed_1439.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_781.jpg)

Highly stylized art similarly cranks up reality through visual hyperbole. Every element gets exaggerated just shy of breaking believability, amplifying the stories they're into operatic spectacles. Super-exaggerated anatomy heightens the humor. By escaping the shackles of realism, artists can bend the medium to meet the vision. Costumes morph into Ã¼ber-symbolic power fantasies. Weapons swell to outrageous proportions mirroring their warrior's outsized strength. Highly distorted bodies become human ideograms. Backdrops warp surreally to externalize psychological subtexts. Realism depicts what is; stylization reveals what could be.

But the most effective exaggeration remains anchored to humanity. [Caricatures][uIMsHQvRkM] work best when we perceive the real person peering through the warped/simplified/exaggerated version you see. Unique character art style choices should align with and amplify authentic traits rather than pile on arbitrarily. Like a bungee cord, the most elastic visions snap back to their origins.

![image](./images/unnamed_1699.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_966.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1259.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_335.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_280.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_732.jpg)

Preferences in stylization and appeal can be a subjective topic of discussion, it's all about how much to simplify, or not simplify, how representative to real-life people, animals, and objects it needs to be, and how far can the exaggeration be pushed.

![image](./images/unnamed_245.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_854.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_560.jpg)

- ðŸ‘‰ [Tomm Moore: Style is Defined by my Limitations][YwcnOraNts]
- ðŸ‘‰ [How to Create Your Own Aesthetic][JDStiKxjbd]

A term you might hear a director or client say: "_Push the style_".

It may seem like an arbitrary statement, but it's usually referring to revising/improving the design in a way that further exaggerates the aspects and elements that give the artwork its unique appearance. If the eyes are proportionally quite big, make them bigger. If the hands are small, make them smaller. If the character is quite square and blocky, make them more so. If the characters seem very flat and rigid, make them more so. If they are very squashy and gooey, then make them more so. If the characters have long skinny limbs, make them more so.

![image](./images/unnamed_077.jpg)

All these are just samples of various looks you can find and collect - showcasing the incredibly vast possibilities of the graphic styles to be inspired from.

![image](./images/unnamed_627.png)

Pushing styles is about finding out how far is too far - if the eyes are small, make them extra small. If they have big hair, make them extra big. If they have long skinny limbs, snake them extra long. If they have tiny hands, make them extra tiny. If they have thick feet, make them extra thick.

Pushing style levels is always fun, as you can see from these examples, shapes and proportions and scale are the main factors that can have anyone experiment with how to stylize your designs more or less, it's boundless the range you can explore.

![image](./images/unnamed_277.png)

Discovering what the limitations are within the particular designs you are creating is part of the process. Developing the structure, design logic, and anatomy of your character's style can be a journey in itself:

![image](./images/unnamed_1625.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_338.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1111.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1085.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_575.png)

> Remember, all cartooning is to some degree, essentially [caricaturing][BweBsJncHp]

![image](./images/unnamed_1268.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_917.jpg)

> Here's good advice from KeshArt: [How To Draw Cartoon People][IWgHvErodp]
> Here's good advice from Hossein Afzali: [How to Cartoonize People][VjBHptsrcf]

There can be a vast spectrum to consider when finding the style you're looking for. Most times, people don't know what style they want to see, or create, until they've seen it. But usually offering a range of realistic to cartoony, covers a spectrum of possibilities to begin narrowing down the **style** a client (or you) may be looking for.

![image](./images/unnamed_1154.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_855.png)

One method you can use to find your style is to test out different ways of drawing a body until you find something that works for you, adapting it until it becomes your own. For methods and inspiration for drawing stylized anatomy specifically, see [this][pZCPieqmDS] and [this][rarLWMRsDF].

![image](./images/unnamed_562.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1049.png)\
![image](./images/unnamed_707.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1682.png)

Research can be gathered from all sorts of different places, here's an eye make-up guide a designer to help create the eyeshapes she needed for her characters:

![image](./images/unnamed_1439.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_159.png)

ðŸ‘‰ For more on hair, see these tutorials:

- [Tutorial 1][IDIMXVTCqx]
- [Tutorial 2][ivaNrdMgnb]
- [Tutorial 3][ktYDwEvJFW]
- [Tutorial 4][PIBwDKHBkY]

Experiment with linework; try different sketching styles, like using harsh confident linework or smooth-flowing lines.

Because styles are so limitless, there are so many methods you can use to learn how to stylize your body.

![image](./images/unnamed_1639.png)

Staying consistent within your own style can take a long time to figure out and accomplish, the secret to fostering and developing this with yourself is through lots and lots and lots of drawing, it's the only way to discover how YOU can draw your own thing, in your own way, and explore and fine-tune how YOU would handle any type of character. Observe the works of [Mike Mignola][JFnSXjfBzD], part of the appeal of his art style in how consistent he is within his own style.

![image](./images/unnamed_1379.png)

Remember that the term for "style" in character design for animation can refer to a few different things:

It can refer to a specific **person** who originated a certain look, like [Matt Greoning][vKaPXwXpzi] (seen in shows like Simpsons, Futurama, and Disenchantment) or [Norman Rockwell][xanshgjOrg] (seen in American commercial illustrations from the 1940s and 50s).

It can refer to a **property**, like [Scott Pilgrim][MPzGwmnMqr] (created by Bryan Lee O\'Malley) or [Adventure Time][hafRZFGJNX] (initially created by Pendleton Ward, then many others refined, expanded and continued the style).

It can refer to a **time period**, like the [1930s][uzpgCDjOLC] (as emulated in the animated series and video game Cuphead, which was derived from the very early works of Disney/Warner Bros/Fleischer).

It can refer to a **studio**, like [Aardman][XsrIRGJlXT] (as seen in the Wallace and Gromit films), or [UPA Studios][KIqMDArfrH] cartoons from the 1950s, or [Studio Ghibli][TVJhAmManR] (as seen in many works of Hayao Miyazaki), or both a studio and a certain time period, like [Hanna-Barbera][mGwVidcber] (as seen in many 1960s and 1970s animated series).

With many other combinations of these categories as well.

![image](./images/unnamed_341.png)

For studios in particular - it's referred to as '_the house style_' when a specific studio has a very particular and consistent look to all their designs for all their productions. _Old Disney_, compared to _Modern Disney_, same as _Klasky Csupo_, _Pixar_, _Don Bluth_, _Filmation_, _Nelvana_, _Aardman_ or _Ghibli_, they all have their own distinct, consistent, and long-running visual styles (especially in their early years) that they are most known for. Other more modern animation companies like _Studio Cachette_, _The Line_, _Madhouse_, _Laika_, and _Screen Novelties_ all have fairly unique and distinct house styles seen in most of their work. Though the art direction may vary from production to production, they seem to have a signature "look" to all the shows and films they've made.

Study various graphic styles from art history and architecture too, you never know when you'll need to borrow ideas from [Neo Expressionism][DZlmgDzkGk], [Brutalism][oOgfYLZThj], [Art Deco][cfUIeKVHrV], or [Cubism][KlgVxGEfBN].

There can be lots to learn from the old comic book ways too, including the criss-crossing spectrum between Realism vs Exaggeration and Low Detail vs High Detail.

- See [here][RxZZxTLIVN] for some insight on these various styles and methods.
- See [here][FadghXnqvX] for Scott MacCloud's breakdown from the world of sequential art.

There's hundreds of different combinations of styles to draw inspiration from.

![image](./images/unnamed_219.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_288.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1117.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_500.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1256.jpg)

There's over 100 years of character design history to become familiar with. Every few years we see trends change, tastes change, and new talent gets injected into the industry with new and fresh ideas. Often old styles tend to resurface and get re-imagined, and as new designers discover and rediscover old art styles, they often get remixed into the new modern styles we see today.

![image](./images/unnamed_247.png)

For example, looking back at the designs from the **80s** and **90s**, the characters are more realistic and in a way, have many similar looks and traits, comparable to the comic books of that era. But going into the **2000s** and **2010s**, many shows began to have a wider range of shapes and silhouettes for character design styles, borrowing aspects from the **50s** and **60s**:

![80s-90s kids be like "all CN shows look the same these days" :CartoonNetwork](./images/unnamed_343.jpg)

Trends, visual preferences, and styles seem to change and evolve every few years, then old trends circle back around and become popular again.

![image](./images/unnamed_592.jpg)

A **stylized character**, in general, is a character that has its proportions deformed and exaggerated in some ways to create a magnified character - made out to have a softer/cuter look, or just a super-simplified version of what would be a realistic character as well. Other ways to "push the style" is to overemphasize certain physical attributes, if the character is blocky and angular - make them more so, if the character is bubbly and cute make them more so. There are many ways to approach how you want your character to look.

![image](./images/unnamed_459.png)

Robert Valley

![image](./images/unnamed_599.png)

Sylvain Chomet

You can try creating a Chibi version (a term derived from Japan), or a stylized character similar to those seen in contemporary animated movies.

![image](./images/unnamed_1370.png)

The main distinction between them lies in the level of deformation these characters undergo. Chibis, originating from already stylized characters in manga or anime, exhibit significant deformations.

Currently, these types of characters find use in several applications, primarily in animated series or video games. Their visually appealing aesthetics easily capture the attention of younger audiences, but their charming appearance also attracts adults.

![image](./images/unnamed_646.png)

Typical Chibi Style:

![image](./images/unnamed_1516.png)

However, there are numerous ways to create highly-stylized characters, making them one of the most challenging aspects to achieve, in both 2D and 3D/CGI. They can also be used ironically for a [cute][oNyTkTqYuK] or humorous effect.

â€‹â€‹![image](./images/unnamed_1044.png)

When it comes to proportions, stylized characters share common characteristics - the process can be divided into three parts: head, body, face. Depending on the proportions you choose, you can achieve a more realistic impression similar to Fortnite or a more cartoonish character.

- **Head:** the bigger the better. The head, along with the face, is the most crucial aspect of these models. Not only do you need to enlarge the head, but you should also make it rounder to give the character a softer appearance. If there are special features on the head that are characteristic of the character, such as scars or wrinkles, include them, but exaggerate their appearance.
- **Face:** the face, together with the head, is one of the most important parts. The face should be proportionate with the head, so maintain balance in their proportions. Pay special attention to the eyes, making them rounder and larger than the other facial features. Even if you are aiming for a more realistic proportions, consider enlarging the eyes slightly to enhance expressiveness.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_622.png)
- **Body:** in contrast to the head, the body tends to lose its shape and become smaller, often as small as two and a half heads. Additionally, the body tends to lose its landmarks on the muscles. To create the desired shape, you will need to exaggerate the basic forms of the body. For instance, you can use a triangular shape to emphasize a muscular body or a rectangular shape to create an slender body.

â€Big head and big eyes - Body proportions of a Chibi or 'very cartoony' character:

![image](./images/unnamed_259.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_885.png)

Compare the 2003 version with the 2013 redesign of Teen Titans:

![image](./images/unnamed_395.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_149.jpg)

As a general rule; try to use a small color palette, ideally with no more than five main colors. This will help you focus on your artistic goals.

Mastering stylized art requires a thoughtful blend of proportion and a limited yet impactful color scheme for charming character design. With these small tips, hopefully, you can start designing your own characters.

![image](./images/unnamed_644.png)

Always remember, the devil lies in the details.

As you continue to practice and refine your skills, you\'ll discover your unique style and approach to character design.

Don't forget, every artist has their own journey, and every character you create is a step on your path of artistic discovery and growth.

### What is Art Direction?

It is the LOOK of your production. Design, Color, Lighting, Shapes, Textures - many elements are involved. Art direction can be a broad term depending on which industry and what types of productions you're referring to, but this usually pertains to the design **style** of the [backgrounds][LDOpQgisiz] (locations / sets / environments / scenery) for an animated production. However, in a broader sense, it also includes the [character][jtjcTVMHPE] art style as well, which also consists of clothing, props & FX, and how well they all work together - for the overall look and visual aesthetics of the entire production. As a result, lots of research and development is made to find the right look.

![image](./images/unnamed_1097.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1166.jpg)

These are important factors to know since character design does affect other aspects of the design process on a production. Usually many levels of communication between various artists is needed for creating all aspects of the characters, props, and backgrounds. Keeping everything consistent while meeting the production's demands can be challenging. Be aware that as a character designer, you may have a _Design Supervisor_ or _Character Supervisor_ or _Art Director_ as your manager/department lead.

![image](./images/unnamed_1513.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_249.png)

By Alan Stewart

The title of **Art Director** and **Production Designer** are often interchangeable. Sometimes they can be a solitary artist working on concept designs and visual development alone, or they can be the lead on a team of designers and held responsible for keeping consistency and cohesiveness through the many departments and stages of the design process.

The duties may vary from production to production and studio to studio, but Art Directors are usually responsible for all aspects of the visual style of the animated film or series. On big productions, there can be an art director for each department - bringing to life the production designer's vision and style. They decide how the characters, props and environments are going to look and provide a basis for the rest of the design department to work from.

- **Art Directors on a 3DCG animated film**
- **Overall Vision:** The Art Director sets the overall style and aesthetic of the film, ensuring it aligns with the director\'s vision and story.
- **Collaboration:** They work closely with the director, production designer, and other artists to guide the creative process.
- **Creative Leadership:** They provide direction, feedback, and guidance to the art team, ensuring the film\'s visual elements are cohesive and impactful.

For example, you can have cartoony-looking characters within hyper-realistic environments like [here][PLiKmnPBJi] and [here][vwRbhPDAVf], or semi-realistic characters with highly-stylized or abstract backgrounds like [here][rELFjBeSBj]. The art direction of any animated production can be a very wide spectrum of visual possibilities.

> ðŸ‘‰ [Pixar Art Director On How To Bring Characters To Life][WOcCwGIhdC]

Art directors start by examining the script or story premise and working with the director and any lead production designers to understand the vision for the film or series. Then they create concept designs and determine the tone, mood, and color palettes of the story.

![image](./images/unnamed_930.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1103.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_356.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1144.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1426.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1354.jpg)

\@AkiiRaii

- **Responsibilities of an Art Directors on a 2D animated series**
- **Define the visual style:** They determine the look of characters, props, and environments
- **Create designs:** They create designs for backgrounds, lighting, and visual effects
- **Establish the tone and mood:** They determine the color palettes and mood of the production
- **Work with the director:** They work with the director to understand the vision for the production
- **Work with concept artists:** They work with concept artists, background designers & character designers to develop sketches and artwork to find the style of the production.

![image](./images/unnamed_221.png)

By Alex Kirwan

In animated feature films - Art directors can also be known as CG Supervisors, VFX Supervisors, and Production Designers.

Concept art is the earliest exploration for finding the look of the production and to create visual ideas for everyone to see and comment on and this work is often used as part of a pitch to sell the project, film or series, before production is fully underway. The work of an art director is more specific and directive than the work of a concept artist or visual development artist.

![image](./images/unnamed_901.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_322.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_601.jpg)

Concept artists are usually freelancers, they come in, bang out a few sketches or color concepts and out they go, and they are the first to draw the exploration sketches for the production. Sometimes when a concept artist's work becomes the style that the production is targeting to be what they are going for (whether it be for environments or characters) then they stick around for longer. Since their art is the template that all other designs will be based off of. Usually a few different concept artists are brought in, so they can do a scatter shot of possible visual styles for the creative leads to use as reference. This way they can see which artistic direction they would rather go with. Their artwork is then used as a "look target" for background and character designers further down the line.

In these very early stages, they produce artwork and collaborate with the studio and directors to find a visual style that works. It's important that they communicate well with the client (creator/producer) to understand what is needed. They will often have to go back and redo their designs several times before they find something the studio/client is happy with. Sometimes the first passes are quick and loose for faster results and feedback. Other times, they add more detail and quality. They use computer software as well as hand-drawing and painting to create their art.

Sometimes _concept boards_ or reference boards are made, where lots of images are gathered as an initial inspiration pack by the visual development artist to see if they're going in the proper direction. Before committing to sketches, these style boards can cover color palettes, costuming, and hairstyles of all sorts. They can also be made by the art director or creative producers to give to the concept artists and designers, to guide them in the general direction they need to go.

![image](./images/unnamed_487.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_140.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_186.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_639.png)

These can also be called mood boards, image boards or aesthetic boards; when it's a big job, they are the best place to start before you begin working on your characters/story. It'll be where you lay out the inspiration for your character as a whole, from their personality and traits to what they wear and how they look. Think of it like a collage of ideas for who your character is. It's a chance for you to brainstorm and gather your thoughts before diving into the writing or drawing part of your project.

![image](./images/unnamed_467.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1517.png)

Art directors determine the '_design language_' of the film, which requires them to have an understanding of what the final, on-screen image will look like and how to get there. They work with the concept artists to develop sketches and artwork that communicate the artistic vision to the rest of the crew. In big-budget feature films, the Production Designer tends to be the one to give the consistent and singular LOOK for the visual style of the world, and the team of Art Directors supervise the many departments at every stage, to execute that vision into realization.

![image](./images/unnamed_740.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_453.png)

By Nikolas Illic

Associated with Art Direction is the process of [Look Development][mltUKiSBHJ] - it's the stage in most 3D/CG animated productions that builds the world and characters by creating the colors, textures, and materials for everything you see in the final rendered images. Look Development also explores how to actually achieve the intended visual look, pursuing the technical side of taking the concept art and discovering the process needed to practically attain and achieve the **look** for the actual production.

In many 3D/CG feature films, Art Directors are involved throughout the entire project, up to its release, acting as a supervisor to the other artists, such as modelers, character designers and environment designers. This is a job that involves a lot of communication with people and needs strong management skills. They are responsible for ensuring all artwork is of a high quality and in keeping with the director's vision. They are also responsible for making sure everyone in the art department stays on budget and on schedule. Art directors are sometimes employed as freelancers working on a project by project basis. Art Direction usually splits into two main categories - **Environments** (also known as: Sets / Backgrounds / Locations) and **Characters**.

#### Environment Art Direction

Set Design Concepts for _Hotel Transylvania_:

![image](./images/unnamed_926.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1275.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1315.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_676.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_486.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_705.jpg)

#### Character Art Direction

Design Language Guide for Spies in Disguise:

![image](./images/unnamed_1485.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1054.png)

Design Language Guide for Nimona:

![image](./images/unnamed_1206.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_983.png)

For animated feature films, hundreds upon hundreds of sketches & color concepts are drawn and painted in order to explore and later refine the final look of the characters and settings found in the movie.

In a 2D animated series - Character Design is a portion of the **_Production Design_** phase of animation (which can also include Environment & Background Design, FX Design and Prop Design), which is all part of the **_Pre-Production_** process, where scripts, voices, visual development, and story art are also produced.

![image](./images/unnamed_175.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_049.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_271.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_770.jpg)

> **Study Hans Bacher's book for more insight on this process:** \
> \
> ðŸ‘‰ [Dream Worlds - Production Design for Animation][ZdZUhoDajD]

Art Direction covers all aspects of design and color, see Brtiney Liu's work on characters, props and locations for this Robin's Ghost concept:![image](./images/unnamed.jpg)

![image](./images/unnamed_079.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1398.png)

When creating these characters and the world they reside in, you include exploration designs and color keys:

To observe the differences in **Location Designs** versus **Character Designs** for animated films, see the works of Sylvain Marc as he presents his concept designs for locations on [Hotel Transylvania 2][lTsGUxUHTI], and then his concept designs for characters on [Boxtrolls][uKOoVNamTb].

It's not too often the same artist can contribute to both these areas of art direction in such a major way, usually production designers specialize in either Characters or Locations, not often both.

More info about all this in great detail here:

> ðŸ‘‰ [Overview on the Production Design & Art Direction Process][xllIjPfrvM]

### Prop Design

A quick note about prop designs - Most of the time prop designs are made by a separate artist from the design department, there are times where a character designer may be asked to help design props as well. Items like any hand-held objects or vehicles and any item the characters interact with, that are separate from the background or environment are known as 'props'. It's important to cover prop design here since they are unavoidably linked to character design.

![image](./images/unnamed_1294.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1491.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_458.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_984.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_242.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_305.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1369.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_362.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1227.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1161.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_969.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_042.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1125.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_748.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_137.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_309.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_053.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1274.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_974.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1389.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_366.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_416.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_681.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1083.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_498.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_905.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_479.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_056.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1637.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_811.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_368.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_336.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1585.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1602.png)

Just like characters, props need to be designed from various views and angles:

![image](./images/unnamed_037.jpg)

By Alexis May Lambert

For Mobile Games, Prop Designers and BG Designers often share the same tasks as props and various items found in the environment become part of the backgrounds, whether they have animated aspects to them or not:

![image](./images/unnamed_081.png)

It's common to extract elements from background designs and construct these as their own self-contained item with turnaround views as well, depending on the art director, sometimes props are designed out of the background concepts and sometimes background artists borrow from the prop designs first to incorporate the item in the scenery. Sometimes both background designs and prop designs use the storyboards for reference, other times the designs need to be worked out in detail first, so that the storyboard artists use them as reference and then it gets carried through to final design in the animation scene setup process.

![image](./images/unnamed_589.png)

Prop designers have very similar skill sets to character designers and background designers, usually with a strong sense of [perspective drawing][ZoiCCFukxu] and structural drawing, with the ability to sketch complex objects in various three-dimensional views and angles.

- ðŸ‘‰ See [these samples][DeaMAMBYba] by Brent Noll
- ðŸ‘‰ See [these samples][lhZgZNBNDj] by Cat Sukiman
- ðŸ‘‰ See [these samples][irGciQVdlU] by Allison Kim
- ðŸ‘‰ See [these samples][vIHapIkyJu] by March Bachand

Just like characters, prop designs are created in a certain visual style, usually matching the graphic art style of the backgrounds/environments. Sometimes they match more closely to the character art style, especially if the backgrounds are very different from the characters' aesthetics. But they are objects needing to be designed at various angles since they are meant to be animated. For example, [Lilo & Stitch](<https://people.com/thmb/OuqC8cHTbN8OTu7c-ThAiPDn0Hk%3D/1500x0/filters:no_upscale():max_bytes(150000):strip_icc():focal(899x449:901x451)/lilo-and-stich-42716eea64ad4d16a01c76f8c1ec30c3.jpg&sa=D&source=editors&ust=1759597865156848&usg=AOvVaw22DPffkITsT78w84zLBOb2>) has watercolor-style painted backgrounds, so the prop designs needed to look like they could easily fit within the environments, but still match the characters' overall art style, for when they're interacting with them. Sometimes the props would be watercolor-style, sometimes they would be cel-painted with flat colors when needing to be animated.

ðŸ‘‰ Shape language, perspective & structure plays a major part in constructing prop models:

- [Exemple 1][cTLFqMeiaq]
- [Exemple 2][wYWAknAqdI]

Explorations sketches are made to rough out concepts before refining them into final designs:

![image](./images/unnamed_572.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1588.png)

Grouping thumbnail roughs together to see how they will work when they're all in one location is common exercise too:

![image](./images/unnamed_724.png)

Rotations are illustrated for props that will be seen and used a lot:

![image](./images/unnamed_1611.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1532.png)

Size comparisons are usually needed by having a faded version of the main character on display for size relationship, especially for props and items that are being handled and used by characters:

![image](./images/unnamed_1356.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_587.jpg)

Just like for character models, various views and angles are illustrated:

![image](./images/unnamed_488.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_812.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_720.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_028.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_114.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1398.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1347.jpg)

By Eva Strautkalne

![image](./images/unnamed_895.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_849.jpg)

By Skifati Moe

Prop designs follow the same visual language as any character and background design:

![image](./images/unnamed_535.jpg)

Props can also be attached to backgrounds, a common example are doors, they are first designed by background artists, but then handled as prop designs once they need to be animated to rotate and be shown at many angles, and being handled by the characters as well. A car parked in the background will only need to be designed from that one angle, but once a character steps inside and begins to drive it around, it will need to be properly designed and built with many angles and views, both the interior and exterior.

Entire buildings can be considered as props, whether or not they are animated (2D or 3D), even if they are levitating around by magic, and not turning or rotating, they still become an object needing to be moved around, therefore they become props.

- Observe the product design style of Steven De Groot [here][GgMzBGstnw].
- Watch [this guide][CAMsyTxGex] from Robert Laszlo, on translating proportions into perspective.
- If you are interested in more prop design tips, see [this tutorial][HvTPtILxqw] by Philip Dimitriadis.

![image](./images/unnamed_824.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_383.jpg)

Just like character or background designs, props are subject to rough sketching during the conceptual stages as well.

![image](./images/unnamed_1731.png)

![image](./images/unnamed_1355.png)

> ðŸ‘‰ [Advice on using Varied Proportions in Prop Design][KHrMgVpwJL]

Color concepts and color models are made in the same way as characters, you start off as roughs, then clean lines, then color.

![image](./images/unnamed_1007.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_712.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_376.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_627.jpg)

It's common for Prop Designers to create their own colors, they may have guidance from the Art Director or need to use background designs or character designs for color reference.

![image](./images/unnamed_768.jpg)

There can be some visual symbolism and **personification** involved in prop design:

This is the attribution of a personal nature or human characteristics to something [nonhuman][gEvjFUGdzx], or the representation of an abstract quality in human form. Like a tree designed with its branches resembling long arms reaching out, or simply adding a tiny face or an eye on a prop design, all these can add a quality of personality to the object. Examples found in creative writing: "The sun smiled down on us."

"The story jumped off the page."

"The light danced on the surface of the water."

Examples of this could be: a backpack being designed to look old, saggy, weathered and beaten up, to match the state-of-mind of its owner, or to show what hardships this backpack has undergone. A mirror designed to look monstrous and evil because it holds a magical creature inside. You can always subvert expectations by having a sweet, cute, innocent-looking object actually be used by someone dark and sinister for a certain effect. Imagine what an enchanted key would look like if it was designed to be linked to an all-seeing entity.

![image](./images/unnamed_393.png)

In novel writing and scriptwriting, personifying an object means to figuratively describe it with human traits in order to craft a vivid image of that object in your reader's mind. But to interpret it in illustration and animation would be to visually depict it so that it is instantly recognizable; to subconsciously have the viewer identify it with a personality type or behavior. The simple act of making a prop look old, worn out and drooping, versus making it look fresh, young, new, clean, perky and firm - can drastically change the feeling of how the design will be perceived and what history may lie in that prop's narrative and purpose.

![image](./images/unnamed_151.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_012.png)

Principles of appeal, structure, and contrasting shapes also apply to prop design, even the most simple objects:

![image](./images/unnamed_471.png)

By Isaac Orloff

Christine Klein's Prop Design Explorations:

![image](./images/unnamed_1662.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_955.png)

Sometimes the props become the characters:

![image](./images/unnamed_228.jpg)

Beetlejuice+Doomie inspired _car monsters_ by Jeanette Arroyo:

![image](./images/unnamed_1644.png)

Just like for characters, research is often necessary to do. For example, something as simple as _old fashion forks_, could have you researching the [history of silverware][SEUzVaBVwt] to help in your inspiration.

There are sites like [hum3d.com][QemKDESSkj] that are great for searching up and observing hundreds of objects, props, vehicles and anything else of this nature at various angles, for drawing reference.

Gathering photo references to help in the design process is important to the exploration and discovery part of your early concept art and rough sketching. For example, let's say you need to design an item for an _alchemist's portable toolbag_. Items to search up would be a portable tackle box or tool bag (adding old or vintage in your search) - begin sketching with a wide range of research images and see what items you can combine together:

![image](./images/unnamed_373.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_815.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1270.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1626.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_559.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_191.png)

Sometimes props can become very complex, as a single item may need to be illustrated from many different views and angles, and may have to be opened up to reveal its interior. Vehicles are often the case, as scenes can take place inside the vehicle as its own location, with plenty of moving parts, and lots of items needing to be handled and interacted with.

![image](./images/unnamed_1189.png)

Oftentimes props can be very detailed and need lots of guides and instructions on how they work, along with versions made for close-ups and for far shots so that detailed versions can be reserved for up-close/insert shots.

![image](./images/unnamed_169.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_526.png)

Notice how a red line is commonly used to indicate to the color stylist that those lines should be self-colored or for that linework is meant to be removed once the shapes are painted:

![image](./images/unnamed_1027.jpg)

In a typical 2D animated series, there's frequently more props to be designed than they are characters. Sometimes props and character designs cross paths, like when a character removes an article of clothing from their body, then it becomes this independent item that comes off the character and is now technically a 'prop', since it's now an item another character could grab and pick up and interact with, and maybe dropped on the ground, then becoming part of the scenery.

![image](./images/unnamed_1284.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1556.png)

Sometimes, as part of the character design, the model sheets need to have props included, like a samurai warrior with their katana, or a wizard that's always holding their magic staff, a character always wearing their backpack, or a butcher always carrying a cleaver with them. Usually it's items that are inherently linked to the character at all times, making it part of their character design by default. These are accessories that the character always has with them, therefore become a part of that character's design.

![image](./images/unnamed_682.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_744.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1415.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_047.jpg)

Just like for character design, reference is just as important (if not more so).

![image](./images/unnamed_1551.png)

![image](./images/unnamed_864.png)

- When researching for various styles, there are hundreds of places to be inspired from:
- [Exemple 1][wYDgCBRAKk]
- [Exemple 2][hXHItlRyyE]

When it comes to prop design, an element of selective exaggeration and caricaturing is often implemented as well, just like you would for any character stylization/simplification - it all depends on the needs and the **look** of the production. Often the environments, props, and vehicle designs must be shaped and constructed in very particular ways to make them appear like they belong and match the world they're in, alongside the characters they'll be used with. They can evoke lots of life and personality in their appearance, but they are usually built to be **appealing** and **functional**. As an example, let's look at one particular aspect of prop designs; focusing specifically in the area of vehicles in the "choro-Q" style:

![image](./images/unnamed_1326.jpg)

> _"When designing vehicles for an animated production, it's always good to clearly display the characters' faces. Many shows have the characters with larger proportioned heads, so the windshields should be adjusted accordingly. Toriyama knew this, and a lot of his manga work featured appropriately-designed vehicles, but cartooned in a style that works well for showcasing the characters. This chibi-style of vehicles became a Japanese toy fad in the early 80s named choro-Q, and they were known in North America as penny racers." -Murray Bain, Art Director_

![image](./images/unnamed_463.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_555.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1037.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1079.jpg)

![image](./images/unnamed_557.png)

Keep in mind that the props your characters handle and the vehicles they operate are essentially acting as supporting characters. They need to be practical for the needs of the production and what is happening in the story. How the characters USE the props must be kept in mind when constructing their shapes, forms and proportions.

For whatever props you may be designing, experimenting how to exaggerate and simplify is part of the design exploration process, as with everything else, **research** is crucial. For example, drawing vehicles can be one of the most challenging aspects of prop design. Just like for character design, vehicle designs need to be researched and collected for you to be inspired from.

**Style guides** can be made for prop design packs as well, these cross-over into background designs of course, as seen here for the Big Hero 6 series:

![image](./images/unnamed_135.jpg)
