Notice how interior design and furniture as well as exterior architecture and be "caricatured" to give buildings a sense of personality and distinctiveness as well:

![image](./images/unnamed_1062.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_730.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_980.jpg)

All animated productions have rules and guidelines like this, sometimes they are outlined in large volume and detail, many times they are barely documented officially at all, for reasons like lack of time to do so, and usually these style guides and directives are not solidified until late into the production.

The spectrum of Realism vs Caricature is present in all aspects of design stylization, including Backgrounds and FX designs as well. Some productions can barely have any FX design in them due to the nature of the stories, they don't require too many animated elements like, water, smoke, fumes, fire, splashes, ripples, sparks, electricity, and explosions. Other shows can have massive amounts needing to be made, depending on what the style and genre the story and world are set in. Sometimes the FX animators create their own FX designs based upon the storyboards and established art direction, other times dedicate 2D FX animators become designers to create key poses and colors to make sure the FX animations will stay on-style and consistent in its design and appearance. FX design are often overlooked and usually grouped into the Prop Design category of the design process.

![image](./images/unnamed_1097.jpg)

> ðŸ‘‰ [Rise of the TMNT movie: concept art and design sheets][vnirNyrpkc]

Sometimes FX designs cross-over into character model sheets, these could be special poses or custom design sheets to show how the FX are functioning with the characters:

Character![image](./images/unnamed_1021.png)

Character designers are essentially creating props all the time, the
clothes their characters wear, the glasses on their face, the hats on
their heads, as soon as they come off the character's body technically
becomes a prop.

### Walkthrough the Process

#### Pre-Boards and Post Boards

While character design happens continually through the production of each episode of an animated series, there are usually two primary stages:

Designs done BEFORE the storyboard artists start [boarding][qqwEexznKL], in order to give the storyboard artists the reference they need to draw the characters in their shots...

And designs done AFTER the storyboard is complete, wherein the designers base their designs on the storyboard artists' drawings.

The completed pack of design assets is something always in progress, from first reading and analyzing the script, through the storyboards stage and their revisions, then during character builds, and then even last minute additions and changes during layout & scene prep stages (just before animators start to animate the characters in their shots), and sometimes after animation has started. The odd thing here and there will need a quick design, since often some changes occur throughout each stage, and also, items get missed all the time and need to get caught and designed.

![image](./images/unnamed_1501.png)

Sometimes there's a strict and detailed breakdown made of the script, where most assets for characters, props and backgrounds are designed before storyboards begin, this way the storyboard artist has all the reference they need to plan out all their shots and sequences. Other shows have a very small, loose, rough preliminary design pack made, with barely any reference for the storyboard artist to use, giving the storyboard artist more creative liberties to create the concept of these designs, for you to use later to make the official design sheets out of. In this method the storyboard artist is encouraged to create, invent, expand upon the location designs, to make-up any secondary and incidental characters,whatever costume changes required, and create props/FX as needed, whether they were mentioned in the script or not.

This leaves a long list of items for the character designer to tackle, beyond all the assets mentioned in the initial script, but then they have the storyboards as a rough reference to start from, to eventually complete the list of design assets.

![image](./images/unnamed_788.png)

Incidental characters, designed using the storyboard as reference, is a very common part of the job.

No two productions are alike and the role and scope of a character designer's job can change significantly depending on the production, the pipeline, the supervisors/directors in charge and the style of the show

#### Design Handout

At the beginning of each episode, the character designer is given the script to read in advance of their design handout.

The design handout is the meeting where character designers are assigned their specific characters that they will need to design in preparation for boards. Sometimes the designs made before boards are called the _Fun Pack_.

This initial meeting, often called a Design Brief (or Briefing) is basically where it is decided what character(s) will be assigned to which artist and for what purpose. If the designer works directly for a client, for example, this is the part of the process that includes their communication with the person (Producer / Art Director / Supervisor) regarding the character(s).

In this phase, the artist should know the core personality of said character: is it a human, an animal, a creature, or an object (sometimes combinations of a few of these). It's not always obvious from the script or story outline alone. This brief also includes a list of certain personality traits and common emotional core values. Establishing the overall mood and mentality this character will communicate to the viewer. This list can be made by the designer by jotting down notes they made while reading the script or during the meeting with the art director or showrunner.

![image](./images/unnamed_957.png)

For example, if the project is an educational presentation aimed at third graders, it will require a cute and cheerful character that will appeal to children. Often your client may not be too certain or rock-solid about the backstory or personality, so they offer suggestions of what visual style they are looking for. They can instruct that the overall mood must be very warm and welcoming, even silly and fun. They may point you in the direction of a series, film, or short that they want you to use as inspiration. This phase, depending on the role of the character, is when the artists (or the clients) decide what the characteristics are. Some of those fundamental questions could be:

- What is the character's age? Where is the character from?
- What do they look like? What are their likes and dislikes?
- What are the character's dominant traits and personality?

During this meeting the Showrunner and the Director of the episode will also tell the designer if there are any particular attributes, racial specificity or personal characteristics that they want the designer to incorporate while designing.

Examples:

- _This character has a kind of Ryan Gosling, comical-cool type vibe._
- _This character is half Korean/half Puerto Rican so try to be conscious of this while designing them._
- _This character is an old alien boss, think Mr. Burns; make him frail, no teeth, hunchback, skinny limbs, big big-eyes, potbelly with a large cape._

![image](./images/unnamed_1055.png)

The designers receive their character assignments based on several factors:

- **The specific designer's strengths and sensibilities**
  - For example a character designer who is better with fashion may get the majority of the costume designs. A character designer who specializes in drawing animals will be favored to design the cast of characters for a pride of lions.
- **The availability of the designer**
  - Because multiple episodes are in play at once, some designers may wind up with more on their plate than others.
- **The designer's speed**
  - If a design needs to be turned out fast, it will usually be handed to the speedier designer.

Once the character designer has their allotted characters, they head back to their Cintiq/Tablet and get cracking.

![image](./images/unnamed_348.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_397.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_685.png)

#### Rough Sketches

Using photo reference, online images, and any specifications from the Director/Showrunner as their guide, the designer will start sketching out their rough ideas for the character.

After doing any necessary research on the subject, the designer will also incorporate their own taste and interpretation to give their designs flair and use their own artistic vision to really push the design.

The designer will create 3-6 different variations of sketches for the character with different [body shapes][ziuUzoXykL], outfits, hair styles, etc \-- this is the visual development/concept art stage. The goal is to create a nice variety of potential directions that still serve the story's designated characteristics. Make several iterations and go through your [checklist][rwLXtxQTYk].

Example:

- The script calls for a "_gym teacher_" character... Are they tall? Short? Lean? Buff? Super buff?
- The script calls for "_kids in the classroom_"... Are they 6, 8, 10 years old? Are they bored? Rowdy? Energetic?

The character designer explores all options.

![image](./images/unnamed_053.png)

#### Design Review

Once the designers have their rough sketches lined up, it's time for the review!

The Design Review is a meeting where the Art Director (main overseer of the overall look of the series) and the Showrunners look at the new designs and give their feedback and notes.

Usually the result of this initial design review will be a frankensteined new design, based on specific parts from the different sketches submitted.

The Showrunner might say: _"I like the head from #1, but the body from #3 and let's use the hair from #6"_.

Sometimes the Showrunner or Art Director will just like the one whole design with just some tweaks and adjustments needed \-- "_Let's go with #4, just shorten the hair, make the legs a bit longer_".

But the Frankenstein-ing approach is far more common.

![image](./images/unnamed_398.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_126.png)

#### Revisions and Revisions and Revisions

The process of _Design Review_ \>\> _Tweaks/Adjustments_ \>\> _Repeat_, can go on several more times until the Art Director and then the Showrunner is satisfied with the design.

It can take a while to get the exact look they are going for, to make certain it fits just right for the purposes of the show.

Usually if the small tweaks persist past a certain point, a Line Producer or Producer will step in and tell the design team and the Showrunner when time has run out, and to move on with the latest version, applying the final round of notes. Production Managers and Line Producers keep an eye on schedules and budgets, they let the creative process grow, flourish and expand as it's needed, but deadlines and deliverables can only be pushed so far, then they intervene to make sure the flow of production doesn't get bogged down for too long.

**Red-lining**, this the process an art director takes to draw-over your work with visual notes:![image](./images/unnamed_803.png)

![image](./images/unnamed_625.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1265.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_501.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_258.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1119.png)

Variations in clothing and hair styles are the most common last-stage adjustments to make.

![image](./images/unnamed_805.png)

By Jeff Merghart

#### Rotations

Once a rough 3/4 front design of the character is somewhat settled and approved, it's time for the designer to turn the character!

TURN- AROUNDS are the process by which the character designer draws the design in different rotations: 3/4 Front, Front, Profile (Side), 3/4 Back, Back.

In TV animation, [turnarounds][GodgcXxUMt] are essential.

They are the guides that show the storyboard artists and more importantly, the animators, how the character looks from different angles and shows them how to keep the character on-model as they move them through different poses. On rigged character builds the builds artists use the designs to setup and assemble the character as a digital puppet for animators to move around and pose them in their scenes to prep them to be animated.

![image](./images/unnamed_628.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1041.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1010.jpg)

#### Does This Turn?

In order for the Builds team to construct and setup your characters into a proper and efficient rig (and for the animation to be fluid and believable), character designs NEED to be able to turn properly... and often times during this stage of rotating a character, designers will realize where certain features on the character do not turn well. Sometimes more flat and graphic designs are made out of many cheats where they are only meant to look good in a certain way at specific view.

Common things that can come up are:

- Body shapes or costuming that looks awkward from different angles
- Facial features that do not translate to certain angles well (ie: when the nose doesn't work in the front view)
- Clunky or angular parts of the design that are messy to turn

![image](./images/unnamed_074.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1165.jpg)

If a design doesn't turn well, chances are it won't animate well.

As a result of this, the designer often has to keep tweaking and changing the design to get it to rotate just right.

![image](./images/unnamed_065.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_623.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1125.jpg)

By Parker Nielsen

Find the supervisor who will lead the **animation crew**, get advice from them and watch through the animatic/storyboards (if possible) to see if the character is meant to only be viewed in certain ways. This is because turning their head/body into these views might be quick-and-snappy in order to keep the stylistic qualities of the designs.

Communicating also with the department that will convert your designs into **builds** is important because oftentimes you can't be drawing attention to the fact that the character does not translate well in a three dimensional way. Some designs have lots of "cheats" to make them turn. The cheats make the design look good for each view, however it's made to look appealing with its graphic shapes, even though it may not be actually possible to three-dimensionally turn as a rotation, and that's fine for more stylized characters.

![image](./images/unnamed_1324.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_829.jpg)

#### Clean Lines

Once the character designer gets the design to rotate and has the roughs approved, they start cleaning up the line work... and once again more inconsistencies or odd things may arise in this stage, final detailing and fine-tuning in this stage of tying things down.

Designs are often tweaked again as they are cleaned up because the tighter line work makes design issues more obvious.

The entire character design process for the pre-board characters usually takes about 2 weeks from handout to clean-up.

Of course this can vary, depending on the production.

![image](./images/unnamed_909.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1348.png)

#### Is The Design Finished?

Designs may still be in flux right up until the episode actually goes out to go get animated.

The storyboards can also greatly affect the design.

For Example: The Director may request design changes based on new story changes or physical actions required of the characters made in the boards.

"I have a scene where Character X pulls up their hood to cover them from the rain, so let's be sure to give their jacket a hood"

![image](./images/unnamed_388.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_816.png)
![Captain Skull - Character Design](./images/unnamed_672.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_050.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_003.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_013.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_050.gif)

DESIGN and BOARDS are very collaborative, almost in a hidden way, some shows independently operate in their own bubbles but rely on each other to use each other's work as reference, other times they're often informing each other both ways. The Director and Art Director usually work together to make sure their respective teams are alert to the shifts in design and story.

Another collaborative overlap are the riggers, who come in after the characters are completed in design and proceed to build and set up the designs for the animators to use. [Rigging][TjWaeocjUQ]
is a crucial process in animation that enables characters and objects to move fluidly.

Rigging in 2D animation is the process of creating a digital skeleton for characters or objects. This skeleton allows animators to control and manipulate movements with precision.

Effective rigging is essential for producing smooth, believable animations.

Key components of 2D rigging include:

- Joints and bones
- Control handles
- Deformation tools
- Hierarchy systems

By setting up these elements properly, they ensure that characters can be easily posed and animated. This saves time and enhances the overall quality of the animation.

As a rigger, they're primary responsibility is to build the underlying structure that brings characters to life. They work closely with animators and character designers to create rigs that are both functional and efficient.

Typical tasks include:

1. Analysing character designs
2. Placing joints strategically
3. Setting up control systems
4. Testing rig functionality

Riggers need a combination of technical skills and artistic understanding. They must anticipate how characters will move and ensure the rig supports these movements seamlessly.

![image](./images/unnamed_066.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_056.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_072.gif)

#### Post Board Designs

Once the boards are done, the character designers may receive new designs in addition to whatever designs need to be tweaked based on boards.

To save designers time, during that initial **Handout**, Directors can call out which characters the board artists can board WITHOUT a pre-design...

But once the [storyboards][ZgCDKPbafN] are done, those boarded characters will still need to be designed properly.

![image](./images/unnamed_1163.png)

Screenshots of the boards are sent to the character designers who then create on-model character designs based on what the storyboard artists roughed in.

It is very common for storyboards to be completed with un-approved character designs in them, storyboards are rough, never 100% on-model or on-style, and the characters sketched in the boards are meant to be place-holders and used as reference for the process of creating the final designs. Then the rigs created from these designs will be the ones placed in & set up in each individual scene for the crew to animate with.

![image](./images/unnamed_675.png)

#### Alternates and Multiple Versions

These can be sometimes referred to as "ALTs" \-- these are designs required for hair and costume changes, but is also used for different states as well, like a charred or burned or wet versions of the character design.

![image](./images/unnamed_070.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_673.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1217.png)

#### Expressions, Mouth Charts & Special Poses

Another stage in the TV animation character design process is designing **expressions** and **mouth charts**. Once the boards are finished, the character designer will typically design mouth charts for the characters to be sent to the Builds team along with the turn-around which will have the characters get assembled and rigged into a build then sent to the animation team, along with the animatic (the storyboard assembled into a video with voices and sound effects to have the episode constructed into a blueprint showing all the camera moves, the length of each shot, and with the episode as a whole, at the proper length of time.

![image](./images/unnamed_868.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_881.jpg)

The mouth chart will guide the animators when they animate the characters speaking. They show what the character's mouth shapes are for the different syllables for all characters with speaking roles, this is for all the lip sync animation they'll have to do. This ensures that the character remains consistent as they speak their dialogue.

Expressions sheets are usually only made for primary characters, to show how to stay on-style as a spectrum of expressions are made as reference for animators to have so they can understand the characters better for their facial anatomy and range of emotions.

![image](./images/unnamed_706.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_673.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_1433.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_686.png)

A **Special Pose** is a specific character design based on extreme or exaggerated or purposefully off-model drawings done by the board artist in the storyboard.

The character designer will tie down this extreme pose and make it more on-model, while keeping the same energy found in the board drawing.

This is to help the animation team know how to interpret and handle the very exaggerated or very specific pose in the board so it doesn't break style. These are usually drawn by the best artists on the crew and can be the most fun (and most difficult) to do.

Sample of Special Poses with storyboard comparisons:

![image](./images/unnamed_1087.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1335.png)

#### Do Character Designers Color Design?

Typically NO.

Most TV animation production pipelines have separate artists known as Color Stylists or Color Designers whose job is to color the characters and props.

TV animation character designers usually work predominantly in black and white.

Some character designers may use gray tones in their designs to help break up the linework, to make the designs clear for presentation purposes. Blocking out different shapes and subtly guiding the color stylist in the direction they were thinking.

Every show is it's own unique animal and there may very well be instances where you may be allowed (or asked) to color your characters designs. Especially on smaller productions where character designers are asked to complete many aspects of the design process.

![image](./images/unnamed_1052.png)

If you happen to have a knack for color, you may be asked to do color concepts in your rough drawings as well, at the very least, to get the process going.

![image](./images/unnamed_683.jpg)

#### Who Do I Work With? - Part one

Primarily, character designers work closest with the Art Director.

The art director (or design supervisor) is the foremost person in charge of the overall look of the series and tracking all assets. They guide and support the designers to make sure all the character designs stay ON-STYLE with the look of the show.

Character Designers will also receive notes from Directors and Showrunners. They may communicate with COlor Stylists to discuss their ideas for the color treatment on certain things. For example: "_I thought maybe this character's hair could be two-toned_".

![image](./images/unnamed_477.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_802.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1648.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1500.png)

#### Who Do I Affect? - Part one

Character designers affect the [storyboard artists][zhPXNnwqbz] but they are also heavily affected by them, along with directors as well. The way designers are used for the story can greatly dictate what the design needs. Designs and Boards are two very interdependent departments, and are often very symbiotic.

![image](./images/unnamed_1559.png)

Another department they communicate with are the artists that take the designs and create rigs out of your designs. The Builds team take the turnarounds and assemble it all into a model that the animators can use and manipulate in their scenes.

![image](./images/unnamed_064.gif) ![image](./images/unnamed_047.gif)

### Tools of the Trade

For the most part, character designers in TV animation are working with Adobe Photoshop, or similar programs like Procreate. Photoshop provides a tidy UI to both sketch and polish designs and is a software any aspiring designer needs to be fluent in.

There are many other illustration software like Gimp, Affinity, Clip Studio Paint, Krita, Paint Tool SAI, and Procreate. In reality, they can ALL do the job very well. Anything that allows you to export layers as bitmap (PNG alpha) images for the next artist to grab and use to build the character as a 2D rig is all you need. Your employer or supervisor may have specific requirements to use certain software, templates and resolution settings, these are all easy to ascertain and adapt to. Ask your client/director so that you can get familiar with the tools and templates ahead of time.

![image](./images/unnamed_872.png)

- ðŸ‘‰ [Guide to all the various file types for design and illustration][DJkRmklOQQ]
- ðŸ‘‰ [Full tutorials on drawing characters in Photoshop my Dermot O'Connor][LqhqLbyejR]

Pen Tablets/Drawing Tablets are an essential tool nowadays, whether you are designing with bitmap or vector-based software, or designing for TV/film or video game industries.

Look into this guide on the best [digital tablets][oiSkYYBzsQ] (expensive, but it's the best investment you can make and they last forever)

> ðŸ‘‰ [Improve your tablet drawing experience][UBgHaaaJFd]

- Here's some introductions to using Photoshop as your character design tool:[(1)][QfbDIrWCWR] [(2)][vkupMZFCuN] [(3)][kmXQwrnupX] [(4)][dINteeDahS] [(5)][rFFtmMgCmO]
- Using Harmony as your character design tool:[(1)][kAjlgWnXre] [(2)][VQlSGutiEj]
- Using Illustrator as your character design tool: [(1)][dbVqbqCoPS]

![image](./images/unnamed_003.gif)

Never underestimate the power of [sketching][LrEjTAmTXZ] in your sketchbooks, it's one of the most important self-teaching tools to use, and try it with a pen instead of a pencil, [the results][QKrpYbuViF] may surprise you.

This is a great thing to do at the beginning of each day before starting on your design work: [Warm-up Exercises][eAQdhCalPI]

### Terminology

- **Intellectual Property:** Pertaining to creations of any artistic works, usually referred to as "IP". It can include designs, symbols, logos, names, and images used in all film, television or video games. It can be for individual characters, the series or film title, and even the concept of the world and story they reside in. A 'property' is always referring to the concept or show itself. See more general info on the term [here][nheUCtbrXL].
- **POC:** Proof of Concept is material (artwork or writings) typically derived from a pitch or pilot project, which demonstrates that a design concept or story proposal is feasible, and shows what the idea actually is. In animation it comes from pitching and attempting to sell a new intellectual property as an idea for a potential series or film and it applies for series, shorts and videogames. Creating a proof of concept for a new animated film or series could involve creating a pitch deck, which is a document or package of writings and artwork aimed at outlining what the concept is about and how it looks, usually containing character designs, background designs, character descriptions and the story premise. Sometimes an animated teaser or sizzle reel is made to show off the look and feel of what the animated property would be and these are also called a Proof of Concept.
- **Public Domain:** This refers to creative materials that are not protected by intellectual property laws such as copyright, trademark, or patent laws. The public owns these works, not an individual author or artist. Anyone can use a public domain work without obtaining permission, but no one can ever own it. Researching which characters are in public domain can help insure that and resemblances you do create for your own characters will or will not be in danger of any copyright infringements.
- **NDA:** A Non Disclosure Agreement is one of the first forms a producer will have you sign for them, even before a work contract. This is because an NDA is a legally binding contract that establishes a confidential relationship between you and your employer (producer/studio/client). The individual or parties signing the agreement all agree that the sensitive information they may obtain will not be made available to others. An NDA may also be referred to as a confidentiality agreement. If you are employed by a studio, oftentimes you need to sign one just to receive the visuals or written info about the characters/productions before your studio bids on the production or negotiates for the contract and budget, long before the work begins.
  - Non-disclosure agreements are also common for studios entering into negotiations with other studios, or a producer wanting to share information (scripts, designs, discussions about the production) with you, or the studio you're with. They allow the parties to share sensitive information without fear that it will end up in the hands of competitors. In this case, it may be called a mutual non-disclosure agreement. They are all the same in their intentions and purpose. In the video game industry there are very strict processes for security and not allowing any information about their projects to leak out to the public. They are very common and nothing to be afraid of, it means that you agree to not talk about the property with anyone else outside the production and your bosses. This includes anything online; emails, texts, conversations, and social media.
- **Onboarding:** A human resources term referring to the process of introducing a newly hired employee into an organization. For a new designer in an animation or gaming studio, it's an important part of helping employees understand their new position and job requirements. This usually consists of tours and handing the new employee some documents showing contact info of your leads/supervisors, descriptions of the production you are working on, getting familiar with the file server, workflow guides, schedules and lists of deadlines, info as to how to submit your work, and receiving any references for the tasks that are required of you.
- **Pipeline:** A term that refers to the [workflow][vQyatgQSRl] for the entire production. The bigger the production, the bigger the crew, the bigger and longer the pipeline. Design departments sit near the beginning of the production pipeline, but various forms of design tasks continue throughout the entire production, especially on an episodic-based series, and even in feature films where designs are needed even in final visual effects stages. See [this][cXPZJvwWgT] for where and how designs fit into the pipeline of a typical 2D animated series, and see [this][TAnFifXGAj] for how the production portion of the pipeline works in a 3D animated feature film.
- **Demographic:** This term is used to describe who the designs (and the production) are aimed for. In most cases there's an age range (or a target audience) for the animated characters being created along with the stories & world they're a part of. While designing the character you should keep in mind the nature of the audience viewing these characters. For example, if you are designing the characters for children it should be designed with basic shapes and colors. The genre and rating on the game or the genre and rating of the film you are designing for is usually your main indicator of what age group this production will be targeted for. Designs for a horror game will have a very different art direction than what you will find in a kids' game like Minecraft, Lego, or Little Big Planet. A game or an animated series for mature audiences will have characters looking differently compared to characters found in a game or series made for kids under 12 year-olds, and even more different for preschoolers.
- **S&P:** Standards and Practices, the term used by broadcasters and networks as a set of corporate rules to follow to self-regulate the content on the animated series, strongly depending on the demographic, the younger the intended audience the longer the list for S&P for everyone to follow. For example, there are long lists of S&P for children's or pre-school series, since it's intended for young audiences, for adult animated content, the S&P regulations are far less. For PG or Teen related demographics, the level of violence depicted on screen must be limited. S&P passes are made during script writing, designs, storyboarding, and final animation stages. Revision notes may come in on character designs in order to remove or change aspects of the design they may deem as inappropriate or not on-brand.
- **EP:** Executive Producers consult on the production and provide specialist expertise. They may also help other [producers][ilWSObzdKo] on the business side of things by facilitating the finances and contracts between broadcaster, distributor and the service studio providing the design and animation work. Some producers are in charge of fundraising and marketing for the production. The title of executive producer may also be credited to the original creator of the source material that is being adapted, and as a result, may also serve as head writer and showrunner for the series. They are often the ones seeking out and hiring the writers, directors and other key personnel to get the production started once it is greenlit and set to go.
- **PM:** Production Manager, the person to go to for contracts, schedules, and payment questions and concerns. They manage all the stages of the production's deadlines and track each artist's daily and weekly progress. They can often act as the human resources person as well, but usually are the ones most in communications with the client/executive producer.
- **Showrunner:** The series showrunner is the person with the overall vision for the production. Writing, producing, and creative leadership is their main focus. They work closely with voice casting, voice recording, scriptwriting, character development, and guiding the heads of departments like episodic directors, art directors, animation supervisors, storyboard supervisors, and post production supervisors. This is to ensure that all aspects of the production in each stage on the creative level, are on-style and up to the appropriate levels of quality.
  - Oftentimes the showrunner is the creator of the series concept, and sometimes this role is in two people's hands, where they split the responsibilities - usually one showrunner focuses on scripts, voices, storyboards, sound designs & music, and the other showrunner overlooks designs, animation, backgrounds, colors, compositing/FX and quality assurance. Sometimes they act as the head writer, supervising director, or creative director for the series. Other times they will communicate all their thoughts about character design to the art director so that the info can be transferred to the designers. Other times the design style comes from their personal style directly, so a designer would work closely with showrunners at the beginning, to achieve the look they're going for.
- **Design Coordinator:** This can have many other names, such as, Assets Manager, Design Supervisor, Artistic Coordinator, but their role is to assist the Art Director in managing the deliveries, approvals, versions, and scheduling of the design process. When there's dozens of episodes overlapping, the number of characters, props, and key locations that accumulate on the production can go into the hundreds, and when the Art Director is focusing purely on the creative & artistic side of things while conducting quality control amongst their designers, sometimes a design coordinator is needed to manage all the many lists of assets and design elements on the show.
- **Pilot:** The first episode of a series, created to test the concept, characters, and overall potential of the show before committing to a full season. It serves as a proof of concept and a pitch to networks and streamers, showcasing the show\'s premise and style. If the pilot is successful, the show is \"picked up\" and a full season is produced, with the pilot often becoming the premiere episode.
- **Visual Development:** The building blocks for any type of visual art, from concept art to graphic design, illustration, animation, and gaming. The scope of a visual development artist's work gives them a level of professional creative control that is vital to the entire process. This comes from the versatility of their work being applicable to a wide variety of media formats. They're responsible for creating the mood, tone, and color palette of these worlds created from the writers, and creators of the property. No task or element is too large or small; as a visual development artist, you can design sets, environments, characters, clothing, vehicles, props, furniture, lighting, visual effects, and just about everything else that appears in all aspects of a visual work. More info [here][ObagHnrbyM] on the skill sets required in being part of a Vis Dev team. More info [here][kCIVoFscwL] on the work that is made to develop the Art Direction for a film or series.
- **Shape Language:** A concept used in design and animation to communicate a mood, story, or history behind the design. Giving familiar meanings to the shapes the characters are made with. When used in character, prop, and background design, shapes can tell a story, show personality, and elicit an emotional response in the viewer without using any words or movements. Depending on the character's overall shape or combination of shapes that make up their physicality, it can give visual hints as to the character's nature and core identity. Design Language and Shape Design are alternate terms used for the same theory.
- **Thumbnails:** Small rough sketches, drawn fast to create lots of ideas for designs or poses, and used to explore various concepts, creating a way to visually rehearse what works and what doesn't.
- **Reference:** Materials, assets, and images provided to (or researched by) the artist to have as a strict or casual guide to follow. These can be used for [inspiration][FCELcyDien] to the art direction of the property being designed.
- **Turnaround:** The rotation of the character displaying multiple views/sides to be used as reference for the building and animating of the character, see [this guide][bNFRFvisSv] on the process.
- **Construction:** Refers to when you're breaking down your character into their basic shapes and forms to reveal the inner structure. Construction lines are usually the geometry, cross-hairs, and guidelines that are drawn through the character's features to show the three-dimensional aspects of their body parts.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_564.png)
- **Anthropomorphic:** Human-like physical attributes in a non-human character, usually referring to an animal character that acts human, as seen in many animated properties and storybooks. Not just biped animals but 4-legged animals standing up-right. These are [animals][tupFqwORoK] depicted as walking, talking, dressing, and behaving like people. Even animated smoke that takes the form of a person can be described as anthropomorphic.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_276.png)
- **Asset:** A general term for all aspects of designs and rigs, including all states and versions of characters, props, FX, and backgrounds. Even though they go through their various stages of rough, color, builds, etc., they all need to be tracked for the progress of each design element, and these are all referred to as 'Assets'. On big productions there can be Assets Managers and Design Coordinators in charge of tracking all assets, which is usually referring to all the individual visual elements seen on-screen. Designated asset names and numbers are determined after the script is complete and then again after storyboards are complete.
- **Lead Sheet:** A spreadsheet outlining all design elements found in the production. In an animated series all characters, props, FX, and environments/locations are listed and cataloged for which shots they all appear in for each episode. At first these are broken down from a script as a preliminary design pass, then after storyboards are complete, any additional designs created are extracted from the animatic to then be added to the library of design assets to be officially made. As the production moves forward and more episodes are completed and locked at script and storyboard stages, the more lead sheets are made and updated. These breakdowns are sometimes called a Route Sheet, Assets List, Design List or Log List. A script 'breakdown' is the process of extracting all the information from the story to produce lists of all design assets like this, the process is done again after a storyboard/animatic is finalized to list all the on-screen design elements that were added by the story artists.
- **Design Brief:** A meeting to discuss the design tasks, happening just before the first pass of the design process. On a feature film this can happen for each new sequence that goes into production, on a series it happens before each episode enters production, usually every week or two. It's accompanied with a document created by the showrunner, creative director, art director, or episodic director to give to the design crew as a guide for each episode's breakdown, for any new design elements that need to be created. It's a process usually reserved for 2D or 3D animated shows where each script is analyzed and visual reference is compiled for specific direction on how to produce the new key backgrounds and characters found in the story.
  - Also known as a **Design Kickoff** or **Design Launch**. These meetings with the designers have the art director or series director talk about the specifics of the design tasks that are next to be started in the production. These are discussions on the direction and approach for new characters or character re-dresses.Briefings can look like excerpts from the script pages with images in the margins (on the side of the text); Showing reference images like sketches, photos, and thumbnails for the artist to follow as visual guides. Other briefings can look like a simple document or design kit with reference images and descriptions, provided by the director.
- **Design Kit:** Usually something handed out during a Design Brief, these are also known as a Ref Sheet, Guide Sheet or Reference Pack. It's a document containing various visual references or guidelines for particular designs that can be used by the designers as a target to have in mind while they work. A Design Kit or Design Handout can even be a "how-to guide" with files and imagery showing how to create certain artistic aspects of the design style, it can have tutorials on how to achieve a certain look or effect. These sometimes include focused step-by-step processes and technical information as well. These kits can be handed out during or just after the design launch and are made for designers to have them to better know what the intended look and style of specific design tasks they are assigned, usually made after the storyboards are done.
- **Show Bible:** Can be referred to as a Design Bible when specifically made for Art Direction. Show Bibles contain all the general information needed for the design team to use as reference for the overall style of the series for both characters and backgrounds. It can also have guidelines for how to handle and produce other aspects of the production as well, like the writing style (for the scriptwriters), the shooting style (for the storyboard artists), and the sound design style (for the musical score and sound effects artists).
- **Deliverables:** A list of what stages will need to be delivered for your work. Sometimes there's specific deadline dates to follow, other times it's more of a general weekly arrangement of deliverables to submit for review for each character and for each stage of the design process. The actual design package put together that is needed to go out every week for a client or showrunner to review can also be referred to as 'deliverables'.
- **Redress:** A character that's already been designed and finished, but is now partially re-designed to create new and different versions. Usually alternate varieties like hair style and costume changes. Examples can be stuff like adding a backpack, having wet hair, added gloves, torn clothes, different boots, burned/charred clothes, damaged armor, muddy clothes, or a new hat/hooded jacket pulled over the head.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_551.png)
- **ALT:** Alternate or Alterative, usually referring to an alternative to a design. When presenting ALTs, you usually show different versions as options to use, sometimes minor design or color variations.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1306.jpg)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_674.jpg)
- **Off-Model:** A term used to point out how a design or the drawing/posing of a character is off-style or inaccurate to how they should look. When some feedback is given on some designs, character layouts, or animation poses the notes can refer to them as being "off-model". They need to be revised and redrawn to be "on-model" by using the reference shown in the provided revision notes, style guides and model sheets. Sometimes moments are drawn to purposefully look off-model to capture the intent of the scene for the more extreme nature of the expression/pose at the time, [Ren & Stimpy][bclbwYQovE] famously did this constantly. Other times the designs go off-model but for the purpose of creating or copying another art direction style entirely, therefore creating new models to be used for just a scene or specific to that sequence or episode, as often seen in [Teen Titans Go!][PnmemvCDsg]
- **3pt, 4pt, 5pt:** Abbreviations for how many views to illustrate when creating the rotations for the characters, for example - 3 point turnarounds are usually Front, 3/4 Front, 3/4 Back views - 5 point turnarounds are usually Front, 3/4 Front, Side, 3/4 Back, Back views. This can range between 2pt and 16pt.
- **Lineup:** When one view of multiple characters is placed on one sheet to see them all at once for [size comparison](#size-comparison-charts) purposes. It's important to show the scale of all your characters relative to each other.
- **Fun Pack:** The pre-board design bundle, all the design sheets for the primary characters and locations, sometimes as rough designs, to be used as reference for the storyboard artists. This is usually the bare minimum design sheets a board artist needs to know what the cast and settings depicted in the script look like. Final model sheets then builds happen during and after the storyboard process.
- **MDP:** Main Design Pack, the completed sets of all the main characters and background designs, to have as reference for all departments.
- **Style Guide:** This expands upon the Pitch Deck/Show Bible, having elements of the model pack but refers to all character and prop/location design references for the film or series, and expands upon it. With a focus on guides for how to create the backgrounds and characters in its particular style. It covers many aspects of the production's art direction; as shown [here][EnOdWUETgf] and [here][vixrTCJgQb]. They can be small or large and elaborate documents that outline DOs & DONTs for the animation teams and background painters, or specific "how-to-draw" playbooks for the design crew.
  - These are sometimes also called Pasteup Sheets and [Breakdown][CARdkahxoa] Sheets. Style guides can also be made for [background layout][QSAgTeMHEm] designs and [storyboarding][gYHYJYgkff]; and for painting and color guides as shown [here][nyKMoDmAve]. Style guides are a great way to bring together a group of people to create one cohesive style for the design and layout/animation teams. [Style guides](#style-guides) can even be created before the concept goes into production, and later the MDP becomes included in the style guide.
  - Thinking about what your style guide would look like is a great place to start when developing your show or film concept as a pitch idea, you begin by laying down all the visual rules and standards. These can also be called a Design Bible, usually seen in animated series, and one can be made for each department: Script Bible, Storyboard Bible, Animation Bible, each one made specific for the team of people working in those stages of production.
- **Workbook:** Usually used in 2D animated feature film productions, it is an expanded upon style guide/lookbook & background design guide. It is made during the character design / production design phase and it keeps growing in size and scope while overlapping storyboards and layout stages. It's a collection of artwork and guidelines for design, color, lighting, staging and layouts, and handed out to all artists to use as reference.
- **Readable:** A term used to identify how much a design or pose can be "read". Meaning the more readable a design is, the more clear and recognizable it will be. A design that doesn\'t read refers to a character visual that is difficult to understand or identify at a glance, often due to confusing silhouettes, overly complex details, or a lack of distinct features that make it hard to discern their personality or role in a story.
- **Plusing:** A broad term you get from notes made by a director or producer, generally meaning to plus the design further, adding more quality and refinement to a certain area or to the overall artwork.
- **Naming Convention:** A system established on how to label and name the designs relative to how the digital files are named. There's a million different ways of doing so, and most of the time your supervisor/director on the production will establish a template for you to follow.
  - Char_Julian_CLR_TRN_V05
  - Char_Maximus_CLN_TRN_V04
  - Char_RoyBatty_RGH_TRN_V03
  - Here's a common template to use for the asset naming method. This one shows the first part identifying the asset as a character, the second part is the name of the character, then the status being concept art or rough or clean line art or color, then the state that it is a turnaround, and then the version number. You can have acronyms of the series name and the season and episode numbers and short forms for Rough (RGH) Clean-up (CLN) and Color (CLR) and many others that a supervisor or director usually determines.
  - You can also have the series abbreviated name and season+episode number as shown here...
  - JT_S01E10_Cha_MrEagle_CON_TRN_V02
- **Color Roughs:** Thumbnails or rough sketches made with paints rather than line work.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1425.png)
- **Draw-Overs:** When a supervisor or director sketches over a pose or design to visually show what adjustments need to be made for the design revisions. These are also called redlines or red-lining.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_045.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_208.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_208.png)
- **Clean-up:** The finished line art, the process of [tracing-over][nJokjiFBPG] rough designs to produce the [final lines][JdtPktvCSa] on the characters. Various styles and [weights][OvBuRpDCnZ] to the lines can be used. Depending on the art direction, sometimes a much thicker outline is required (like Dexter's Laboratory and The Grim Adventures of Billy and Mandy), or very thin linework (like Family Guy and Bob's Burgers), or wobbly/boiling linework (like Ed, Edd & Eddy), or thick-to-thin tapered lines (like Sam & Max, The Hollowand Primal), or a messy dry brush style (like Foster's Home for Imaginary Friends and Jacob Two-Two), or a loose/sketchy line style (like Tante Hilda), or a graphite pencil-textured line art style (like Penn Zero and Green Eggs & Ham), or a nearly lineless style (like Samurai Jack). Search up all of these, have fun collecting design styles!
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1725.png)
- **Line Weight:** The thickness of your lines. Sometimes referred to as Line Gauge.
- **Line Value:** The color and lightness/darkness of your lines.
- **Self-traced:** Also known as self-colored or self-inked, referring to the colored line art in your design. The line work is sometimes just a lighter or darker version of the base fill color found around the line, instead of the typical black lines on a character design.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1433.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_369.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_214.png)
  - Example; Spongebob's colored outlines are just a darker tone from his base skin color. See how Daffy's arms and facial features have outline colors turning white when crossing over the body or line work needing to be visible for expressions - this way they don't get lost as the dark-on-dark color overlaps. [FX designs][QowYnHYJfl] like water, fire, and smoke are often made of colored outlines, or no lines at all.
  - Self-traced lines can be used to make the lines visible when they would normally disappear or blend in too much, or can simply be painted lines, anything other than the default black tone, to make the linework stand out more clearly. Another example is when you need to isolate the edges of where the shadows are on your black-and-white character designs.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1523.png)
- **Draw-Throughs:** When you need to show what is underneath a design element or body part, usually hair, capes, cloaks or even an arm that is overlapping the torso.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1151.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_461.png)
- **Economy of Line:** The concept of the line economy is a familiar one to anyone who has studied ï¬gure drawing or has done classical animation. Basically, it means that we should strive to draw efï¬ciently by using fewer lines to say what we need to say, rather than a lot of unnecessary lines that lack a sense of intent. In character design this translates to visually depicting everything you need without unnecessary details, capturing the shapes, forms and personalities with as few lines as possible. This is associated with the term 'line mileage' which means the amount of lines drawn in a design, illustration, or any hand-drawn animation scene. The more line mileage a design has, the longer it takes to re-draw that design over and over again.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1509.png)
- **Cel Shading:** A technique used in illustrations and animation to give 3D objects and characters a more 2D/cartoon-like appearance. The term \"cel\" refers to celluloid, the clear plastic sheets that were once used in traditional animation to create [hand-drawn][zJeXHZqhZy] animations. Most commonly seen in [anime][pFHhwjVvJr], where the shadows are hard-edged as opposed to a soft-edge. There's a similar look with [toon-shading][WPfOgNVBrs]; a rendering technique designed to make 3D computer graphics appear to be flat by replacing smoothly mixed in gradient shaders from the shadows, highlights and tones to appear as blocks of color and hard-edged shapes, along with the outer edges and inner details having an illustrative line added to it, to mimic [flat cartoon][kFVFcUJxGl]/[comic book][cveLFbbbsV] style artwork.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_194.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_628.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1651.png)
- **Rim Light/Rim Shadow:** Tone shading indicating a light source on a character, they can also have a hard edge or soft edge.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1599.png)
- **DX or Alpha:** The level of transparency on a color or tone, usually for a shadow overlay, or the amount of opacity on a certain character element, often seen for eyeglasses.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_935.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1367.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1475.png)
- **Back Lit:** Also known as BCKLIT, simply an old school term that means having a glow effect on a certain element or the entire character. Could be a glowing gem on your character's chest, a glowing light from the helmet on their head, or glowing eyes.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1120.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_349.png)
- **Breaking the Model:** Also known as 'break the design' - simply the process done before cleaning up the character into vector art, by separating all the elements of your character. Which means drawing all the pieces in full that are hidden by other overlapping body parts. This is to set it up for the [rigging process][oebyXvlcTO] by layering all the elements separately, filling in any gaps. This can be done by a clean-up artist or character setup artist or builds artist.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1053.png)
- **Builds and Rigs:** When character designs are broken up, converted and assembled into fully [setup rigs][daJaIxKtFW] with all their colored assets included. Mouth packs for lip sync, body/head rotations & tilts for various views of the subject, and blink comps for the eyes are just some of the many components created in the [rigging][JnXJtXkGoj] stage of production. These [rigs][OZMbDhbDWA], once completed, get handed over to scene setup and then for animators to use in their shots. Often referred to as Builds or Models in 3D/CG animated productions. Also referred to as Puppets or Rigs for Stop-motion productions where the 2D designs are translated into handmade sculpts and fabricated into 3D-printed body parts to be assembled with armatures for them to be posed, animated, and filmed.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_069.gif)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_062.gif)
- **Stock:** Referring to Stock Art or Stock Animation. It's when there\'s a pre-made set of designs, animated movements/cycles or body parts that animators use to recycle and implement into their scenes. Stock Hands could be a library of hand poses that animators extract from a build to use in their scene. Stock Walk Cycle would be an animated walk that was created for animators to grab and insert into their scenes to save them the trouble of re-doing one from scratch. Actions and poses like Jumps, Turns, Blinks, Runs, Expressions and Mouth Shapes (mouth charts for lip sync) can all be considered Stock Art to be saved, archived, and re-used by the builds & animation crew when needed.
- **Character Layout:** On a 3D/CG production this is the process of setting up the initial [staging of a scene][MkONnVhzeX], including camera angles, character positions, and key movements to match and improve upon the storyboard's composition and shot framing. This sets up the scene to be prepped for the animators to start on their shots. On a hand-drawn/2D animated production this is a rough posing pass made after storyboards are completed on a traditionally-animated series or film. This is where you draw out the extremes and the main tent poles of the character staging to plot out the acting and action along with the camera angle & framing. Special Poses and Character Layout work can be a great start on your road to becoming a character designer, as work like this shows your ability to draw consistently and on-model, while displaying artistic skill, speed, and diversity in your draftsmanship.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1182.jpg)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_366.jpg)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1448.jpg)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_134.jpg)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_759.jpg)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1242.jpg)
- **Special Poses:** When a designer draws certain moments from the storyboard that need more attention to make sure they're clear for the animator. Similar to character layouts, where the artist essentially does a re-draw of the storyboard pose to be more on-style and more specific, this can include forced perspective, difficult angles, or a very extreme/exaggerated or particular pose of the character as well. A director or supervisor chooses which drawings get to be made into special poses. These are moments that are either pivotal to the episode or gags that need to make a super clear translation from the storyboard to the final animation. Sometimes these special poses are meant to purposefully be off-model to exaggerate the acting in the scene.
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_224.jpg)
  - â˜ï¸Special Poses by Bob Flynn
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1187.png)
  - â˜ï¸Special Poses by Earnie Gilbert
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_211.jpg)
  - â˜ï¸Special Poses by Chris Battle
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_1469.jpg)
  - â˜ï¸Special Poses by Mike Milo
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_756.png)
  - ![image](./images/unnamed_709.jpg)
  - â˜ï¸Special Poses by Serapio Calm

### Summary

- **Designing a New Character**

  - Step 1: Research and discovery
  - Step 2: Hone in on your character\'s traits
  - Step 3: Do lots of sketching
  - Step 4: Finalize your character design drawing (begin with a front view or 3/4 view)
  - Step 5: Create the full rotation for the turn-around views
  - Step 6: Develop some rough poses and expressions
  - Step 7: Get feedback

- **Strategies on your Approach**

  - Personality!
  - Visual Interest!
  - Simplicity & Recognizability - Add details and complexity later, if needed
  - Avoid ClichÃ©s - Unless that's the intention
  - Know The Character's Story - Where does a character begin, where does it end, and what does it do in-between?
  - In the beginning, when you're only exploring concepts; draw more loose, rough, and fast - create lots of thumbnails!
  - Shape & Form - Consider how the character\'s shapes work in three dimensions and how they look from different angles, while you are searching for that character of yours, play around with hair/fur styles, various attitudes/postures and costumes/outfits \> Afterwards, think about detailing & surfacing - are textures, shading or shadows needed?
  - What is the character's linework? - Is it thick or thin, tapered or varied, bumpy or textured, colored lines or perhaps no lines at all!
  - Paint - Explore color options (if required), use color to differentiate personalities, highlight features and create contrast \> Color can also convey a character\'s mood, attitude, and role in the story!

- **Things To Keep in Mind**

  - [âœ“] Appeal/Believability: Balance originality and familiarity. Create visually appealing silhouettes. Consider how the character\'s design reflects their role and personality traits. For example, iconic characters like Homer Simpson, Mickey Mouse, and Mario have simple yet recognizable designs.
  - [âœ“] Use References Effectively: Research distinctive styles. Try out a range of possibilities, then repeat and experiment. Seek out and incorporate feedback from others you trust.
  - [âœ“] Uniqueness: Make your character visually distinct and easily recognizable by incorporating symbolic or iconic elements.
  - [âœ“] Audience: Consider what will make your character appealing and believable to your demographic.
  - [âœ“] Backstory: Give your character some history, personality, and motivations that help explain how they look; torn jeans, makeup, scars, braces, hunched back, messy hair, a cane, an eyepatch, an arm cast - things that could add story to their disposition and individuality.
    - Full breakdown [here][aHTcjszNVG] and [here][tPjKhLFdQl].

---

> Four factors to consider when designing your new characters from an established property:

1. Recognizability of the intended visual style
2. Shapes, features, and volumes are consistent throughout the rotation
3. Linework and details all follow a natural sense of logic and appeal
4. Overall anatomy, proportions, and costumes/surfaces are consistent to the established graphic style

â˜ï¸ This is also a general rubrics for each of the assignments outlined below.

![image](./images/unnamed_1490.png)

---

The assignments and theory covered below are more intended for 2D animated series/films, but the practice and approach for them can all be applicable to 3D/CGI/Stop-motion & Video Game development as well. The most important thing to remember is to have fun with them!

![image](./images/unnamed_377.png)

In terms of timespan; you can hit these 12 assignments full-time and expect to complete them all in roughly 30 to 60 hours total \-- Depending on research-time and personal speed, your results may vary. This was originally created as part of an animation program where students would spend 3-6 hours per week, executing each assignment as homework.

The hours increase as the requirements & parameters go up in the later assignments - in which case, picking away at these exercises and completing all assignments within 10 weeks at a very casual pace is often the preferred approach. But this could be all done within just a 1 to 3 weeks at a more steady and dedicated pace, this includes reading, watching, listening to all external links and supplementary materials. So don't be concerned about your output, go at your own speed, and produce the best work you can.

You may use the designs you create from these assignments for your portfolio, while citing credit to the original studio/artist \-\-- Example: Â©Disney Â©WB Â©Matt Groening

---

The Assignments outlined below are to create a NEW character within a chosen established style from an existing series/film.

There are many more specific aspects of character design methods and theories to apply to your work within the rest of these chapters - you will see these with each Assignment, as they gradually cover more principles and techniques with tutorials and advice on various subjects.

You will notice a wide range of design aesthetics to learn from, and some visual guides showing you how to explore these design styles even further. So far, this document (and the many links provided) display a solid starting ground, which can help inform your decisions in the early stages of the development for your character design process. There's a few more technical things to be aware of, and you'll see them as you go through these series of practice exercises. There's lots more links and visual aids to be inspired from, time to place all this knowledge into practice, keep scrolling down to see.

![image](./images/unnamed_018.gif)

---

## Assignment #1

Use these two reference packs [STYLE GUIDE][LGbzqTDfdl] & [MODEL SHEETS][sFEWHmySnS] + [DESIGN PACK][CWcUDRMEGv] to learn the style of this series and create a new character that would fit in Cartoon Network's **Adventure Time** animated series. Another series in the same style to research is [Bravest Warriors][TDFrCQzeTr].

![image](./images/unnamed_1177.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_910.png)

[The Art of Ooo][uNAmNuNSsS]

Designs by Pendleton Ward, Andy Ristaino, Natasha Allegri, Alex Campos, Michael DeForge, Joy Ang, Jon Vermilyea, Michelle Xin, Matt Forsythe, Tom Herpich, Erica Jones, and Derek Kim.

Produce a 4pt turnaround model sheet: **Front** | **3/4 Front** | **Side** | **3/4 Back**

Line quality: **Tiedowns** ðŸ‘‰ tight roughs, not clean, but not too loose/rough (samples shown below)

1 Image file (JPG or PNG format)

Dimensions: 3840x2160 pixels @ 300dpi

> Support Material:

- [Using Photoshop to Draw Characters][TGEUrRIrbB]
- [Form & Structure in Design with Antonio Stappaerts][zIrDSgdPnn]
- [Character Design Fundamentals from Robert Cory][bHIUmiDvfI]

Begin with lots of rough sketches, study the references provided, and start to narrow down the sort of character you want to create.

Make some decisions as to which character you wish to refine and create a **front** or **3/4 front** view.

Once you've done one view, use it as a guide to match the proportions and positions of your body parts for other views so they'll line up properly for an accurate rotation:

![image](./images/unnamed_042.gif)
![image](./images/unnamed_054.gif)

Make sure you have those horizontal guide lines in place, it will help drawing the turn-around:

![image](./images/unnamed_1539.png)

By Adem Kaan

Advice from Andy Ristain, lead designer on _Adventure Time_:

Developing an understanding of how to construct a successful character design, and bringing together the components required to execute it commercially, are hard-won skills. But with this expertise comes a wealth of opportunity for those with the passion, drive and imagination to pursue it.

The goal of a character designer is to make the main characters as appealing as possible in as simple a way as possible.

A cartoon about the last human boy, Finn, and a shape-changing dog named Jake, Adventure Time follows their journey in a post-apocalyptic world called Ooo. I think that challenge is what attracted me to character design.

The cast of characters from the Adventure Time series, featuring the main protagonists Finn and Jake, was originally created for a younger audience, but the series has grown and matured into a cultural phenomenon that appeals to adults and children alike. The show has continuity and the actions and decisions the characters make have lasting repercussions.

To become truly great at crafting characters, illustrators need to invest the time in developing their own visual language at the most basic level -- by drawing every day. Taking inspiration from nature and his surroundings, mastering character design is a process that can\'t be rushed.

If you\'re building a family of characters, it can help to create a set of rules that define their graphical elements, before then experimenting with size and proportion. And try to think in three dimensions, draw a character and consider how it would look from the side, back and front -- not just flat on the screen.

For budding character designers, I have three key tips:

- Experiment with shape and form
- Take the time to let your mind wonder
- Keep drawing

> "It takes time, practice, effort and a lot of exploration, but if you keep digging you\'ll eventually find a place that\'s totally your own."

### Approach for Designing Characters

The secret to being a versatile character designer is to absorb the knowledge from as many artists as possible and filter-in the aspects that speak true to you. Take your time, watch and listen to the video essays provided below here, and in all the chapters from all the following Assignments. Remember and apply the aspects that make most sense to you. But be open to everything, various styles and techniques are explored and displayed. These are just to get you in the proper mindset, they explore an assortment of different principles, methods, and theories:

- [Learn to Draw in Any Style with Joshua Adeoye][iqJGCTUbai]
- [Talking About Character Design with Daniel Arriaga][FdFBMLdTIp]
- [Wouter Tulp Design Tips 1][rCNplyYGRA]
- [Wouter Tulp Design Tips 2][tDwhOGJfCK]
- [Brookes Eggleston - How To Be A Character Designer][DlalGXFLCH]
- [Brookes Eggleston - The Process][AlcghHAshO]

![image](./images/unnamed_486.png)

Valerio Fabbretti was the art director for _The Fungies!_ animated series and Becky Dreisdadt who worked as the character supervisor for _Adventure Time: Distant Lands_, have gone over the process together involved in a typical cartoon character design and how to build a portfolio focused on this aspect, these are portions from a webinar they had hosted:

> CHARACTER DESIGN FOR AN ANIMATED SERIES

![image](./images/unnamed_479.jpg)

In terms of character design, Becky comments that it is important to know how to draw in a great variety of angles. Depending on the relevance of each character, it may even be necessary to include additional information and sketches. That's why it's better that you are deeply familiarized with each character, in case you ever need to bring up more details about them as needed.

![Cartoon Character Design](./images/unnamed_1366.jpg)

Either way, with every character that you work on you need to create a register on how the mouth moves when making sounds or speaking. This is of great help to the animators when they need to work on scenes where there are dialogues. Of equal importance is to draw the characters with different expressions on their faces, for example, when they are happy, sad, scared or angry.

![image](./images/unnamed_166.jpg)

And although it is something not done frequently, Becky admits that one of the most difficult tasks in character design is to draw scenes where there are crowds involved. You need to know not only which characters are going to be in the crowd, but also how many of them will be and how to draw them together but in a way that each one is easily distinguishable. Of course, you need to avoid making tangent lines. This can be difficult while working in a unique character design and even more so where there are multiple ones in a crowd. If this isn't hard enough, you also need to be able to draw the crowds as seen from different angles.

![image](./images/unnamed_552.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_1311.png)

Sometimes you also need to modify a character design that's already established. This can be either because it needs a new costume, accessories or to show some battle damage in their attire. In this case, you need to follow the references provided by the artist that made the original design, but you are also free to take your own approach and give it some of your personal style. This way you can come up with new and exciting versions of a character design.

> THE PROCESS BEHIND CREATING A CHARACTER DESIGN

![image](./images/unnamed_889.png)

![image](./images/unnamed_1076.png)

![Cartoon Character Design](./images/unnamed_1025.jpg)

- The Art of Steven Universe [(1)][objlqvjvKT] [(2)][jyAnxtmBkn]

When a team of artists gets a new storyboard, Valerio explains that they first analyze it to find if there are any new characters or elements that need to be designed for the episode. After taking note of any new designs that need to be done, he assigns them to each artist taking in consideration their strengths and weaknesses, as well as what they tend to like drawing more.

![image](./images/unnamed_1176.png)

Being the art director, Valerio is responsible for making sure that each character design stays true to the show creator's vision. Not only does he supervise the design process for the characters, but also the way color is applied on them and finally, the way they are animated. After all, it's his job to make sure that there aren't any errors and that the final art is top notch.

![image](./images/unnamed_1233.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_384.png)

As for Becky, being a character supervisor, she is also deeply involved in this process. She is in charge of supervising her team of artists specializing in character design, as well as reporting back to the art director. One of her tasks is also making a guide of rules and important elements that every artist on her team must follow while creating a character design.

This can be like the way eyes should be drawn, the proportions that must be considered, general mistakes to avoid and so on. This in turn helps to decrease the times a design needs to be corrected, speeding up the process of working on each one of them. Also she is the one responsible for creating the designs for the major characters in each episode.

![image](./images/unnamed_537.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_194.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_206.jpg)

> THINGS YOU NEED TO TAKE INTO ACCOUNT FOR YOUR PORTFOLIO

![Cartoon Character Design](./images/unnamed_019.jpg)

Valerio says that one of the most important things is to know about anatomy. That's why he is so keen on observing if an artist is capable of drawing eyes, hands and feets. In his opinion, this is an indication that the artist knows about anatomic proportions, thereforth is easily able to adapt to different art styles while maintaining the correct proportions.

![image](./images/unnamed_712.png)
![image](./images/unnamed_914.png)

It's also vital to know about perspective, because as a character design artist, you need to be able to draw in a wide range of angles while giving a sense of depth in your work. Becky, on the other hand, says that it's fundamental to know about storytelling. That is why she prefers portfolios that show some experience or at least understanding in storyboards and comics. Of course, authenticity is greatly appreciated, so you need to include mostly designs for original characters that you created.

![image](./images/unnamed_942.png)

How about showing off that you are proficient in drawing multiple art styles? If you are really great at it, then go for it. But it's not necessary in Valerio's opinion. He says he prefers seeing an artist with a unique style in which he truly is good at. After all, if you are talented enough at drawing and understand about anatomic proportions, you can learn the art style for a series over time. Becky agrees with this statement, saying it's preferable to show consistency and mastery of one art style than regular or poor abilities in multiple ones. But if you're sticking to one style (your own personal style), then make sure it's appealing and consistent.

![image](./images/unnamed_401.jpg)

When including your character designs, you must show them not only in static poses but in movement also. A good artist is capable of showing some of their character's personality through movement, and that's why Valerio says he values a portfolio where he can see characters with more dynamic poses.

And while a finished character design is the most valued work in a portfolio, you also need to show the creative process behind it. Valerio comments it's helpful to include at least some quick and rough sketches, so a recruiter can see your ability in cleaning up lines and strokes. Finally, this may seem obvious but Becky states that you need to include colored works of art. While it is true that most character design artists only need to work in grayscale or monochromatic schemes, including some colored pieces can make your portfolio look more visually appealing.

![image](./images/unnamed_1018.jpg)
![image](./images/unnamed_325.png)

The show is set in a comical version of a family living in the [Stone Age][ApEIYuHLgy], but has added features and technologies that resemble mid-20th-century suburban America. The plots deliberately resemble the [sitcoms][XdxCKyTgQw] of the era, with the [caveman][skEhnYrFSH] Flintstone and Rubble families getting into minor conflicts, characteristic of modern life. The show is set in the Stone Age town of [Bedroc][lILrURLzxl]k. [Dinosaurs][RnbGegpoNv] and other prehistoric creatures are portrayed as co-existing with cavemen, [saber-toothed cats][DwzPmappEk], and [wooly mammoths][ecikHZDWPP], Brontosaurus are all used as pets or tools in everyday life.

The series draws its humor in part from creative uses of [anachronisms][EjAQLxMRrU]; elements belonging or appropriate to a period other than that in which it exists, especially a thing that is conspicuously old-fashioned. The main one is the placing of a \"modern\", 20th-century society in [prehistory][iZqQyoXwtB]. This society takes inspiration from the [suburban sprawl][xoIdWdiCqf] developed in the first two decades of the postwar period. This society has modern home appliances, but they work by employing animals. They have automobiles, but they hardly resemble the cars of the 20th century. These cars are large wooden and rock structures and burn no fuel. They are powered by people who run while inside them. This depiction is inconsistent, however, on some occasions, the cars are known to have engines (with appropriate sound effects), requiring ignition keys and gasoline. Also, the stone houses of this society are [cookie-cutter homes][rKOLyUWAzK] positioned into neighborhoods typical of mid-20th-century American suburbs.

The origins of the Hanna-Barbera & Flintstones visual style is found in the history of UPA Studios:

- [Part 1][tziIuFNrAQ]
- [Part 2][bUOuIFJbUC]
- [Part 3][kLkezxwcbI]
